The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Enumerating Moods with Categorical Major
Counts the productive moods for each combination of categorical and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the number of productive moods for each combination of categorical (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and conditional (universal/particular) premises when the categorical is the major premise.

Core Idea
Four moods arise from a universal categorical with universal conditionals; the numbers change with different premise types.

In the study of mixed syllogisms, knowing how many valid moods exist for each premise type is essential for quickly checking argument validity. This passage begins by listing these counts. We will work through them step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types considered
The passage considers three types of categorical premises: universal (affirmative/negative), universal negative, and particular affirmative. It pairs them with universal or particular conditionals.
The categorical is always the major premise here.
2
Count moods for universal categorical with universal conditionals
The text says 'الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة' meaning 4 moods for this combination.
These likely correspond to the four figures of the embedded categorical syllogism.
3
Count moods for universal categorical with particular conditionals
It says 'وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجباً كليا أو سالبا كليا' meaning 4 moods, but only if the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative or universal negative.
The condition restricts the antecedent's quantity and quality.
4
Count moods for universal negative categorical with universal conditionals
The passage continues: 'وثمانية من الحملية السالبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة' indicating 8 moods from a universal negative categorical with each universal conditional.
This doubles the previous count because the categorical is negative.
5
Continue to other combinations
The passage also mentions 6 moods from particular affirmative categorical with universal conditionals, and 2 moods from it with particular conditionals under a condition.
Total moods: 4+4+8+4+6+2 = 28, but not all are listed explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجباً كليا أو سالبا كليا. وثمانية من الحملية السالبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبا كليا أو جزئيا. وستة من الموجبة الجزئية الحملية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروب ثلاثة وهي التي لا يكون المقدم فيها سالبا كليا. وضربان منها مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين المتصلتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبا كليا. أما النتائج فإن كانت المتصلة كلية مقدمها سالب كلي، والحملية سالبة كلية أو المقدم موجب جزئي أو سالب جزئي، والحملية موجبة كلية فالنتيجة كلية مقدمها كلي عند سلب المقدم ومخالف للمقدم عند سلب الحملية، وكلي وجزئي عند إيجابه، وموافق للحملية لإنتاج مقدم النتيجة مع الكبرى الحملية مقدم الصغرى أو ما ينعكس إلى مقدم الصغرى أو إلى ما يكون مستلزما له من الأشكال الأربعة؛ وذلك عشرة أضرب؛ أربعة من الشرط الأول، وأربعة من الثاني، وستة من الثالث كقولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من «ب ج» فـ «ه ز»، ولا شيء من «أ ب» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» لأنه يصدق كلما كان كل «ج أ» فكل «ج أ» ولا شيء من «أ ب»، وكلما كان كذلك فلا شيء من «ج ب» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فلا شيء من «ج ب»، وكلما كان لا شيء من «ج ب» فلا شيء من «ب ج» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فلا شيء من «ب ج»؛ وإنه مع الصغرى ينتج المطلوب. وكذلك في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان بعض «ب ج» فـ «ه ز»، وكل «أ ب» ينتج كلما كان بعض «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» أو ليس البتة إذا كان بعض «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» لما مر. وكذلك في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان ليس بعض «ب ج» فـ «ه ز»، وبعض «أ ب» ينتج فكلما كان لا شيء من «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» فـ أو ليس البتة كما مر. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب. ثم الصغرى إذا لم تكن كلية مع شيء من الأمور الثلاثة كانت النتيجة جزئية مقدمها نتيجة التأليف بالخلف وغيره من الثالث. والأوسط فيه مقدم الصغرى لكن بعد رد المتصلة الكلية التي مقدمها موجب جزئي. وبالجملة فاللازم في إنتاج هذه الأقيسة في الأقسام الأربعة المذكورة اشتمال المتشاركين على تأليف منتج مع كون مقدم المتصلة الكلية كليا وشركة الحملية مع تاليها. فإنه إذا لم يكن كذلك كانت النتيجة متصلة جزئية. والنتيجة أبدا تتبع المتصلة في الكيف كما مر.
The universal [categorical] with each of the two universal conditionals in four moods, and four from them with each of the two particular conditionals if their antecedent is universal affirmative or universal negative. And eight from the universal negative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in four moods, and four from them with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or particular. And six from the particular affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in three moods, which are those where the antecedent is not universal negative. And two from them with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative. As for the conclusions: if the conditional is universal with a universal negative antecedent, and the categorical is universal negative, or the antecedent is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the categorical is universal affirmative, then the conclusion is universal, its antecedent is universal when the antecedent is negated, and contrary to the antecedent when the categorical is negated, and universal and particular when it is affirmed, and concordant with the categorical to produce the antecedent of the conclusion with the major categorical as the antecedent of the minor, or what is converted to the antecedent of the minor, or what is entailed by it from the four figures; that is ten moods: four from the first condition, four from the second, and six from the third. For example: 'Whenever it is or is not absolutely, if nothing of B is C, then H Z, and nothing of A is B' yields 'Whenever every C is A, then H Z' or 'is not absolutely whenever every C is A, then H Z' because it is true that whenever every C is A, then every C is A, and nothing of A is B, and whenever that is the case, then nothing of C is B' yields 'Whenever every C is A, nothing of C is B' and 'Whenever nothing of C is B, nothing of B is C' yields 'Whenever every C is A, nothing of B is C'; and this with the minor produces the required. Similarly in our statement: 'Whenever it is or is not absolutely, if some B is C, then H Z, and every A is B' yields 'Whenever some C is A, then H Z' or 'is not absolutely, whenever some C is A, then H Z' as before. And similarly in our statement: 'Whenever it is or is not absolutely, if not some B is C, then H Z, and some A is B' yields 'Whenever nothing of C is A, then H Z' or 'is not absolutely' as before. And this applies to the remaining moods. Then if the minor is not universal along with any of the three matters, the conclusion is particular, its antecedent being the result of composition by reductio and other than it from the third. And the middle there is the antecedent of the minor, but after reducing the universal conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative. In summary, the requirement for producing these syllogisms in the four mentioned divisions is that the two shared [terms] contain a productive composition while the antecedent of the universal conditional is universal and the categorical shares with its consequent. For if it is not so, then the conclusion is a particular conditional. And the conclusion always follows the conditional in quality as before.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the productive moods when a categorical premise serves as the major premise in a mixed syllogism with a conditional premise. The author begins by counting moods based on the quantity and quality of the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. For example, four moods arise from a universal categorical with two universal conditionals, four more with particular conditionals when the antecedent is universal affirmative or negative, etc. The author then explains the resulting conclusions: for certain combinations, the conclusion is a universal conditional with a specific relationship between the antecedent and the terms. He provides examples using logical variables (e.g., 'ب ج', 'ه ز') to illustrate how the syllogism works. He also notes that if the minor premise is not universal, the conclusion is particular. Finally, he summarizes that the key requirement is that the shared terms produce a valid composition with a universal antecedent and the categorical sharing the consequent. The conclusion always matches the quality of the conditional premise. This passage is an expansion of the rules for mixed syllogisms found in earlier logical texts.

According to this passage, how many moods arise from a universal categorical with each of the two universal conditionals?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The number of productive moods in a mixed syllogism depends on the quantity and quality of both premises, with specific counts given for each combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the universal negative categorical 'No intoxicant is permissible' and a universal conditional 'If something is khamr, it is intoxicant'. According to the passage, how many moods are productive from this combination? (Answer: 8, because the passage says 'وثمانية من الحملية السالبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين')
?
Given a particular affirmative categorical 'Some scholars are just' and a universal conditional 'If one is a scholar, one is knowledgeable', use the passage to determine how many moods are productive. (Answer: 6, from 'وستة من الموجبة الجزئية الحملية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين')

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Conditions for Universal Conclusions
Specifies when the conclusion of a mixed syllogism is universal and when it is particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the conclusion of a mixed syllogism (categorical major, conditional minor) is universal or particular based on the types of premises.

Core Idea
A universal conclusion requires the conditional premise to be universal with a universal negative antecedent, and the categorical to be universal negative, or other specific conditions.

After enumerating the moods, the author turns to the conditions that yield a universal conclusion. This is crucial because a universal conclusion provides stronger certainty. We will analyze the text's description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for universal conclusion
The passage says: 'إذا كانت المتصلة كلية مقدمها سالب كلي، والحملية سالبة كلية أو المقدم موجب جزئي أو سالب جزئي، والحملية موجبة كلية فالنتيجة كلية'. So the conditional must be universal with a universal negative antecedent, and the categorical must be universal negative; or the antecedent is particular with categorical universal affirmative.
There are multiple sufficient conditions for a universal conclusion.
2
Understand 'contrary to the antecedent' and 'concordant'
The text explains that the conclusion's antecedent is universal when the antecedent is negated, contrary to the antecedent when the categorical is negated, etc. This describes the logical relationship between the conclusion and the premises.
These details ensure the conclusion matches the logical relationships.
3
Recognize when conclusion is particular
Later: 'ثم الصغرى إذا لم تكن كلية مع شيء من الأمور الثلاثة كانت النتيجة جزئية'. If the minor premise (the conditional) is not universal with one of the three matters, the conclusion is particular.
The 'three matters' refer to the conditions mentioned earlier for universal conclusion.
4
Synthesize the universal/particular threshold
The universality of the conditional is key: only when the conditional is universal and meets the specific quality conditions can the conclusion be universal. Otherwise, it is particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبا كليا أو جزئيا. وستة من الموجبة الجزئية الحملية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروب ثلاثة وهي التي لا يكون المقدم فيها سالبا كليا. وضربان منها مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين المتصلتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبا كليا. أما النتائج فإن كانت المتصلة كلية مقدمها سالب كلي، والحملية سالبة كلية أو المقدم موجب جزئي أو ز»، ولا شيء من «أ ب» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» لأنه يصدق كلما كان كل «ج أ» فكل «ج أ» ولا شيء من «أ ب»، وكلما كان كذلك فلا شيء من «ج ب» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فلا شيء من «ج ب»، وكلما كان لا شيء من «ج ب» فلا شيء من «ب ج» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فلا شيء من «ب ج»؛ وإنه مع الصغرى ينتج المطلوب. وكذلك في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان بعض «ب ج» فـ «ه ز»، وكل «أ ب» ينتج كلما كان بعض «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» أو ليس البتة إذا كان بعض «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» لما مر. وكذلك في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان ليس بعض «ب ج» فـ «ه ز»، وبعض «أ ب» ينتج فكلما كان لا شيء من «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» فـ أو ليس البتة كما مر. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب. ثم الصغرى إذا لم تكن كلية مع شيء من الأمور الثلاثة كانت النتيجة جزئية مقدمها نتيجة التأليف بالخلف وغيره من الثالث. والأوسط فيه مقدم الصغرى The universal [categorical] with each of the two universal conditionals in four moods, and four from them with each of the two particular conditionals if their antecedent is universal affirmative or universal negative. And eight from the universal negative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in four moods, and four from them with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or particular. And six from the particular affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in three moods, which are those where the antecedent is not universal negative. And two from them with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative. As for the conclusions: if the conditional is universal with a universal negative antecedent, and the categorical is universal negative, or the antecedent is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the categorical is universal affirmative, then the conclusion is universal, its antecedent is universal when the antecedent is negated, and contrary to the antecedent when the categorical is negated, and universal and particular when it is affirmed, and concordant with the categorical to produce the antecedent of the conclusion with the major categorical as the antecedent of the minor, or what is converted to the antecedent of the minor, or what is entailed by it from the four figures; that is ten moods: four from the first condition, four from the second, and six from the third. For example: 'Whenever it is or is not absolutely, if nothing of B is C, then H Z, and nothing of A is B' yields 'Whenever every C is A, then H Z' or 'is not absolutely whenever every C is A, then H Z' because it is true that whenever every C is A, then every C is A, and nothing of A is B, and whenever that is the case, then nothing of C is B' yields 'Whenever every C is A, nothing of C is B' and 'Whenever nothing of C is B, nothing of B is C' yields 'Whenever every C is A, nothing of B is C'; and this with the minor produces the required. And similarly in our statement: 'Whenever it is or is not absolutely, if not some B is C, then H Z, and some A is B' yields 'Whenever nothing of C is A, then H Z' or 'is not absolutely' as before. For if it is not so, then the conclusion is a particular conditional. And the conclusion always follows the conditional in quality as before.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the productive moods when a categorical premise serves as the major premise in a mixed syllogism with a conditional premise. The author begins by counting moods based on the quantity and quality of the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. For example, four moods arise from a universal categorical with two universal conditionals, four more with particular conditionals when the antecedent is universal affirmative or negative, etc. The author then explains the resulting conclusions: for certain combinations, the conclusion is a universal conditional with a specific relationship between the antecedent and the terms. He provides examples using logical variables (e.g., 'ب ج', 'ه ز') to illustrate how the syllogism works. He also notes that if the minor premise is not universal, the conclusion is particular. Finally, he summarizes that the key requirement is that the shared terms produce a valid composition with a universal antecedent and the categorical sharing the consequent. The conclusion always matches the quality of the conditional premise. This passage is an expansion of the rules for mixed syllogisms found in earlier logical texts.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
The Key Requirement for Productiveness
Summarizes the necessary condition for any productive mixed syllogism: the shared terms must form a valid composition with a universal antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general requirement for a mixed syllogism (categorical major, conditional minor) to be productive, and identify it in an example.

Core Idea
The categorical must share its consequent with the conditional, and the conditional's antecedent must be universal for the syllogism to yield a conclusion.

After working through specific moods and conditions, the author gives a concise rule: the key to a productive mixed syllogism is that the shared terms between the conditional and categorical produce a valid composition, with the conditional's antecedent being universal. We will unpack this summary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the crucial phrase
The passage says: 'فاللازم في إنتاج هذه الأقيسة في الأقسام الأربعة المذكورة اشتمال المتشاركين على تأليف منتج مع كون مقدم المتصلة الكلية كليا وشركة الحملية مع تاليها'. This states the requirement: the two shared terms must form a productive composition, the antecedent of the universal conditional must be universal, and the categorical must share with the consequent.
'المتشاركين' refers to the terms shared between the conditional and categorical.
2
Understand 'اشتمال المتشاركين على تأليف منتج'
The shared terms must combine into a valid categorical syllogism (like Barbara, Celarent, etc.). In the example, the shared term is 'ب' appearing in both the conditional antecedent ('ب ج') and the categorical ('أ ب').
This embedded syllogism is what makes the mixed syllogism productive.
3
Apply to the given example
In the example: 'إذا كان لا شيء من ب ج فـ ه ز, ولا شيء من أ ب' the shared term is 'ب'. The categorical syllogism 'لا شيء من أ ب, لا شيء من ب ج, إذن لا شيء من أ ج' is valid (Celarent). This satisfies the requirement.
The conclusion's antecedent 'كل ج أ' is derived from 'لا شيء من أ ج' by conversion.
4
Recognize the condition 'مقدم المتصلة الكلية كليا'
The antecedent of the universal conditional must be universal. In the example, 'لا شيء من ب ج' is universal negative, meeting the condition.
If the antecedent were particular, the conclusion would be particular, as the text says.
5
Conclude the consequence if condition fails
The passage warns: 'فإنه إذا لم يكن كذلك كانت النتيجة متصلة جزئية'. Without these conditions, the conclusion is a particular conditional.
Thus the requirement is necessary for a universal conclusion, but also for any productive syllogism? Actually, the passage states it as 'اللازم في إنتاج هذه الأقيسة' meaning necessary for production, so even a particular conclusion requires the shared terms to form a productive composition, but the antecedent must be universal for a universal conclusion. The text is ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجباً كليا أو سالبا كليا. وثمانية من الحملية السالبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبا كليا أو جزئيا. وستة من الموجبة الجزئية الحملية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروب ثلاثة وهي التي لا يكون المقدم فيها سالبا كليا. وضربان منها مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين المتصلتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبا كليا. أما النتائج فإن كانت المتصلة كلية مقدمها سالب كلي، والحملية سالبة كلية أو المقدم موجب جزئي أو سالب جزئي، والحملية موجبة كلية فالنتيجة كلية مقدمها كلي عند سلب المقدم ومخالف للمقدم عند سلب الحملية، وكلي وجزئي عند إيجابه، وموافق للحملية لإنتاج مقدم النتيجة مع الكبرى الحملية مقدم الصغرى أو ما ينعكس إلى مقدم الصغرى أو إلى ما يكون مستلزما له من الأشكال الأربعة؛ وذلك عشرة أضرب؛ أربعة من الشرط الأول، وأربعة من الثاني، وستة من الثالث كقولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من «ب ج» فـ «ه ز»، ولا شيء من «أ ب» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» لأنه يصدق كلما كان كل «ج أ» فكل «ج أ» ولا شيء من «أ ب»، وكلما كان كذلك فلا شيء من «ج ب» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فلا شيء من «ج ب»، وكلما كان لا شيء من «ج ب» فلا شيء من «ب ج» ينتج كلما كان كل «ج أ» فلا شيء من «ب ج»؛ وإنه مع الصغرى ينتج المطلوب. وكذلك في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان بعض «ب ج» فـ «ه ز»، وكل «أ ب» ينتج كلما كان بعض «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» أو ليس البتة إذا كان بعض «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» لما مر. وكذلك في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان ليس بعض «ب ج» فـ «ه ز»، وبعض «أ ب» ينتج فكلما كان لا شيء من «ج أ» فـ «ه ز» فـ أو ليس البتة كما مر. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب. ثم الصغرى إذا لم تكن كلية مع شيء من الأمور الثلاثة كانت النتيجة جزئية مقدمها نتيجة التأليف بالخلف وغيره من الثالث. والأوسط فيه مقدم الصغرى لكن بعد رد المتصلة الكلية التي مقدمها موجب جزئي. وبالجملة فاللازم في إنتاج هذه الأقيسة في الأقسام الأربعة المذكورة اشتمال المتشاركين على تأليف منتج مع كون مقدم المتصلة الكلية كليا وشركة الحملية مع تاليها. فإنه إذا لم يكن كذلك كانت النتيجة متصلة جزئية. والنتيجة أبدا تتبع المتصلة في الكيف كما مر.
The universal [categorical] with each of the two universal conditionals in four moods, and four from them with each of the two particular conditionals if their antecedent is universal affirmative or universal negative. And eight from the universal negative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in four moods, and four from them with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or particular. And six from the particular affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in three moods, which are those where the antecedent is not universal negative. And two from them with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative. As for the conclusions: if the conditional is universal with a universal negative antecedent, and the categorical is universal negative, or the antecedent is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the categorical is universal affirmative, then the conclusion is universal, its antecedent is universal when the antecedent is negated, and contrary to the antecedent when the categorical is negated, and universal and particular when it is affirmed, and concordant with the categorical to produce the antecedent of the conclusion with the major categorical as the antecedent of the minor, or what is converted to the antecedent of the minor, or what is entailed by it from the four figures; that is ten moods: four from the first condition, four from the second, and six from the third. For example: 'Whenever it is or is not absolutely, if nothing of B is C, then H Z, and nothing of A is B' yields 'Whenever every C is A, then H Z' or 'is not absolutely whenever every C is A, then H Z' because it is true that whenever every C is A, then every C is A, and nothing of A is B, and whenever that is the case, then nothing of C is B' yields 'Whenever every C is A, nothing of C is B' and 'Whenever nothing of C is B, nothing of B is C' yields 'Whenever every C is A, nothing of B is C'; and this with the minor produces the required. Similarly in our statement: 'Whenever it is or is not absolutely, if some B is C, then H Z, and every A is B' yields 'Whenever some C is A, then H Z' or 'is not absolutely, whenever some C is A, then H Z' as before. And similarly in our statement: 'Whenever it is or is not absolutely, if not some B is C, then H Z, and some A is B' yields 'Whenever nothing of C is A, then H Z' or 'is not absolutely' as before. And this applies to the remaining moods. Then if the minor is not universal along with any of the three matters, the conclusion is particular, its antecedent being the result of composition by reductio and other than it from the third. And the middle there is the antecedent of the minor, but after reducing the universal conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative. In summary, the requirement for producing these syllogisms in the four mentioned divisions is that the two shared [terms] contain a productive composition while the antecedent of the universal conditional is universal and the categorical shares with its consequent. For if it is not so, then the conclusion is a particular conditional. And the conclusion always follows the conditional in quality as before.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the productive moods when a categorical premise serves as the major premise in a mixed syllogism with a conditional premise. The author begins by counting moods based on the quantity and quality of the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. For example, four moods arise from a universal categorical with two universal conditionals, four more with particular conditionals when the antecedent is universal affirmative or negative, etc. The author then explains the resulting conclusions: for certain combinations, the conclusion is a universal conditional with a specific relationship between the antecedent and the terms. He provides examples using logical variables (e.g., 'ب ج', 'ه ز') to illustrate how the syllogism works. He also notes that if the minor premise is not universal, the conclusion is particular. Finally, he summarizes that the key requirement is that the shared terms produce a valid composition with a universal antecedent and the categorical sharing the consequent. The conclusion always matches the quality of the conditional premise. This passage is an expansion of the rules for mixed syllogisms found in earlier logical texts.

التالي
الضروب

Checkpoint

A) The categorical must be universal
B) The conditional antecedent must be universal and the categorical must share with the consequent
C) The conditional must be particular
D) The categorical must be the minor premise

Key Takeaway
A mixed syllogism with a categorical major and conditional minor is productive only if the shared terms form a valid composition and the conditional antecedent is universal.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Third Division: Categorical as Minor, Conditional as Major
Defines the third division and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third division of mixed syllogisms and state its essential conditions.

Core Idea
In the third division, the categorical is minor, the conditional major, and the consequent must envelop the categorical.

This passage introduces the third division where the categorical premise acts as the minor and the conditional as the major. The author specifies two conditions: the conditional must be affirmative and the consequent must be inclusive of the categorical. Understanding these conditions is key to recognizing this syllogism type in Islamic logical works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles: minor and major
Determine which premise is categorical and which is conditional. In this division, the categorical premise is the minor, and the conditional premise is the major.
2
Check the conditional's quality
The condition requires the conditional to be affirmative (positive). If it is negative, it is not a valid third-division syllogism.
3
Verify the inclusivity of the consequent
The categorical must be entirely about the consequent (the 'then' part) of the conditional. This means the categorical term must be the same as the consequent's subject or predicate.
4
Apply the figure conditions
The categorical must conform to the standard conditions of its figure (e.g., in first figure, the categorical minor must be affirmative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث: فهو أن يكون المشارك الحملية تالي المتصلة والحملية صغرى. فالشرط فيه إيجاب المتصلة واشتمال التالي؛ والحملية في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة على شرائط ذلك الشكل. والمتصلة قد تكون موجبة كلية، وقد تكون موجبة جزئية. وعلى هذا الضروب المنتجة في شكل ضعف ضروبه المخصوصة بذلك الشكل. أما في الشكل الأول فالمتصلة الموجبة إذا كانت كبرى، وتاليها كلي لا محالة موجبًا كان أو سالبا. وعلى كل واحد من التقديرين فالحملية إما موجبة كلية أو موجبة جزئية، فهذه أربعة أضرب. وتحصل أربعة أخرى إذا كانت الكبرى المتصلة موجبة جزئية. وعلى هذا تحصل من الشكل الثاني ثمانية أضرب، ومن الثاني اثني عشر، ومن الرابع عشرة والنتيجة في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة متصلة موجبة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين الحملية؛ والتالي كلية إن كانت المتصلة كلية، وجزئية إن كانت جزئية. أما في الشكل الأول فذلك مثل قولنا كل ج ب، وكلما كان هز فكل ب أ كلما كان هز وكل ج أ. وإن كانت الحملية جزئية كما في قولنا بعض ينتج
And as for the third division: it is that the categorical shares with the consequent of the conditional, and the categorical is the minor premise. The condition in it is that the conditional is affirmative and the consequent is inclusive; and the categorical in each of the four figures according to the conditions of that figure. The conditional may be universal affirmative or particular affirmative. And accordingly, the productive moods in a figure are double the moods specific to that figure. In the first figure, the affirmative conditional if it is major, and its consequent is necessarily universal whether affirmative or negative. In each of the two cases, the categorical is either universal affirmative or particular affirmative; thus these are four moods. And four others arise if the major conditional is particular affirmative. Accordingly, from the second figure arise eight moods, from the second [third?] twelve, and from the fourth ten. And the conclusion in each of the four figures is an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of composition between the categorical; and the consequent is universal if the conditional is universal, and particular if it is particular. In the first figure, for example, our statement: 'Every C is B, and whenever H is Z, then every B is A' yields 'Whenever H is Z, every C is A'. And if the categorical is particular as in our statement: 'Some...' yields.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines the third division of mixed syllogisms, where a categorical premise serves as the minor premise and the conditional as the major premise. The author states the condition: the conditional must be affirmative, and its consequent must be shared by the categorical (i.e., the whole categorical premise is about that consequent). The categorical must meet the conditions of the figure it is in. The conditional can be universal or particular affirmative. The number of productive moods in a figure is double the moods specific to that figure. For example, in the first figure, the conditional is major, its consequent is always universal. With two possibilities for the categorical (universal or particular affirmative), we get four moods. Another four arise when the conditional is particular affirmative, making eight total for the first figure. Similar enumerations are given for the second, third (though text says 'second' twice, likely a scribal error), and fourth figures. The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is the result of composing the categorical with the conditional's consequent. The quantity of the conclusion's consequent matches the quantity of the original conditional. An example from the first figure is given: 'Every C is B, and whenever H is Z then every B is A' yields 'Whenever H is Z, every C is A'. The author cuts off while giving a particular example.

Key Terms
القسم الثالث
المشارك
اشتمال التالي
ضعف ضروبه

Key Takeaway
The third division requires an affirmative conditional and that the categorical is fully contained within the consequent.

Mood Enumeration in the Third Division
Enumerates moods per figure in the third division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of productive moods in each figure for the third division.

Core Idea
The number of moods is double the base moods of each figure: first 8, second 8, third 12, fourth 10.

After defining the structure, the author enumerates the productive moods per figure, showing how the quantity of the conditional (universal/particular) multiplies the moods. This enumeration is crucial for logicians to know which combinations are valid.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base moods for each figure
Know how many simple categorical syllogisms are productive in each figure: first figure has 4, second has 4, third has 6, fourth has 5.
These numbers are standard in Aristotelian logic.
2
Apply the doubling rule
The author states: 'على هذا الضروب المنتجة في شكل ضعف ضروبه' meaning the number is double the moods of that figure. So multiply by 2.
The doubling accounts for the conditional being universal or particular.
3
Verify with the text
For first figure: 4 × 2 = 8 (four moods when conditional is universal, four when particular). Second: 4×2=8, third: 6×2=12, fourth: 5×2=10.
The text confirms: 'ثمانية أضرب' for second, 'اثني عشر' for third, 'عشرة' for fourth.

The Syllogism

Constructing the Conclusion in the Third Division
Shows how to derive the conclusion from premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a third-division mixed syllogism given its premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion is an affirmative conditional: antecedent unchanged, consequent is the composition of the categorical with the original consequent.

The author explains that the conclusion's form is fixed: an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is the result of combining the categorical premise with the consequent. The example illustrates this derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original conditional's antecedent and consequent
In the example, 'كلما كان هز فكل ب أ', the antecedent is 'كان هز' and the consequent is 'كل ب أ' (every B is A).
2
Identify the categorical premise
The categorical premise is 'كل ج ب' (every C is B).
3
Produce the consequent of the conclusion
Combine the categorical premise with the original consequent via transitivity: from 'كل ج ب' and 'كل ب أ', deduce 'كل ج أ' (every C is A).
This is a first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara).
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is an affirmative conditional with the same antecedent: 'كلما كان هز فكل ج أ' (whenever H is Z, every C is A).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Syllogism

Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure: Conditional Major and Categorical Minor
Author explains mixed syllogisms with conditional major and categorical minor in first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid mixed syllogism in the first figure with a conditional major and categorical minor, and derive the correct conditional conclusion.

Core Idea
In a mixed syllogism, the categorical minor must be the consequent of the conditional major to yield a valid conclusion.

In this lesson, we explore the third division of mixed syllogisms: where the major premise is a conditional (shartiyya muttasila) and the minor is a categorical (hamliyya). The author explains the conditions for validity and provides examples in the first figure. You will learn how to combine a conditional major with a categorical minor to produce a new conditional conclusion.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the two premises
Locate the conditional major (e.g., 'Whenever H is Z, every B is A') and the categorical minor (e.g., 'Every C is B'). Note that the categorical must be the consequent of the conditional.
2
2. Verify the figure conditions
Confirm that the categorical minor satisfies the conditions of the first figure: the middle term (B) is subject in the minor and predicate in the major's consequent.
The categorical minor must be affirmative in the first figure.
3
3. Combine to form the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the major, and a consequent that is the result of substituting the categorical's subject into the major's consequent using categorical syllogism rules.
The categorical minor effectively replaces the subject of the major's consequent.
4
4. Check the quantifier and quality
Ensure the conclusion's consequent is universal if the conditional is universal, and particular if the conditional is particular. Adjust based on the quality of the categorical (affirmative or negative).
If the major's consequent is negative, the conclusion's consequent will also be negative.
5
5. Practice with negative consequents
Repeat the process when the major's consequent is negative (e.g., 'no B is A') and see how the conclusion's consequent becomes negative.
The categorical minor remains affirmative; only the quality of the consequent changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث: فهو أن يكون المشارك الحملية تالي المتصلة والحملية صغرى. فالشرط فيه إيجاب المتّصلة واشتمال التالي؛ والحملية في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة على شرائط ذلك الشكل. والمتصلة قد تكون موجبة كلية، وقد تكون موجبة جزئية. وعلى هذا الضروب المنتجة في شكل ضِعْفُ ضروبه المخصوصة بذلك الشكل.
[1] أما في الشكل الأول فالمتصلة الموجبة إذا كانت كبرى، وتاليها كلي لا محالة موجبًا كان أو سالبا. وعلى كل واحد من التقديرين فالحملية إما موجبة كلية أو موجبة جزئية، فهذه أربعة أضرب. وتحصل أربعة أخرى إذا كانت الكبرى المتّصلة موجبة جزئية. وعلى هذا تحصل من الشكل الثاني ثمانية أضرب، ومن الثالث اثني عشر، ومن الرابع عشرة والنتيجة في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة متّصلة موجبة مقدمها مقدم المتّصلة، وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين الحملية؛ والتالي كلية إن كانت المتّصلة كلية، وجزئية إن كانت جزئية. أما في الشكل الأول فذلك مثل قولنا كل «ج ب»، وكلما كان «هـ ز» فكل «ب أ» ينتج كلما كان «هـ ز» وكل «ج أ». وإن كانت الحملية جزئية كما في قولنا بعض «ج ب»، وكلما كان «هـ ز» فكل «ب أ» فإنه ينتج النتيجة المذكورة؛ هذا إذا كان التالي موجبا. فأما إذا كان سالبًا كما في قولنا كل «ج ب»، وكلما كان «هـ ز» فلا شيء من «ب أ»، فإنه ينتج قولنا كلما كان «هـ ز» فلا شيء من «ج أ». وكذلك إذا كانت الحملية جزئية كما في قولنا بعض «ج ب» وكلما كان «هـ ز» فلا شيء من «ب أ» فإنه ينتج النتيجة المذكورة. وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية مثل قولنا كل «ج ب»، وقد يكون إذا كان «هـ ز» وكل «ب أ» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «هـ ز» فكل «ج أ». وكذلك إذا كان التالي سالبا كقولنا كل «ج ب»، وقد يكون إذا كان «هـ ز» فلا شيء من «ب أ» فإنه ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «هـ ز» فلا شيء من «ج أ»؛ وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية.
As for the third division: it is that the categorical partner be the consequent of the conditional and the categorical be the minor. The condition for it is the affirmativeness of the conditional and the inclusion of the consequent; and the categorical in each of the four figures according to the conditions of that figure. And the conditional may be affirmative universal, or affirmative particular. And accordingly the productive moods in a figure are double the moods specific to that figure.
[1] As for the first figure: if the conditional is affirmative and is major, and its consequent is necessarily universal, whether affirmative or negative. And on each of the two suppositions, the categorical is either affirmative universal or affirmative particular; these are four moods. And four others are obtained if the major conditional is affirmative particular. And thus from the second figure eight moods are obtained, from the third twelve, and from the fourth ten. And the conclusion in each of the four figures is an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of the composition between the categorical; and the consequent is universal if the conditional is universal, and particular if it is particular. For the first figure, that is like our saying: 'Every C is B', and 'Whenever H is Z, every B is A' yields 'Whenever H is Z, every C is A'. And if the categorical is particular, as in our saying 'Some C is B', and 'Whenever H is Z, every B is A', then it yields the mentioned conclusion; this if the consequent is affirmative. As for if it is negative, as in our saying 'Every C is B', and 'Whenever H is Z, no B is A', then it yields our saying 'Whenever H is Z, no C is A'. And similarly if the categorical is particular, as in our saying 'Some C is B' and 'Whenever H is Z, no B is A', then it yields the mentioned conclusion. And if the conditional is particular, such as our saying 'Every C is B', and 'It may be that if H is Z, every B is A', it yields 'It may be that if H is Z, every C is A'. And similarly if the consequent is negative, like our saying 'Every C is B', and 'It may be that if H is Z, no B is A', then it yields 'It may be that if H is Z, no C is A'; and so on for the remaining moods.

Commentary
This passage defines a specific class of mixed syllogisms where the major premise is a conditional (muttasila) and the minor is a categorical (hamliya). The author specifies that the categorical minor must be the consequent of the conditional major, and the categorical must satisfy the conditions of its figure. The conditional can be affirmative universal or particular. The number of productive moods in each figure is double the number of moods specific to that figure (from pure categoricals). The author then details the first figure, showing that when the conditional is universal, its consequent is universal, and the categorical can be universal or particular. This yields four moods when the conditional is universal affirmative, and four more when it is particular affirmative. For each, the conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the major and a consequent that is the result of combining the categorical minor with the consequent of the major. Examples in the first figure are given with positive and negative consequents, and both universal and particular categoricals.

Mixed Syllogisms in Figures Two, Three, and Four
Author provides examples of mixed syllogisms with conditional major in figures two, three, and four

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate mixed syllogisms with a conditional major premise in the second, third, and fourth figures, using the method of reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms combine categorical and conditional premises; the figure determines the conclusion's type and quantity.

In this lesson, we examine the author's treatment of mixed syllogisms with a conditional major premise across the second, third, and fourth figures. Through examples using variables (أ, ب, ج, هـ, ز), you will learn how the categorical minor and conditional major interact to produce a valid conclusion. The author also mentions the use of reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf) to prove validity.

Guided Path
1
Extract the premises
In each example, there is one categorical premise (e.g., 'Every C is B') and one conditional premise (e.g., 'Whenever H is Z, no A is B'). Identify the terms: the middle term (B) appears in the categorical and in the consequent of the conditional.
2
Locate the figure
The figure is determined by the position of the middle term relative to the extremes. In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises (C is B, no A is B). In the third, it is the subject in both (every B is C, every B is A). In the fourth, it is the predicate of the conditional's consequent (every A is B) and subject of the categorical (every B is C).
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the major premise. Its consequent is obtained by combining the categorical minor with the major's consequent following the figure's rules. For example, in the second figure with a negative conditional, the consequent is a universal negative; in the third, it is a particular affirmative.
4
Verify with reductio
The author mentions the use of al-khalf (reductio). Suppose the opposite of the conclusion, combine with the minor, and derive a contradiction with the major. This confirms validity. For instance, in the second figure example, assume 'some C is A', combine with 'every C is B' to get 'some A is B', contradicting 'no A is B'.

Mixed Syllogisms: Categorical as Subject of Conditional's Consequent
Author explains mixed syllogisms when categorical is subject of conditional's consequent across four figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct valid mixed syllogisms in any of the four figures where the categorical proposition serves as the subject of the conditional's consequent, and identify the resulting conditional conclusion.

Core Idea
The categorical premise determines the new consequent of the conditional conclusion.

This passage from the classical logic text explains how to combine a categorical proposition with a conditional proposition when the categorical term appears as the subject of the conditional's consequent. The author walks through all four figures, showing how the conclusion is always a conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional and a new consequent derived from the categorical and the original consequent. Understanding these patterns is essential for building complex arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where conditional reasoning often involves categorical classifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The syllogism has two premises: one categorical (حملية) of the form 'كل/بعض ج هو/ليس ب', and one conditional (متصلة) of the form 'كلما كان هز فكل/بعض/لا شيء من ب هو/ليس أ'. The categorical's subject (ج) and predicate (ب) must be identified, as well as the conditional's antecedent (هز) and consequent (which contains ب and أ).
In the passage, the categorical is always the first premise, and the conditional is second.
2
Determine the figure
The figure depends on the position of the categorical's subject (ج) and predicate (ب) relative to the conditional's consequent. In the first figure, the categorical's subject (ج) is the subject of the consequent? Actually, the text uses schema: categorical: 'ج ب'; conditional: 'كلما كان هز فكل ب أ' → conclusion: 'كلما كان هز فكل ج أ'. Here the categorical's predicate (ب) is the subject of the conditional's consequent. So figure classification is based on the arrangement of terms across premises. Refer to the four figures as given: 1st, 2nd, 3rd, 4th.
The text explicitly labels figures with letters [ب], [ت], [ث] for second, third, fourth.
3
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is always a conditional proposition with the same antecedent (هز) as the original conditional. The consequent is formed by taking the categorical's subject (ج) as the subject and combining with the conditional's consequent's predicate (أ), while preserving the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) from the categorical. For example, in the first figure, from 'كل ج ب' and 'كلما كان هز فكل ب أ' we get 'كلما كان هز فكل ج أ'.
If the categorical is particular (بعض ج ب), the conclusion's consequent is also particular (بعض ج أ). If the conditional's consequent is negative (لا شيء من ب أ), the conclusion's consequent is negative (لا شيء من ج أ).
4
Verify validity using conditions
The text asserts that these inferences are valid given the conditions for production. The standard syllogistic conditions (e.g., distribution of middle term, no illicit process) apply. For mixed syllogisms, the middle term is the term connecting the two premises (here, ب appears in both premises). The conclusion's terms must follow the rules of categorical syllogisms within the conditional's consequent.
The author assumes the reader knows these proofs from earlier sections; we accept the validity as given.
5
Practice with all figures
Apply the same method to the remaining three figures. For the second figure, example: 'كل ج ب' and 'كلما كان هز فلا شيء من أ ب' yields 'كلما كان هز فلا شيء من ج أ'. For the third figure: 'كل ب ج' and 'كلما كان هز فكل ب أ' yields 'كلما كان هز فبعض ج أ'. For the fourth figure: 'كل ب ج' and 'كلما كان هز فكل أ ب' yields 'كلما كان هز فبعض ج أ'.
The text says 'and so on for the remaining moods' – the pattern is consistent.

«ج ب»، وكلما كان «هز» فكل «ب أ» فإنه ينتج النتيجة المذكورة؛ هذا إذا كان التالي موجبا. فأما إذا كان سالبًا كما في قولنا كل «ج ب»، وكلما كان «هز» فلا شيء من «ب أ»، فإنه ينتج قولنا كلما كان «هز» فلا شيء من «ج أ». وكذلك إذا كانت الحملية جزئية كما في قولنا بعض «ج ب» وكلما كان «هز» فلا شيء من «ب أ» فإنه ينتج النتيجة المذكورة. وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية مثل قولنا كل «ج ب»، وقد يكون إذا كان «هز» وكل «ب أ» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «هز» فكل «ج أ». وكذلك إذا كان التالي سالبا كقولنا كل «ج ب»، وقد يكون إذا كان «هز» فلا شيء من «ب أ» فإنه ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «هز» فلا شيء من «ج أ»؛ وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية.
[ب] وأما في الشكل الثاني فكذلك مثل قولنا كل «ج ب»، وكلما كان «هز» فلا شيء من «أ ب» ينتج كلما كان «هز» فلا شيء من «ج أ» الخلف وغيره. وكذلك إذا كانت الحملية جزئية كقولنا بعض «ج ب»، وكلما كان «هز» فلا شيء من «أ ب» فإنه ينتج النتيجة المذكورة. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية منه.

«ج ب», and whenever it is «هز» then every «ب أ» produces the mentioned conclusion; this if the consequent is affirmative. But if it is negative, as in our statement every «ج ب», and whenever it is «هز» then nothing from «ب أ» is, then it produces our statement: whenever it is «هز» then nothing from «ج أ» is. And similarly if the categorical is particular, as in our statement some «ج ب», and whenever it is «هز» then nothing from «ب أ» is, then it produces the mentioned conclusion. And if the conditional is particular, like our statement every «ج ب», and it may be that if it is «هز» and every «ب أ», then it produces: it may be that if it is «هز» then every «ج أ». And similarly if the consequent is negative, like our statement every «ج ب», and it may be that if it is «هز» then nothing from «ب أ» is, then it produces: it may be that if it is «هز» then nothing from «ج أ» is; and so on for the remaining moods.
[ب] As for the second figure, likewise, like our statement every «ج ب», and whenever it is «هز» then nothing from «أ ب» is, produces: whenever it is «هز» then nothing from «ج أ» is—the [؟] and others. [ث] As for the fourth figure, that is like our statement every «ب ج», and whenever it is «هز» then every «أ ب» produces: whenever it is «هز» then some «ج أ». And similarly if the categorical is particular. And so on for all moods—their composition is not hidden from you after examining the conditions for production and the proofs indicating them; and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of mixed syllogisms where a categorical proposition is combined with a conditional proposition, specifically when the categorical premise is the subject of the conditional's consequent. The author first explains the first figure. For example, if we have the categorical 'every ج is ب' and the conditional 'whenever it is هز, every ب is أ', the conclusion is 'whenever it is هز, every ج is أ'. The consequent of the conditional may be affirmative or negative, and the categorical may be universal or particular. Various combinations are given. Then the author proceeds to the second figure, where the categorical premise and the conditional share the term in a different position, e.g., 'every ج is ب' and 'whenever it is هز, nothing from أ is ب' yields 'whenever it is هز, nothing from ج is أ'. The third figure has the categorical in the middle, e.g., 'every ب is ج' and 'whenever it is هز, every ب is أ' yields 'whenever it is هز, some ج is أ'. Finally, the fourth figure: 'every ب is ج' and 'whenever it is هز, every أ is ب' yields 'whenever it is هز, some ج is أ'. The author concludes that by understanding the conditions for valid production and the proofs, the student can construct all moods. The commentary follows the order of figures, explaining each with examples from the Arabic logical notation (ج, ب, أ, هز as variables). All examples are from the internal logic of the text, using the same variables.

The Syllogism

Fourth Division Mixed Syllogisms: First Figure
Author defines fourth division of mixed syllogisms and gives first figure example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a fourth-division mixed syllogism in the first figure given the premises.

Core Idea
When the categorical is the consequent of the conditional, the number of moods doubles.

This lesson introduces the fourth division of mixed syllogisms, where the categorical premise serves as the predicate of the conditional's consequent. Understanding this structure is essential for analyzing arguments that combine conditional and categorical statements, common in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth division
Locate the definition: the shared term (al-mushārik) is the consequent of the conditional, and the categorical is the major premise. This is the opposite of the third division where the shared term is the antecedent.
2
Understand the condition
The condition for validity is the same as in the third division: the categorical must be a major premise and the conditional a minor premise, with the consequent of the conditional being the subject of the categorical.
This ensures the middle term connects properly.
3
Enumerate the first figure moods
For the first figure: if the conditional is universal affirmative, its consequent is necessarily affirmative. The categorical can be universal affirmative or universal negative, giving 4 moods (2 x 2). If the conditional is particular affirmative, another 4 moods arise. Thus, double the usual number of moods for each figure.
Double the moods because the conditional can be universal or particular.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional. Its antecedent is the antecedent of the minor conditional. Its consequent is the result of combining (al-ta'līf) the categorical with the consequent of the conditional. The conclusion is universal if the conditional is universal, particular if particular.
For example: 'Whenever it is H, then every A is B' and 'Every B is J' yields 'Whenever it is H, every A is J'.
5
Apply the examples
Study the given examples for the first figure: when the categorical is affirmative (universal or particular) and when it is negative. Verify that the conclusion follows the rule.
The text provides both universal and particular cases.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Mixed Syllogisms: Introduction and First Figure
Rules and first figure examples of mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid mixed syllogism of the fourth division in the first figure and determine its conclusion.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the categorical major shares a term with the consequent of the conditional minor, and the conclusion is a conditional linking the original antecedent to the categorical result.

This lesson introduces the fourth division of mixed syllogisms, where the categorical premise is the major and is related to the consequent of the conditional minor. We will focus on the first figure, where the conditional is minor and its consequent acts as the minor of an embedded categorical syllogism. The author explains how the moods double and provides examples with the variables H, Z, A, B, J. Understanding this structure is key to mastering mixed syllogisms, which are common in Islamic theological and legal reasoning when conditional premises are used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The first premise is a conditional (e.g., 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B'), which is the minor. The second premise is a categorical (e.g., 'Every B is J' or 'No B is J'), which is the major. Note that the categorical major must share a term (B) with the consequent of the conditional.
The consequent of the conditional is always a categorical proposition (here, 'Every A is B').
2
Extract the embedded categorical syllogism
Treat the consequent of the conditional as the minor premise and the categorical major as the major premise of a standard categorical syllogism. For example, 'Every A is B' (minor) and 'Every B is J' (major) yield 'Every A is J' in Barbara.
The figure and mood of this embedded syllogism depend on the position of the middle term (B). Here it is the first figure.
3
Determine the conclusion of the embedded syllogism
Apply the rules of categorical syllogism to find the conclusion. In the first figure, the conclusion relates A and J. The quantity and quality depend on the premises. For universal affirmative conditional, the embedded conclusion is universal; for particular, particular.
The conclusion of the embedded syllogism becomes the consequent of the final conclusion.
4
Form the final conclusion
The final conclusion is a conditional proposition whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional (e.g., 'H is Z') and whose consequent is the conclusion of the embedded categorical syllogism (e.g., 'Every A is J' or 'No A is J'). The quantity of the final conditional matches the original conditional (universal if original was universal, particular if original was particular).
For example, 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B' and 'Every B is J' yield 'Whenever H is Z, every A is J'.

As for the fourth division, it is that the categorical partner is the consequent of the conditional, and the categorical is the major premise. The condition for it is what was mentioned previously in the third division.
[A] As for the first figure: because the universal affirmative conditional when it is minor, its consequent is necessarily affirmative, whether universal or particular. On each of the two estimations, the categorical is either universal affirmative or universal negative; thus these are four moods. Another four result if the conditional is particular affirmative. On this basis, from each figure there results double the moods specific to that figure. The conclusion in each of the four figures is a universal affirmative conditional if the conditional is universal, and particular if the conditional is particular, its antecedent being the antecedent of the conditional and its consequent being the result of the composition as previously. For example, in the first figure, our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and every B is J' yields 'Whenever H is Z, every A is J'. Likewise, if the conditional is particular, it yields a particular conclusion, as in our statement: 'Sometimes when H is Z, every A is B; and every B is J' yields 'Sometimes when H is Z, every A is J'. This is when the categorical is affirmative. If it is negative, as in our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and no B is J' yields 'Whenever H is Z, no A is J'. Likewise, if the conditional is particular, it yields a particular conclusion, such as our statement: 'Sometimes when H is Z, no A is J'. And so on for the remaining moods.
[B] As for the second figure: that is like our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and no J is B' yields 'Whenever H is Z, no A is J'. If the conditional is particular, that is like our statement: 'Sometimes when H is Z, every A is B; and no J is B' yields 'Sometimes when H is Z, no A is J' by reductio and other methods. And so on for the remaining moods.
[C] As for the third figure: that is like our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and every A is J' yields 'Whenever H is Z, some B is J'. Likewise if the conditional is particular, and so on for the remaining moods.
[D] As for the fourth figure: that is like our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and every J is A' yields 'Whenever H is Z, some B is J'. Likewise if the conditional is particular, and so on for the remaining moods. So consider what you have learned.
Know that the opinions about the conclusions differ in each of these four divisions, especially in these two divisions: for the conclusion in each mood may be an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of composition between the categorical and the consequent as mentioned, and this is the necessary [conclusion] according to some people. And it may be an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the result of composition and whose consequent is either the antecedent of the conditional or its consequent; so it is not necessary that it be this specifically or that. This is among the views of Imam Zayn al-Din al-Kashi, and it is the more natural view. And just as the opinions about the conclusions differ, so too do they about the moods as to how many they are. What the Shaykh [Ibn Sina?] held is that there is a reduction to half relative to what was mentioned in each of the four figures from these two divisions for producing universal and particular negatives, producing universal and particular affirmatives, but on condition that the consequent in the negative is the contradictory of what should be in the affirmative agreeing with it in quantity; and that is the conversion of the negative into the affirmative which agrees with the negative in the antecedent and quantity, and contradicts it in
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the fourth division of mixed syllogisms (al-qiyas al-mukhtalit) where the categorical premise is the consequent of the conditional and also serves as the major premise. The author first restates the condition from the previous third division. Then he proceeds figure by figure, starting with the first figure. He explains that when the conditional is universal affirmative and the minor premise, its consequent is necessarily affirmative, and the categorical major can be either universal affirmative or universal negative, yielding four moods; another four arise from a particular conditional. In total, each figure gets double the moods of the purely categorical syllogism. The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional and whose consequent is the result of combining categorical premises (as in a standard categorical syllogism). Examples are given in each figure using the variables H, Z, A, B, J (هز, أب, بج, أج). In the first figure, both affirmative and negative categoricals are illustrated. In the second figure, the author shows the negative case (no J is B) and mentions reductio (al-khalf). The third figure yields a particular affirmative conclusion (some B is J) because the middle term is subject in both premises. The fourth figure also yields particular affirmative (some B is J). The author then notes diversity of scholarly opinion. Some hold that the necessary conclusion is as described (with antecedent from the conditional and consequent from the composition). Others, following Imam Zayn al-Din al-Kashi, allow the conclusion to have its antecedent as the result of composition and its consequent as either the original antecedent or consequent, without strict determinacy. There is also disagreement on the number of valid moods. The Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina) held that the number of productive moods is half of what was previously enumerated, because negative conclusions can be transformed into affirmative ones by converting the negative into its affirmative contradictory, provided the consequent in the negative is the contradictory of what would be in the corresponding affirmative. This section illustrates the richness of post-Avicennan logic and the ongoing debates about mixed syllogisms.

الحملية

Key Takeaway
A mixed syllogism of the fourth division in the first figure combines a conditional minor and a categorical major sharing a term with the consequent, producing a conditional conclusion whose antecedent is from the original conditional and whose consequent is from the embedded categorical syllogism.

Mixed Syllogisms in the Second, Third, and Fourth Figures
Examples of mixed syllogisms in figures 2-4

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct mixed syllogisms in the second, third, and fourth figures and identify their conclusions.

Core Idea
The same structure of mixed syllogisms applies to all four figures, but the conclusions vary: the second figure yields universal or particular negatives, while the third and fourth yield particular affirmatives or negatives depending on the premises.

This lesson extends the mixed syllogism structure to the remaining three figures. The author provides examples for each, showing how the categorical major and the conditional consequent interact. In the second figure, the categorical major has a term in the predicate position, leading to negative conclusions. In the third and fourth figures, the categorical major shares a term with the subject of the consequent, resulting in particular conclusions. Understanding these patterns is crucial for handling mixed syllogisms in any figure, as they appear in complex arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure of the embedded categorical syllogism
The figure is determined by the position of the middle term (the term shared between the consequent and the categorical major). In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises; in the third, it is the subject in both; in the fourth, it is the subject of the categorical major and predicate of the consequent.
For example, in the second figure example 'لا شيء من ج ب' (no J is B), the middle term B is predicate in both the consequent 'every A is B' and the categorical 'no J is B'.
2
Apply the rules of the figure
Each figure has specific validity conditions. For the second figure, one premise must be negative; for the third, the conclusion must be particular; for the fourth, the conclusion is particular and the premises must satisfy certain conditions.
The author's examples follow these rules: the second figure examples have a negative categorical, the third and fourth yield particular conclusions.
3
Determine the conclusion of the embedded categorical syllogism
Combine the consequent (as minor) and the categorical major to derive the embedded conclusion. For the second figure, if the categorical is negative, the conclusion is negative. For the third and fourth, the conclusion is particular because of the figure rules.
In the second figure example: consequent 'every A is B', categorical 'no J is B' yields 'no A is J' (Cesare mood). In the third: 'every A is B' and 'every A is J' yield 'some B is J' (Darapti). In the fourth: 'every A is B' and 'every J is A' yield 'some B is J' (Bramantip).
4
Form the final conclusion
Wrap the embedded conclusion in a conditional with the original antecedent. The quantity and quality of the final conditional match the original conditional (universal or particular, affirmative or negative).
For example, the second figure yields 'Whenever H is Z, no A is J' for a universal conditional, and a particular version for particular conditional.

واعلم بأن الأقوال في النتائج مختلفة في كل قسم من هذه الأقسام الأربعة على الخصوص في هذين القسمين: فإن النتيجة في كل ضرب منهما قد تكون متصلة موجبة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين الحملية والتالي كما مر وهذا هو اللازم عند قوم . وقد تكون متصلة موجبة مقدمها نتيجة التأليف؛ وتاليها إما مقدم المتّصلة وإما تاليها؛ فلا يلزم أن يكون هذا على التعيين أو ذلك وهذا من جملة ما ذهب إليه الإمام زين الدين الكشي وإنه هو الأقرب إلى الطبع. وكما أن الأقوال في النتائج مختلفة فكذلك في الضروب بأنها كم هي؛ وما ذهب إليه الشيخ فذلك على الضعف بالنسبة إلى ما مرَّ ذكره في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة من هذين القسمين لإنتاج السالبة الكلية والجزئية إنتاج الموجبة الكلية والجزئية لكن شرط أن يكون التالي في السالبة نقيض ما يجب أن يكون في الموجبة الموافقة لها في الكم؛ وذلك انقلاب السالبة إلى الموجبة التي توافق السالبة في المقدم والكم، وتناقضها في
As for the fourth division, it is that the categorical partner is the consequent of the conditional, and the categorical is the major premise. The condition for it is what was mentioned previously in the third division.
[A] As for the first figure: because the universal affirmative conditional when it is minor, its consequent is necessarily affirmative, whether universal or particular. On each of the two estimations, the categorical is either universal affirmative or universal negative; thus these are four moods. Another four result if the conditional is particular affirmative. On this basis, from each figure there results double the moods specific to that figure. The conclusion in each of the four figures is a universal affirmative conditional if the conditional is universal, and particular if the conditional is particular, its antecedent being the antecedent of the conditional and its consequent being the result of the composition as previously. For example, in the first figure, our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and every B is J' yields 'Whenever H is Z, every A is J'. Likewise, if the conditional is particular, it yields a particular conclusion, as in our statement: 'Sometimes when H is Z, every A is B; and every B is J' yields 'Sometimes when H is Z, every A is J'. This is when the categorical is affirmative. If it is negative, as in our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and no B is J' yields 'Whenever H is Z, no A is J'. Likewise, if the conditional is particular, it yields a particular conclusion, such as our statement: 'Sometimes when H is Z, no A is J'. And so on for the remaining moods.
[B] As for the second figure: that is like our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and no J is B' yields 'Whenever H is Z, no A is J'. If the conditional is particular, that is like our statement: 'Sometimes when H is Z, every A is B; and no J is B' yields 'Sometimes when H is Z, no A is J' by reductio and other methods. And so on for the remaining moods.
[C] As for the third figure: that is like our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and every A is J' yields 'Whenever H is Z, some B is J'. Likewise if the conditional is particular, and so on for the remaining moods.
[D] As for the fourth figure: that is like our statement: 'Whenever H is Z, every A is B; and every J is A' yields 'Whenever H is Z, some B is J'. Likewise if the conditional is particular, and so on for the remaining moods. So consider what you have learned.
Know that the opinions about the conclusions differ in each of these four divisions, especially in these two divisions: for the conclusion in each mood may be an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of composition between the categorical and the consequent as mentioned, and this is the necessary [conclusion] according to some people. And it may be an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the result of composition and whose consequent is either the antecedent of the conditional or its consequent; so it is not necessary that it be this specifically or that. This is among the views of Imam Zayn al-Din al-Kashi, and it is the more natural view. And just as the opinions about the conclusions differ, so too do they about the moods as to how many they are. What the Shaykh [Ibn Sina?] held is that there is a reduction to half relative to what was mentioned in each of the four figures from these two divisions for producing universal and particular negatives, producing universal and particular affirmatives, but on condition that the consequent in the negative is the contradictory of what should be in the affirmative agreeing with it in quantity; and that is the conversion of the negative into the affirmative which agrees with the negative in the antecedent and quantity, and contradicts it in
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the fourth division of mixed syllogisms (al-qiyas al-mukhtalit) where the categorical premise is the consequent of the conditional and also serves as the major premise. The author first restates the condition from the previous third division. Then he proceeds figure by figure, starting with the first figure. He explains that when the conditional is universal affirmative and the minor premise, its consequent is necessarily affirmative, and the categorical major can be either universal affirmative or universal negative, yielding four moods; another four arise from a particular conditional. In total, each figure gets double the moods of the purely categorical syllogism. The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional and whose consequent is the result of combining categorical premises (as in a standard categorical syllogism). Examples are given in each figure using the variables H, Z, A, B, J (هز, أب, بج, أج). In the first figure, both affirmative and negative categoricals are illustrated. In the second figure, the author shows the negative case (no J is B) and mentions reductio (al-khalf). The third figure yields a particular affirmative conclusion (some B is J) because the middle term is subject in both premises. The fourth figure also yields particular affirmative (some B is J). The author then notes diversity of scholarly opinion. Some hold that the necessary conclusion is as described (with antecedent from the conditional and consequent from the composition). Others, following Imam Zayn al-Din al-Kashi, allow the conclusion to have its antecedent as the result of composition and its consequent as either the original antecedent or consequent, without strict determinacy. There is also disagreement on the number of valid moods. The Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina) held that the number of productive moods is half of what was previously enumerated, because negative conclusions can be transformed into affirmative ones by converting the negative into its affirmative contradictory, provided the consequent in the negative is the contradictory of what would be in the corresponding affirmative. This section illustrates the richness of post-Avicennan logic and the ongoing debates about mixed syllogisms.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Differing Opinions on Conclusions and Moods in Mixed Syllogisms
Discussion of scholarly disagreements on mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the different positions on the form and number of conclusions and moods in mixed syllogisms, and evaluate their logical implications.

Core Idea
The form of the conclusion in mixed syllogisms is controversial: some require the antecedent to be the original conditional's antecedent, while others allow the antecedent to be the result of composition, reflecting the richness of post-Avicennan logic.

This lesson explores the scholarly debate surrounding mixed syllogisms after presenting the basic framework. The author notes that the conclusions and the number of moods are not universally agreed upon. Some logicians, including the influential Imam Zayn al-Din al-Kashi, allow the conclusion's antecedent to be the result of composition (the embedded categorical conclusion) with its consequent being either the original antecedent or consequent. Others, like 'the Shaykh' (likely Ibn Sina), reduce the number of productive moods by converting negatives to affirmatives. Understanding these disagreements enhances one's appreciation of the dynamic nature of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main positions on the conclusion's form
One position (the 'necessary' one) holds that the conclusion is a conditional with the original antecedent as antecedent and the embedded categorical conclusion as consequent. Another position (attributed to al-Kashi) allows the embedded categorical conclusion to be the antecedent, and the consequent to be either the original antecedent or consequent, without strict determinacy.
The author notes that al-Kashi's view is 'more natural' (الأقرب إلى الطبع), suggesting it may be preferred.
2
Understand the disagreement on the number of moods
The author mentions a reductionist view (attributed to 'the Shaykh') that the number of productive moods is half of what was initially enumerated. This is achieved by converting negative conclusions into affirmative ones by replacing the negative consequent with its contradictory, provided certain conditions hold.
This reduction relies on the equivalence between a negative conclusion and an affirmative one with a negated predicate, known as 'inversion' (قلب السالبة إلى الموجبة).
3
Analyze the logical basis of the reduction
If a negative conclusion like 'No B is J' can be expressed as 'Every B is not-J' (affirmative with negated predicate), then the mixed syllogism that produced the negative conclusion can be seen as equivalent to a different mood. This allows the list of moods to be halved.
For example, a syllogism that produced 'Whenever H is Z, no A is J' could be rephrased as 'Whenever H is Z, every A is not-J', which might be achieved by a smaller set of premise types.
4
Evaluate the implications
These debates show that the theory of mixed syllogisms was not fixed; logicians actively refined it. Knowing the different positions helps a student understand why different textbooks might present different rules and how to navigate inconsistencies.
This also highlights the importance of understanding the underlying categorical logic to reconstruct alternative forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
