Third and Fourth Figures of Mixed Conditional-Categorical Syllogisms
Author illustrates the third and fourth figures of mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third and fourth figures of mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms and construct valid examples.

Core Idea
In mixed conditional syllogisms, the third and fourth figures always yield a particular conclusion.

The author extends the concept of syllogistic figures from purely categorical logic to mixed syllogisms where the major premise is a universal conditional and the minor is categorical. Understanding these figures helps you analyze arguments from Islamic legal and theological texts that combine conditional and categorical statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the major premise (conditional) and the minor premise (categorical). In the given example, the major is 'كلما كان هـز فكل أب' and the minor is 'كل أج'.
2
Determine the figure
Check the position of the middle term (A). In the third figure, the middle term is subject in both premises. In the fourth figure, it is predicate in the major and subject in the minor.
3
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion must be a particular conditional. Note that the text explicitly states the conclusion is 'كلما كان هـز فبعض بج', not a universal one.
This is a key rule: third and fourth figures produce particular conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشكل الثالث فذلك كقولنا كلما كان «هز» فكل «أب»، وكل «أج» ينتج كلَّما كان «هز» فبعض «بج». وكذلك إذا كانت المتصلة جزئية، وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية. [ث] وأما في الشكل الرابع فكقولنا كلَّما كان «هز» فكل «أب» وكل «ج أ» ينتج كلَّما كان «هز» فبعض «بج». وكذلك إذا كانت المتصلة جزئية، وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب، فاعتبر بما عرفت.
And as for the third figure, that is like our saying: Whenever H is Z, then every A is B, and every A is J produces: Whenever H is Z, then some B is J. And likewise if the conditional is particular, and so on for the remaining moods. [Th] And as for the fourth figure, like our saying: Whenever H is Z, then every A is B, and every J is A produces: Whenever H is Z, then some B is J. And likewise if the conditional is particular, and so on for the remaining moods. So take heed from what you have known.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage explains the third and fourth figures of mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms. The author provides examples using variables: for the third figure, the major is a universal conditional 'Whenever H is Z, then every A is B', the minor is a universal categorical 'Every A is J', and the conclusion is a particular conditional 'Whenever H is Z, then some B is J'. This matches the pattern of the third figure in categorical logic where the middle term is subject in both premises. The same applies when the conditional is particular. For the fourth figure, the minor is 'Every J is A' and the conclusion is likewise particular. The author concludes by urging the reader to understand the pattern.

Forms of the Conclusion and Negative Conversion in Conditional Syllogisms
Author discusses divergent views on conclusion form and a method for deriving negative conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two possible forms of conclusions in mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms and apply the inqilāb conversion method to derive a negative conclusion from an affirmative one.

Core Idea
Not all conclusions must follow the same structure; negative conclusions are obtained by converting an affirmative via contradictory consequent.

This passage from your coursebook dives into two key topics: the precise form of the conclusion in a conditional-categorical syllogism, and a clever method for obtaining negative conclusions. Some scholars insist the conclusion must have a fixed structure, while others, like Imam Zain al-Din al-Kashi, allow flexibility. The author then demonstrates a step-by-step conversion that turns an affirmative conditional conclusion into a negative one, all within the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on conclusion form
Some hold the conclusion's antecedent must come from the conditional premise and its consequent from the composition of the categorical with the conditional's consequent. Others (e.g., Imam Zain al-Din al-Kashi) allow the antecedent to be the composition and the consequent either the antecedent or consequent of the conditional.
The author favors al-Kashi's view as 'closer to nature'.
2
Understand the negative conversion method
To derive a negative conclusion from an affirmative one, the consequent in the negative must be the contradictory (naqīḍ) of the consequent in the affirmative, while the antecedent and quantity remain the same.
This is called inqilāb, distinct from standard 'aks.
3
Walk through the given example
From the categorical 'Every J is B' and the conditional 'If H is Z then every B is A', derive the affirmative conditional 'If H is Z then every J is A'. Then convert it to the negative 'It is not at all the case that if H is Z then not every J is A' by making the consequent the contradictory of the original consequent.
This method applies to all moods of the four figures.
4
Formalize using Arabic variables
Observe the variables: ج (J), ب (B), هـ (H), ز (Z), أ (A). Trace the logical flow: conditional premise → categorical premise → affirmative conclusion → negative conclusion via contradictory consequent.
This formalization helps internalize the structure for independent application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن الأقوال في النتائج مختلفة في كل قسم من هذه الأقسام الأربعة على الخصوص في هذين القسمين: فإن النتيجة في كل ضرب منهما قد تكون متصلة موجبة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة التأليف بين الحملية والتالي كما مر وهذا هو اللازم عند قوم . وقد تكون متصلة موجبة مقدمها نتيجة التأليف؛ وتاليها إما مقدم المتّصلة وإما تاليها؛ فلا يلزم أن يكون هذا على التعيين أو ذلك وهذا من جملة ما ذهب إليه الإمام زين الدين الكشي وإنه هو الأقرب إلى الطبع (۱) . وكما أن الأقوال في النتائج مختلفة فكذلك في الضروب بأنها كم هي؛ وما ذهب إليه الشيخ فذلك على الضعف بالنسبة إلى ما مرَّ ذكره في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة من هذين القسمين لإنتاج السالبة الكلية والجزئية إنتاج الموجبة الكلية والجزئية لكن شرط أن يكون التالي في السالبة نقيض ما يجب أن يكون في الموجبة الموافقة لها في الكم؛ وذلك انقلاب السالبة إلى الموجبة التي توافق السالبة في المقدم والكم، وتناقضها في التالي بناء على القاعدة التي مرَّ ذكرها من قبل؛ فنستنتج من الموجبة المتصلة والحملية المتّصلة الموجبة كما مر . ثمَّ نردها إلى السالبة الموافقة كما في قولنا كل «ج ب»، وليس البتة إذا كان «هـ ز » فليس كل بج» ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان «هـ ز» فليس كل (ج) أ لأن المتّصلة يلزمها كلَّما كان «هز» فكل «ب أ»؛ وإنها مع الحملية تنتج كلَّما كان هز» فكل (ج) أ». ويلزمها ليس البتة إذا كان «هز» فليس كل «ج أ»؛ وهو المطلوب. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب من الأشكال الأربعة.
Know that the opinions regarding the conclusions differ in each of these four divisions, especially in these two sections: for the conclusion in each mood of them may be an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of composition between the categorical and the consequent as passed, and this is what is necessary according to some people. And it may be an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the result of composition; and its consequent is either the antecedent of the conditional or its consequent; so it is not necessary that this be specifically this or that, and this is part of what Imam Zain al-Din al-Kashi held, and that it is the closest to nature. (1) And just as opinions differ regarding the conclusions, likewise regarding the moods as to how many they are; and what the Shaykh held is weak compared to what was previously mentioned in each of the four figures from these two sections for producing universal and particular negatives, producing universal and particular affirmatives, but with the condition that the consequent in the negative be the opposite of what must be in the affirmative that agrees with it in quantity; and that is the conversion of the negative into the affirmative that agrees with the negative in the antecedent and quantity, and contradicts it in the consequent based on the rule mentioned earlier; so we derive from the affirmative conditional and the categorical the affirmative conditional as before. Then we convert it to the corresponding negative, as in our saying: Every J is B, and it is not at all the case that if H is Z then not every B is J, produces: It is not at all the case that if H is Z then not every J is A, because the conditional entails: Whenever H is Z, then every B is A; and it together with the categorical produces: Whenever H is Z, then every J is A. And it entails: It is not at all the case that if H is Z then not every J is A; and that is what is sought. And so on for the remaining moods of the four figures.

Commentary
This passage discusses two main issues: the nature of the conclusion in mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms and a method for obtaining negative conclusions. Regarding the conclusion, some hold that it must have the antecedent from the conditional and the consequent from the composition of the categorical with the conditional's consequent. Others, like Imam Zain al-Din al-Kashi, allow the antecedent to be the composition and the consequent to be either part of the conditional. The author seems to favor the latter view. On the second issue, the author explains a method to produce negative conclusions from affirmative ones by converting the negative into an equivalent affirmative. The condition is that the consequent in the negative must be the contradictory of the consequent in the affirmative. An example is given: from the categorical 'Every J is B' and a conditional premise, an affirmative conditional is derived and then converted to the desired negative form. This method applies to all moods of the four figures.

The conclusion of a conditional-categorical syllogism can take multiple forms, and a negative conclusion can be obtained by converting an affirmative through making the consequent contradictory.

Conditional Syllogism Validity: Refuting an Objection
Author refutes predecessors' objection using the example of void and dimension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the compatibility condition for a valid conditional-categorical syllogism and explain why the void-dimension objection fails.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism is valid only when the categorical premise is compatible with the conditional's antecedent.

Some earlier logicians objected that combining a conditional with a categorical premise does not always yield a valid conclusion, using a clever example about void and dimension. The author refutes this objection by clarifying the exact conditions under which such syllogisms are productive, and by showing that the example itself is based on an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The predecessors claim that the rule for producing a negative conclusion from a conditional and categorical is invalid. Their reasoning: a categorical premise may be true in reality but false under the condition of the conditional's antecedent, leading to absurd conclusions.
2
Analyze the example given
They propose: 'Whenever void exists, dimension exists by itself' (conditional). In reality, 'No self-subsistent thing is a dimension' and 'No dimension is self-subsistent' (categoricals). These would produce conclusions like 'Whenever void exists, not every dimension is a dimension' — a contradiction.
The example is meant to show that the rule fails because the categorical premises are true in reality but not under the condition (since void cannot exist).
3
Extract the author's first response
The author clarifies: they only claim validity when the categorical premise does not conflict with the truth of the conditional's antecedent — i.e., both must be simultaneously true. The objectors' categoricals are incompatible with the antecedent because void is impossible.
This is the key condition: the categorical must be true under the condition of the antecedent, not just in reality.
4
Extract the author's second response
Second, the author does not concede that the conclusions are impossible. Since void is impossible, the conditional's antecedent is never true, so the conditional itself is vacuously true. The syllogisms are irrelevant because the antecedent is unrealizable.
This undercuts the counterexample: if the antecedent can never be true, no contradiction arises.
5
Apply the condition to test syllogisms in Islamic sciences
Practice: take a conditional from fiqh 'If the sale is valid, ownership transfers.' Categorical: 'Every valid sale transfers ownership.' This categorical is compatible with the antecedent because it is true under the condition. The syllogism is valid. Contrast with an incompatible categorical.
Use in usul al-fiqh or kalam to ensure valid mixed syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند قوم من المتقدمين هذا فاسد لما أن تلك القاعدة فاسدة في اعتقادهم؛ وذلك من جملة الاعتقادات الفاسدة لما مر . ثمَّ إنه يتبع إنتاج الحملية مع المتصلة سواء كانت الشركة مع التالي أو مع المقدم لما أنه يمكن صدق الحملية في نفس الأمر وعدم صدقها على تقدير مقدم المتصلة. وذكر لذلك مثالاً وهو أنه يصدق قولنا كلما كان الخلاء موجودًا كان بُعدًا قائما بذاته؛ وقد صدق في نفس الأمر لا شيء من القائم بذاته بعد. وكذلك لا شيء من البعد بقائم بذاته؛ فلو صدقت كل واحدة من هاتين الحمليتين على تقدير مقدم الصغرى لإنتاج الأولى معها من الشكل الأول كلَّما كان الخلاء موجودًا فليس كل بعد ببعد؛ ولا نعني بالخلاء إلا البعد المجرد عن المادة. والثانية معها من الشكل الثالث كلما كان الخلاء موجودًا فليس كل قائم بذاته؛ قائما بذاته. وأجاب عنه أولا بأنا لا ندعي إنتاج المتصلة مع كل حملية صادقة في نفس الأمر مشاركة لأحد طرفيها مشاركة منتجة بل ندعي إنتاج المتصلة مع الحملية التي لا يكون صدقها منافيا لصدق مقدم المتصلة بل يصدقان معا. وعلى هذا لا يرد المنع والنقيض. وثانيًا بأنا لا نسلم استحالة النتيجة فيما ذكرتم من القياسين. وكيف وقد كان وجود الخلاء من المستحيلات؛
And according to some among the predecessors, this is invalid because that rule is invalid in their belief; and that is among the invalid beliefs as has passed. Then it follows that the production of the categorical with the conditional, whether the partnership is with the consequent or with the antecedent, because it is possible that the categorical is true in reality but not true under the condition of the conditional's antecedent. And he mentioned an example: Our saying 'Whenever void exists, then dimension exists subsisting by itself' is true; and in reality, 'nothing that subsists by itself is a dimension' is true. Likewise, 'no dimension subsists by itself'. So if each of these two categoricals were true under the condition of the minor's antecedent, then the first together with it from the first figure would produce: 'Whenever void exists, then not every dimension is a dimension' — and we mean by void only dimension stripped of matter. And the second together with it from the third figure would produce: 'Whenever void exists, then not everything that subsists by itself subsists by itself.' And he answered it first by saying: We do not claim the production of the conditional with every categorical true in reality that shares one of its ends productively; rather, we claim production of the conditional with the categorical whose truth does not conflict with the truth of the conditional's antecedent, but both are true together. On this basis, the objection and its contrary do not hold. And second, we do not concede the impossibility of the conclusion in the two syllogisms you mentioned. And how so, while the existence of void is among the impossibilities?
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a critique and refutation of an objection raised by some earlier scholars. They argued that the method for producing negative conclusions is invalid because the categorical premise can be true in reality but false under the specific condition of the conditional's antecedent. To illustrate, they present an example involving 'void' (al-khalā') and 'dimension' (al-bu'd): if void exists, then dimension subsists by itself; but in reality, nothing that subsists by itself is a dimension, and vice versa. This would lead to paradoxical conclusions like 'whenever void exists, not every dimension is a dimension'. The author gives two responses: first, he clarifies that they only claim validity when the categorical premise and the conditional's antecedent are simultaneously true (i.e., the categorical does not contradict the antecedent). Second, he does not concede the impossibility of the conclusion, noting that the existence of void is itself impossible, so the example is moot. This refines the conditions for valid mixed syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
A conditional-categorical syllogism is valid only when the categorical premise is true under the condition of the conditional's antecedent, not merely true in reality.

Combining Categorical with Conditional Conjunctive: Rule and Defense
Author states rule and rebuts objection with void example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid mixed syllogism combining a categorical and a conditional conjunctive proposition, and defend it against objections regarding premise compatibility.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism's validity hinges on the consistency of its premises, not the impossibility of its antecedent.

In this passage, the author presents the rule for combining a categorical proposition with a conditional conjunctive to produce a valid inference. He then addresses an objection that claims such combinations can lead to absurdities. Understanding this rule sharpens your ability to handle mixed syllogisms in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Locate the conditional conjunctive (if-then) and categorical (subject-predicate) premises in the author's example. Note the variables (ج, ب, هـ, ز, أ).
2
Apply the production rule
The rule: from an affirmative conditional (if HZ then every B is A) and an affirmative categorical (every J is B), infer a negative conditional (never if HZ then not every J is A). Trace the reasoning: the conditional implies that whenever HZ then every B is A; with the categorical 'every J is B', you get whenever HZ then every J is A, which by contradictory gives the conclusion.
This is a mixed syllogism with an immediate inference step.
3
Understand the objection
The objectors claim the rule fails when the categorical is true in itself but false under the antecedent. They use the example of void: conditional 'if void exists, it is a distance subsisting by itself' is true, but categoricals 'nothing subsisting by itself is a distance' and 'nothing that is a distance subsists by itself' are true in reality, leading to absurd conclusions like 'whenever void exists, not every distance is a distance.'
The example uses the impossibility of void to challenge the rule.
4
Analyze the author's two responses
First, the rule only applies when the categorical's truth does not conflict with the antecedent; the categorical must be jointly satisfiable with the antecedent. Second, even absurd results are not problematic because the antecedent (void existence) is impossible; an impossibility can entail an impossibility.
Thus the objection fails because it ignores the condition of joint satisfiability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنستنتج من الموجبة المتصلة والحملية المتصلة الموجبة كما مر. ثم نردها إلى السالبة الموافقة كما في قولنا كل «ج ب»، وليس البتة إذا كان «هـ ز» فليس كل «ب ج» ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان «هـ ز» فليس كل «ج أ» لأن المتصلة يلزمها كلما كان «هـ ز» فكل «ب أ»؛ وإنها مع الحملية تنتج كلما كان «هـ ز» فكل «ج أ». ويلزمها ليس البتة إذا كان «هـ ز» فليس كل «ج أ»؛ وهو المطلوب. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب من الأشكال الأربعة. وعند قوم من المتقدمين هذا فاسد لما أن تلك القاعدة فاسدة في اعتقادهم؛ وذلك من جملة الاعتقادات الفاسدة لما مر. ثم إنه يتبع إنتاج الحملية مع المتصلة سواء كانت الشركة مع التالي أو مع المقدم لما أنه يمكن صدق الحملية في نفس الأمر وعدم صدقها على تقدير مقدم المتصلة. وذكر لذلك مثالاً وهو أنه يصدق قولنا كلما كان الخلاء موجودًا كان بعدًا قائمًا بذاته؛ وقد صدق في نفس الأمر لا شيء من القائم بذاته بعد. وكذلك لا شيء من البعد بقائم بذاته؛ فلو صدقت كل واحدة من هاتين الحمليتين على تقدير مقدم الصغرى لإنتاج الأولى معها من الشكل الأول كلما كان الخلاء موجودًا فليس كل بعد ببعد؛ ولا نعني بالخلاء إلا البعد المجرد عن المادة. والثانية معها من الشكل الثالث كلما كان الخلاء موجودًا فليس كل قائم بذاته؛ قائمًا بذاته. وأجاب عنه أولاً بأنا لا ندعي إنتاج المتصلة مع كل حملية صادقة في نفس الأمر مشاركة لأحد طرفيها مشاركة منتجة بل ندعي إنتاج المتصلة مع الحملية التي لا يكون صدقها منافيًا لصدق مقدم المتصلة بل يصدقان معًا. وعلى هذا لا يرد المنع والنقيض. وثانيًا بأنا لا نسلم استحالة النتيجة فيما ذكرتم من القياسين. وكيف وقد كان وجود الخلاء من المستحيلات؛ ولا يمتنع أن يكون المحال مستلزمًا للمحال. وبالجملة فإنه إذا ادعي إنتاج الحملية مع المتصلة التي يمكن صدق مقدمها معها سواء كان مقدمها صادقًا في نفس الأمر أو لا يكون، فإنه من جملة ما لا يرد عليه المنع أصلاً يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
We infer from the affirmative conditional conjunctive and the affirmative categorical as before. Then we reduce it to the contradictory negative as in our saying: 'Every J is B', and 'It is never the case that if it is H Z then not every B is J' yields 'It is never the case that if it is H Z then not every J is A' because the conditional conjunctive necessarily implies that whenever it is H Z then every B is A; and it with the categorical produces that whenever it is H Z then every J is A. And it necessarily implies that it is never the case that if it is H Z then not every J is A; which is what is sought. And similarly for all the moods of the four figures. According to some of the predecessors, this is invalid because that rule is invalid in their belief; and that is among the invalid beliefs as mentioned. Then it follows that the production of the categorical with the conditional conjunctive, whether the sharing is with the consequent or with the antecedent, because it is possible for the categorical to be true in itself and not true under the assumption of the antecedent of the conditional conjunctive. He gave an example for that: our saying 'Whenever void exists, it is a distance subsisting by itself' is true; while in itself 'Nothing subsisting by itself is a distance' is true. And also 'Nothing that is a distance subsists by itself'. So if each of these two categoricals were true under the assumption of the antecedent of the minor, the first with it from the first figure would produce 'Whenever void exists, not every distance is a distance'; and we mean by void only the distance stripped of matter. And the second with it from the third figure would produce 'Whenever void exists, not everything subsisting by itself is subsisting by itself'. He answered it first: we do not claim the production of the conditional conjunctive with every categorical true in itself sharing one of its two terms in a productive sharing, but we claim the production of the conditional conjunctive with the categorical whose truth does not contradict the truth of the antecedent of the conditional conjunctive, but rather they are both true together. And on this, the objection and the contradictory do not apply. And second: we do not admit the impossibility of the result in the two syllogisms you mentioned. How so? Since the existence of void is among impossibilities; and it is not impossible for the impossible to entail the impossible. In short, if the production of the categorical with the conditional conjunctive whose antecedent can be true with it is claimed, whether its antecedent is true in itself or not, it is among things to which the objection does not apply at all — known by reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule for combining a categorical (حملي) proposition with a conditional conjunctive (متصل) proposition to form a mixed syllogism. The author first states the rule: from an affirmative conditional conjunctive and an affirmative categorical that share a term, one can infer a negative conditional conjunctive, as illustrated with variables J, B, H, Z, A. He then generalizes to all moods of the four figures. Next, he presents an objection from some predecessors who consider this rule invalid. Their argument is that the categorical may be true in itself but false under the assumption of the antecedent of the conditional, giving the example of void (خلاء) and distance (بعد). They claim that if the categoricals 'Nothing subsisting by itself is a distance' and 'Nothing that is a distance subsists by itself' are each combined with the conditional 'Whenever void exists, it is a distance subsisting by itself', absurd results follow: 'Whenever void exists, not every distance is a distance' and 'Whenever void exists, not everything subsisting by itself is subsisting by itself'. The author responds in two ways. First, he clarifies that the rule is not claimed for any arbitrary categorical true in itself, but only for one whose truth does not conflict with the truth of the antecedent of the conditional; both must be simultaneously possible. Second, he argues that even the absurd results are not problematic because the antecedent (existence of void) is impossible; an impossibility can entail an impossibility. Thus the objection is deflected. The passage ends by reaffirming that when the antecedent of the conditional can be true together with the categorical, no objection applies.

Key Terms
متصل
حملي
منع
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, what condition must the categorical premise satisfy for the mixed syllogism to be valid?
A) It must be true in reality.
B) It must share a term with the conditional.
C) Its truth must not contradict the truth of the antecedent.
D) The antecedent must be impossible.

Key Takeaway
A mixed syllogism combining a conditional and a categorical requires that both premises be simultaneously satisfiable, and absurd conclusions from impossible premises do not invalidate the rule.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Structure of Productive Mixed Syllogisms
Author lays out the definition and conditions for a productive categorical-disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the required structure and conditions for a productive categorical-disjunctive syllogism and verify whether a given composite syllogism meets them.

Core Idea
A productive mixed syllogism requires equal categoricals and disjuncts, each sharing a term with one disjunct.

In the previous lessons, we learned about pure categorical and pure disjunctive syllogisms. Now we turn to composite syllogisms that combine both. The author explains the fourth division: productive syllogisms that yield a categorical conclusion from a disjunctive and several categoricals. Understanding the precise conditions for such a syllogism to be valid is essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The author divides composite categorical-disjunctive syllogisms into productive and non-productive. Focus on productive.
2
Count the parts of the disjunction
The disjunctive must have two or more parts (alternatives).
3
Match the number of categoricals
The number of categorical premises must equal the number of disjuncts.
This condition ensures each alternative is addressed.
4
Check the sharing of terms
Each categorical must share one part of the disjunction in one extreme of the conclusion.
5
Identify the middle term
The common concept between categoricals and disjuncts is the middle term; it must be multiple distinct concepts.
Otherwise the syllogism is invalid.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع من الأقسام الخمسة: فيما يتألف من الحملية والمنفصلة، وإنه على قسمين: الأول منهما هو القياس المقسم المنتج لحملية، والثاني ما لا يكون كذلك. أما الأول: فإنه يجب أن يكون تركيبه من منفصلة ذات جزأين أو ذات أجزاء كثيرة، وحمليات عددها مثل عدد أجزاء الانفصال تشارك كل واحدة من الحمليات جزءًا واحدًا من أجزاء الانفصال في أحد طرفي المطلوب. وبيانه في الطرف الآخر منه، وبالضرورة يكون الطرفان المتباينان هما طرفا النتيجة. ويلزم اشتراك الحمليات في أحدهما والانفصال في الآخر. والمشترك بين الحمليات وأجزاء الانفصال هو الحد الأوسط، ولا بد من مفهومات مختلفة متعددة حسب تعدد أجزاء الانفصال والحمليات وإلا لا يصح. ثم المنفصلة إن كانت هي الصغرى فتلك المفهومات محمولات في أجزاء الانفصال موضوعات في الحمليات في الشكل الأول كقولنا إما أن يكون كل «أ ب» أو كل «أ ج» أو كل «أ د»، وكل «ب هـ» وكل «ج هـ» وكل «د هـ»؛ وموضوعات في أجزاء الانفصال محمولات في الحمليات في الشكل الرابع كقولنا إما أن يكون كل «ب أ» أو كل «ج أ» أو كل «د أ»، وكل «هـ ب» وكل «هـ ج» وكل «هـ د». وإن كانت المنفصلة كبرى فتلك المفهومات محمولات في الحمليات موضوعات في أجزاء الانفصال في الشكل الأول كقولنا كل «أ ب» وكل «أ ج» وكل «أ د» وإما أن يكون كل «ب هـ» أو كل «ج هـ» أو كل «د هـ».
The fourth division of the five divisions: concerning what is composed of categorical and disjunctive, and it is of two types: the first of them is the divisive syllogism productive of a categorical, and the second is not like that. As for the first: its composition must be from a disjunctive with two parts or with many parts, and categoricals whose number equals the number of the parts of the disjunction, each one of the categoricals sharing one part of the parts of the disjunction in one of the two ends of the sought conclusion. And its explanation is in the other end of it, and necessarily the two different ends are the two ends of the conclusion. And it is necessary that the categoricals share in one of them and the disjunction in the other. And what is common between the categoricals and the parts of the disjunction is the middle term, and there must be multiple different concepts according to the multiplicity of the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals, otherwise it is not valid. Then if the disjunctive is the minor, then those concepts are predicates in the parts of the disjunction and subjects in the categoricals in the first figure, like our saying: 'Either every A is B, or every A is J, or every A is D', and 'Every B is H', and 'Every J is H', and 'Every D is H'; and subjects in the parts of the disjunction and predicates in the categoricals in the fourth figure, like our saying: 'Either every B is A, or every J is A, or every D is A', and 'Every H is B', and 'Every H is J', and 'Every H is D'. And if the disjunctive is the major, then those concepts are predicates in the categoricals and subjects in the parts of the disjunction in the first figure, like our saying: 'Every A is B', and 'Every A is J', and 'Every A is D', and 'Either every B is H, or every J is H, or every D is H'.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage classifies and explains the fourth division of mixed syllogisms: those composed of categorical and disjunctive propositions. The author divides this into two types: productive (produces a categorical conclusion) and non-productive. The focus here is the productive type. The structure requires a disjunctive (منفصلة) with two or more parts and an equal number of categoricals that each share one part of the disjunction in one of the two extremes of the desired conclusion. The two extremes of the conclusion are the two disparate terms. The common term between the categoricals and the disjunctive parts is the middle term (حد أوسط). Importantly, there must be multiple distinct concepts corresponding to the number of parts and categoricals. The author then illustrates the arrangement depending on whether the disjunctive is the minor or major premise. If the disjunctive is the minor, then the shared concepts (middle terms) are predicates in the disjunctive parts and subjects in the categoricals, giving the first figure mood, e.g., 'Either every A is B, or every A is J, or every A is D; every B is H; every J is H; every D is H' leading to 'Every A is H'? (not explicit but implied). Alternatively, in the fourth figure, the shared concepts are subjects in the disjunctive parts and predicates in the categoricals. If the disjunctive is the major, then the shared concepts are predicates in the categoricals and subjects in the disjunctive parts, again in the first figure. The passage ends with an example for that case. This is a technical analysis of the combinatorial possibilities for productive categorical-disjunctive syllogisms.

Key Terms
منفصلة
قياس مقسم
حد أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They can be any number.
B) They must equal the number of disjuncts.
C) They must be one less than the disjuncts.
D) They must be one more than the disjuncts.

Key Takeaway
A productive categorical-disjunctive syllogism requires the number of categorical premises to match the number of disjunctive alternatives, with each categorical sharing one alternative term.

Figures in Categorical-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author details the two possible figures (first and fourth) for productive mixed syllogisms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure (first or fourth) of a productive categorical-disjunctive syllogism based on the position of the disjunctive and the subject-predicate relations of the shared terms.

Core Idea
The position of the disjunctive and the role of the middle term determine the figure of the mixed syllogism.

After establishing the general conditions, the author now explains how the arrangement of the propositions affects the figure of the syllogism. Depending on whether the disjunctive is the minor or major premise, and how the shared concepts appear as subjects or predicates, the syllogism may take the first or fourth figure. This analysis is crucial for checking the validity and productivity of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Determine the position of the disjunctive
Identify if the disjunctive premise is the minor or the major.
2
Identify the shared concepts (middle terms)
These are the terms that appear in both the categoricals and the disjunctive parts.
3
Check subject-predicate roles in first figure (disjunctive minor)
When disjunctive is minor and shared concepts are predicates in disjunctive parts and subjects in categoricals, it's first figure.
4
Check subject-predicate roles in fourth figure (disjunctive minor)
When disjunctive is minor and shared concepts are subjects in disjunctive parts and predicates in categoricals, it's fourth figure.
5
Check first figure with disjunctive major
When disjunctive is major, shared concepts are predicates in categoricals and subjects in disjunctive parts, first figure.

The Syllogism

Basic Composition of Categorical-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author details conditions and figures for categorical-disjunctive syllogisms yielding a categorical conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the required components (equal number of categorical premises and disjuncts, the middle term) for a categorical-disjunctive syllogism to produce a categorical conclusion.

Core Idea
A valid categorical-disjunctive syllogism requires matching numbers of categoricals and disjuncts, with a unifying middle term.

This passage introduces the fourth division of compound syllogisms: those composed of a categorical and a disjunctive premise. It explains the essential structure: the disjunction must have two or more parts, and there must be an equal number of categorical premises, each sharing a part with the disjunction. Understanding this structure is the foundation for constructing such syllogisms correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types in this division
The passage divides this section into two: those that produce a categorical conclusion and those that do not. Focus on the first type. Note that the structure requires a disjunctive premise with two or more parts.
2
Count the categorical premises
The number of categorical premises must equal the number of disjuncts. Each categorical shares one disjunct in one extreme of the desired conclusion. Trace this in the example later.
3
Locate the middle term
The shared term between the categorical premises and the parts of the disjunction is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). Because the disjuncts are different, there will be multiple middle-term concepts, one for each pair. This is necessary for validity.
4
Understand the condition of unity
All compositions within the syllogism must converge on a single conclusion. If not, the result is a disjunction of exclusive alternatives (mani'at al-khuluw) rather than a categorical conclusion.
This condition prevents the syllogism from having multiple independent conclusions.

والبرهان على الإنتاج فيه صدق واحد من أجزاء الانفصال مع ما يشاركه من الحملية وانتظامهما من ذلك الشكل: مثال ما يكون من شكل واحد قولنا إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل «أد» أو كل «أه».
The fourth of the five divisions: concerning what is composed of categorical and disjunctive [premises], and it is of two types: the first is the dividing syllogism that produces a categorical conclusion, and the second is not like that. As for the first: its composition must be from a disjunctive with two parts or with many parts, and categoricals whose number equals the number of the parts of the disjunction, each one of the categoricals sharing one part of the parts of the disjunction in one of the two extremes of the desired [conclusion]. And its clarification is in the other extreme of it, and necessarily the two distinct extremes are the extremes of the conclusion. And it is necessary that the categoricals share in one of them and the disjunction in the other. And the shared [term] between the categoricals and the parts of the disjunction is the middle term, and multiple different concepts are necessary according to the multiplicity of the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals, otherwise it is not valid. Then, if the disjunctive is the minor, then those concepts are predicates in the parts of the disjunction and subjects in the categoricals in the first figure, like our saying: either every A is B, or every A is C, or every A is D; and every B is E, and every C is E, and every D is E; and subjects in the parts of the disjunction and predicates in the categoricals in the fourth figure, like our saying: either every B is A, or every C is A, or every D is A; and every E is B, and every E is C, and every E is D. And if the disjunctive is the major, then those concepts are predicates in the categoricals and subjects in the parts of the disjunction in the first figure, like our saying: every A is B, and every A is C, and every A is D; and either every B is E, or every C is E, or every D is E; and subjects in the categoricals and predicates in the parts of the disjunction in the fourth figure, like our saying: every B is A, and every C is A, and every D is A; and either every B is E, or every C is E, or every D is E. And as for the second figure, they are predicates in the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals together, whether the disjunctive is minor or major. And in the third figure, they are subjects in the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals together as well. As for the second with disjunctive minor, like our saying: either every A is B, or every A is C, or every A is D; and no E is B, and no E is C, and no E is D. And as for the second with disjunctive major, like our saying: every A is B, and every A is C, and every A is D; and either no E is B, or no E is C, or no E is D. And as for the third with disjunctive minor, like our saying: either every B is A, or every C is A, or every D is A; and either every B is E, or every C is E, or every D is E. And as for the third with disjunctive major, like our saying: every B is A, and every C is A, and every D is A; and either every B is E, or every C is E, or every D is E. And the condition in each of the four figures is what is in that figure, such as affirmation in each one of the parts of the disjunction, and universality of the categoricals if the compositions are from the first figure when the disjunctive is minor, and affirmation of each one of the categoricals, and universality of the parts of the disjunction in it when the disjunctive is major. And similarly in the remaining figures. And among the conditions in this division is the unity of the compositions that the syllogism contains into one conclusion, whether the compositions are from one figure or from different figures, otherwise it does not produce a categorical conclusion but rather a disjunction of exclusive alternatives. And the proof for its production is the truth of one of the parts of the disjunction with what shares with it from the categorical and their arrangement in that figure. An example of what is from one figure is our saying: either every A is B, or every A is D, or every A is E.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth of five divisions of compound syllogisms: those composed of a categorical and a disjunctive premise. It divides into two types: those yielding a categorical conclusion and those that do not. The focus here is on the first type. The author explains that the syllogism must contain a disjunctive premise with two or more parts, and an equal number of categorical premises, each sharing one part of the disjunction in one extreme of the desired conclusion. The shared term between the categoricals and the disjunction parts is the middle term. Then the author details the four figures for both cases: when the disjunctive is minor or major. For the first figure, if disjunctive is minor, the concepts (middle terms) are predicates in the disjunction parts and subjects in the categoricals; if disjunctive is major, they are predicates in the categoricals and subjects in the disjunction parts. For the fourth figure, the roles reverse. For the second figure, the concepts are predicates in both; for the third, subjects in both. Examples are given using variable letters (A, B, C, etc.) to illustrate each combination. Conditions for validity: in the first figure, if the disjunctive is minor, each categorical must be affirmative and each part of the disjunction universal; if the disjunctive is major, each categorical must be affirmative and each disjunction part universal. Similar conditions apply to other figures. Additionally, all compositions must converge on a single conclusion; otherwise, the result is a disjunction of exclusive alternatives rather than a categorical. The proof is that the truth of one disjunct together with its corresponding categorical yields the conclusion. The example started: 'Either every A is B, or every A is D, or every A is E' (incomplete). This systematic treatment helps the student understand how to construct valid categorical-disjunctive syllogisms in any figure.

مانعة الخلو

A) There must be exactly two categorical premises.
B) The number of categorical premises must equal the number of disjuncts.
C) The number of categorical premises must be one less than the number of disjuncts.
D) The number of categorical premises must be one more than the number of disjuncts.

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical-disjunctive syllogism requires an equal count of categorical premises and disjuncts, with each pair sharing a unique middle term.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Figures and Conditions for Categorical-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author details conditions and figures for categorical-disjunctive syllogisms yielding a categorical conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a categorical-disjunctive syllogism in any of the four figures, applying the correct conditions of affirmation and universality for each figure.

Core Idea
The position of the middle term determines the figure, and each figure has specific conditions on the quantity and quality of premises.

After establishing the basic structure, the passage delves into the four figures, showing how the middle term is positioned when the disjunctive is minor or major. For each figure, there are conditions on whether the categoricals and disjuncts must be affirmative and universal. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid syllogisms in practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish when the disjunctive is minor vs. major
The passage treats two cases: disjunctive as minor premise (first in order) and disjunctive as major premise. Note how the roles of the middle term change between these cases.
The middle term is either subject or predicate in the categoricals and in the disjuncts depending on the figure.
2
Trace the middle term in the first figure
In the first figure, when disjunctive is minor, the middle terms are predicates in the disjuncts and subjects in the categoricals (e.g., 'either every A is B, or every A is C; every B is E, every C is E'). When disjunctive is major, the pattern reverses: middle terms are predicates in categoricals and subjects in disjuncts.
The example uses letters A, B, C, D, E as placeholders.
3
Trace the middle term in the second and third figures
In the second figure, the middle term is a predicate in both the disjuncts and the categoricals (regardless of which is minor/major). In the third figure, it is a subject in both. Verify with the examples given.
The passage provides explicit examples for each combination.
4
Apply the condition of affirmation and universality
For the first figure when disjunctive is minor: each categorical must be affirmative and universal. When disjunctive is major: each disjunct must be affirmative and universal. Similar conditions apply to other figures, following the standard rules of those figures.
This is derived from the general conditions of each figure in categorical logic (e.g., for first figure to be valid, the minor premise must be affirmative and the major universal).
5
Ensure unity of composition
The final condition: all compositions within the syllogism must lead to a single conclusion. If they produce multiple conclusions, the result is a disjunction of exclusive alternatives (mani'at al-khuluw) rather than a categorical.

Conditions for Valid Mixed Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author describes conditions for mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogisms across figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogism meets the conditions for a valid categorical conclusion, based on figure and proposition type.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism yields a categorical conclusion only when all parts align and the conclusion is unified.

This passage expands on the logic of mixed syllogisms combining disjunctive and categorical premises across different figures. The author specifies how terms function in the second and third figures and lays down strict conditions for validity, including the type of disjunction and the requirement of a single conclusion. In this lesson, we will walk through these conditions using the author's own examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and position of the disjunctive
Determine whether the syllogism belongs to the first, second, third, or fourth figure, and whether the disjunctive premise is the minor or major premise. This affects the logical roles of the terms.
The author explicitly discusses the second and third figures.
2
Check the condition on the disjunctive premise
The disjunctive must be either 'truly affirmative' (inclusive or) or 'exclusive-of-void' (at least one true). It cannot be 'exclusive-of-union' (at most one true), because then the combination with the categorical would not guarantee truth.
The author states: 'ثم من اللوازم أن تكون المتصلة موجبة حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو'.
3
Examine the categorical premises
Depending on the figure and which premise is disjunctive, the categorical premises must be universal and affirmative. For example, in the first figure with the disjunctive as minor, all categoricals must be universal and affirmative.
The author says: 'والشرط في كل شكل... إيجاب كل واحد من الحمليات، وكلية أجزاء الانفصال'.
4
Ensure unity of conclusion
All the compositions (the disjunctive parts and the categoricals) must converge into a single categorical conclusion. If they produce different conclusions, the result is only a disjunctive exclusive-of-void, not a categorical.
The author emphasizes: 'اتحاد التأليفات التي اشتمل عليها القياس في نتيجة واحدة'.
5
Test with the given examples
Apply the conditions to the two examples provided: one with a single figure and one with mixed figures. Verify that the conclusion follows according to the rules.
Example 1: 'إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «أد» أو كل «أه»، وكل «بج» وكل «دج» وكل «هـج» ينتج كل «أج»'.

وأما في الشكل الثاني فإنها محمولات في أجزاء الانفصال والحمليات معا سواء كانت المتصلة صغرى أو كبرى. وفي الشكل الثالث موضوعات في أجزاء الانفصال والحمليات معا كذلك. أما في الثاني والمنفصلة صغرى فكقولنا إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «أج» أو كل «أد»، ولا شيء من «هـ ب» ولا شيء من «هـ ج» ولا شيء من «هـ د». وأما في الثاني والمنفصلة كبرى فكقولنا كل «أب» وكل «أج» وكل «أد» وإما أن يكون لا شيء من «هـ ب» أو لا شيء من «هـ ج» أو لا شيء من «هـ د». وأما في الثالث والمنفصلة صغرى فكقولنا إما أن يكون كل «ب أ» أو كل «ج أ» أو كل «د أ» وإما أن يكون كل «ب هـ» أو كل «ج هـ» أو كل «د هـ». وأما في الثالث والمنفصلة كبرى فكقولنا كل «ب أ» وكل «ج أ» وكل «د أ» وإما أن يكون كل «ب هـ» أو كل «ج هـ» أو كل «د هـ». والشرط في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة ما يكون في ذلك الشكل نحو الإيجاب في كل واحد من أجزاء الانفصال، وكلية الحمليات إذا كانت التأليفات من الشكل الأول إذا كانت المنفصلة صغرى وإيجاب كل واحد من الحمليات، وكلية أجزاء الانفصال فيه إذا كانت المنفصلة كبرى. وعلى هذا في الأشكال الباقية. ومن الشرائط في هذا القسم اتحاد التأليفات التي اشتمل عليها القياس في نتيجة واحدة سواء كانت التأليفات من شكل واحد أو من أشكال مختلفة وإلا لم ينتج الحملية بل مانعة الخلو. والبرهان على الإنتاج فيه صدق واحد من أجزاء الانفصال مع ما يشاركه من الحملية وانتظامهما من ذلك الشكل: مثال ما يكون من شكل واحد قولنا إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «أد» أو كل «أه»، وكل «بج» وكل «دج» وكل «هـج» ينتج كل «أج» بالبرهان الذي مر ذكره. مثال ما يكون من أشكال مختلفة قولنا إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «أد» أو لا شيء من «هـ أ»، ولا شيء من «بج» ولا شيء من «ج د» وكل «ج هـ» ينتج لا شيء من «أج» من أحد الأشكال الثلاثة التي اشتمل القياس عليها لما مر من البرهان. ثم من اللوازم أن تكون المتصلة موجبة حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو وإلا لا يلزم اجتماع الجزء المشارك من المنفصلة مع الحملية المشاركة إياه على الصدق. ولا يقال مانعة الجمع منتجة أيضا بواسطة مانعة الخلو؛ إذ ذاك لا يعتبر، هذا من جملة ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين؛ وإنه وما هو بمثله في حيز المنع يعرف من بعد.
As for the second figure, they are predicates in the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals together, whether the conditional is minor or major. In the third figure, they are subjects in the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals together as well. As for the second figure with the disjunctive as minor, it is like our saying: Either every A is B or every A is C or every A is D, and no H is B and no H is C and no H is D. As for the second figure with the disjunctive as major, it is like our saying: Every A is B and every A is C and every A is D, and either no H is B or no H is C or no H is D. As for the third figure with the disjunctive as minor, it is like our saying: Either every B is A or every C is A or every D is A, and either every B is H or every C is H or every D is H. As for the third figure with the disjunctive as major, it is like our saying: Every B is A and every C is A and every D is A, and either every B is H or every C is H or every D is H. The condition in each of the four figures is what is in that figure regarding affirmation in each one of the parts of the disjunction, and the universality of the categoricals if the compositions are from the first figure when the disjunctive is minor, and affirmation of each one of the categoricals, and universality of the parts of the disjunction in it when the disjunctive is major. And similarly for the remaining figures. Among the conditions in this section is the unity of the compositions that the syllogism contains into one conclusion, whether the compositions are from one figure or from different figures; otherwise it does not produce a categorical but rather an exclusive-of-void. The proof for its production is the truth of one of the parts of the disjunction together with what shares with it from the categorical and their arrangement according to that figure. An example of what is from one figure: our saying 'Either every A is B or every A is D or every A is H, and every B is C and every D is C and every H is C' produces 'Every A is C' by the proof that was mentioned. An example of what is from different figures: our saying 'Either every A is B or every A is D or no H is A, and no B is C and no C is D and every C is H' produces 'No A is C' from one of the three figures that the syllogism contains, according to what was mentioned of the proof. Then among the necessary conditions is that the conditional must be truly affirmative or exclusive-of-void; otherwise the combination of the shared part of the disjunction with the categorical sharing it being true does not follow. And it is not said that the exclusive-of-union also produces by means of the exclusive-of-void, because that is not considered. This is among what some of the predecessors held; and it and the like are in the scope of denial, known later.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of mixed syllogisms that combine a disjunctive premise (manfasila) with categorical premises (hamliyya) across different figures. The author begins by describing the second and third figures: in the second figure, the middle terms are predicates in both the disjunctive parts and the categoricals; in the third figure, they are subjects. He then gives specific examples for each figure with the disjunctive as minor or major, using placeholders A, B, C, D, H. The key condition for validity in the first figure is that when the disjunctive is minor, the categoricals must be universal and affirmative; when the disjunctive is major, the disjunctive parts must be universal. The same applies analogously to other figures. Another important condition is that all the compositions (i.e., the disjunctive and categorical premises) must converge on a single conclusion; otherwise the inference yields only a disjunctive exclusive-of-void (man‘at al-khuluw) rather than a categorical conclusion. The author provides two examples: one where all compositions are within the same figure, and one across different figures. Finally, he stresses that the conditional (here meaning the disjunctive premise) must be either truly affirmative (i.e., inclusive disjunction where both can be true) or exclusive-of-void (i.e., at least one true), because if it were exclusive-of-union (man‘at al-jam‘, meaning at most one true), the combination with the categorical would not guarantee truth. He notes that some predecessors claimed the exclusive-of-union also produces via the exclusive-of-void, but this is denied.

Key Takeaway
A valid mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogism requires that the disjunction be truly affirmative or exclusive-of-void, the categoricals be universal and affirmative as per the figure, and all compositions yield a single categorical conclusion.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
First type of non-dividing disjunctive syllogisms
Classification of the first type of non-dividing disjunctive syllogisms with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the first type of non-dividing disjunctive syllogism, ensuring the number of categoricals equals the number of disjuncts, and verify the exclusive-of-void conclusion.

Core Idea
Equal pairing of categoricals and disjuncts yields a compound disjunctive conclusion.

In the previous lesson, we distinguished between dividing and non-dividing disjunctive premises. Now, we delve into the first type of non-dividing mixed syllogisms. Here, the disjunction is simple and the categorical premises are paired one-to-one with the disjuncts. The resulting conclusion is an exclusive-of-void disjunction, meaning at least one of the individual conclusions must be true but they cannot all be false. This structure is powerful for combining multiple lines of evidence into a single comprehensive judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction and the categoricals
Look for a simple disjunction with n disjuncts (e.g., 'إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو كل ج ب أو كل د ب') and n categorical premises (e.g., 'وكل ب هـ وكل ب ج وكل ب ط'). Ensure the number of categoricals equals the number of disjuncts.
The disjunction is non-dividing, meaning the disjuncts are not mutually exclusive in terms of dividing a subject.
2
Pair each categorical with one disjunct
Pair the first categorical with the first disjunct, the second with the second, etc. Each pair forms a categorical syllogism. For example, from 'كل أ ب' and 'كل ب هـ', the conclusion is 'كل أ هـ'.
The author assumes each pairing is productive (i.e., yields a valid conclusion). The figures may be the same or different.
3
Derive the individual conclusions
For each pair, deduce the conclusion using the rules of categorical syllogisms. In the examples, all are first-figure Barbara: the middle term is the one shared between the categorical and the disjunct.
If the middle term is not shared across pairs, each pair may have a different middle term.
4
Combine the conclusions into an exclusive-of-void disjunction
Combine all the individual conclusions using the exclusive-of-void (مانعة الخلو) connector. For example, the three conclusions 'كل أ هـ', 'كل ج هـ', 'كل د ط' become 'إما أن يكون كل أ هـ أو كل ج هـ أو كل د ط'.
The final disjunction means at least one conclusion must be true, but not necessarily all.
5
Check for shared or unshared middle terms
Notice that in the first example, all categoricals share the same middle term (ب), while in the second, each categorical has a different middle term (ب, د, ز). The structure still works as long as each pairing is valid.
The condition of no shared term beyond the middle term refers to the categoricals not sharing a limit (term) other than the middle term.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second of the two divisions, which is that which does not divide, is divided into four types: The first is that the number of categoricals equals the number of parts of the disjunction, each categorical sharing one part of the disjunction in a composition that produces, but the categoricals do not share in a limit other than the middle term, nor do the parts of the disjunction; for it produces an exclusive-of-void according to those people, as mentioned, and it is composed of the conclusions of all the compositions from one figure or from different figures, whether the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals share the middle term or not. An example of compositions from one figure with sharing: our saying 'Either every A is B or every C is B or every D is B, and every B is H and every B is C and every B is T' produces 'Either every A is H or every C is H or every D is T' as mentioned. An example of compositions from one figure without sharing: our saying 'Either every A is B or every C is D or every W is Z, and every B is H and every D is T and every Z is C' produces 'Either every A is H or every C is T or every W is C' as mentioned. An example of compositions from different figures and parts...
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major division of mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogisms: the type where the disjunctive premise does not divide the subject (i.e., it is a simple exclusive disjunction). The author says it has four subtypes. The first subtype is when the number of categorical premises equals the number of disjuncts, each categorical pairs with one disjunct in a productive combination, but no two categoricals share a term other than the middle term, and the disjuncts also do not share a term. The conclusion is an exclusive-of-void (man‘at al-khuluw) disjunction composed of the conclusions of each pairing. This can occur with all compositions in the same figure or different figures, and with or without the disjuncts and categoricals sharing the middle term. The author provides two examples: one with sharing of the middle term (all categoricals have the same middle 'B') and one without sharing (each categorical has a different middle). The example variables are letters A, B, C, D, H, T, W, Z. The text cuts off at the page break, but the pattern is clear.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Conditions for Valid Mixed Syllogisms
Author states necessary condition for mixed syllogisms and refutes a contrary view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary condition that the conjunctive premise must be a true universal or exclusive disjunction for a mixed syllogism to be valid, and distinguish acceptable from unacceptable disjunction types.

Core Idea
The conjunctive premise must be a true universal or exclusive disjunction; otherwise, the syllogism fails.

In mixed syllogisms combining categorical and disjunctive premises, the quality of the conjunctive premise determines validity. This lesson unpacks the author's crucial condition that the muttasila must be either a true universal (موجبة حقيقية) or an exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو), and explains why exclusive joint denial (مانعة الجمع) is rejected in this context.

Guided Path
1
Locate the condition statement
Find where the author says 'من اللوازم أن تكون المتصلة موجبة حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو'. This is the core necessary condition.
The condition applies to the conjunctive premise, not the disjunctive.
2
Understand why the condition is necessary
Without this condition, the shared part of the disjunction and the categorical premise cannot be guaranteed to be simultaneously true, breaking the syllogism's productivity.
Think of it as ensuring the premises are compatible.
3
Identify the refuted view
The author rejects the claim that exclusive joint denial (مانعة الجمع) can produce a conclusion. Note his phrase 'لا يقال مانعة الجمع منتجة أيضًا'.
This refutation is directed at a group of earlier scholars.
4
Apply the condition to an example
Take the example 'إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو كل أ د أو لا شيء من ه أ' with categoricals 'لا شيء من ب ج' etc. Check if the muttasila satisfies the condition.
In the example, the disjunction is not a single conjunctive premise; the condition is about the muttasila part of the mixed syllogism, which here is a complex proposition.

And every 'B J' and every 'D J' and every 'H J' produces every 'A J' by the proof mentioned earlier. An example from different figures: we say 'Every (A B) or every (A D) or no (H A)', and 'No (B J)' and 'No (J D)' and 'Every J (H)' produces 'No (A J)' from one of the three figures which the syllogism contains, according to the proof mentioned. Then it is necessary that the conjunctive premise be true universal or exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw); otherwise the co-occurrence of the shared part of the disjunction with the categorical premise sharing it in truth does not follow. And it is not said that exclusive joint denial (mani'at al-jam') also produces by means of exclusive disjunction, for that is not considered. This is part of what a group of earlier scholars held; and that which is like it is in the realm of being prevented, known later. The second of the two divisions—which is not partitioned—has four types: [1] First, that the number of categorical premises equals the number of parts of the disjunction, each categorical sharing one part of the disjunction in a productive composition, but the categoricals do not share a term other than the middle term, nor do the parts of the disjunction share in it; then it produces an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw) according to those people as mentioned, and it is composed of the results of all the compositions from one figure or from different figures, whether the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals share the middle term or not. Example of compositions from one figure with sharing: we say 'Either every (A B) or every (J B) or every (D B)', and 'Every (B H)' and 'Every (B J)' and 'Every (B T)' produces 'Either every (A H) or every (J H) or every (D T)' as mentioned. Example from one figure without sharing: we say 'Either every (A B) or every (J D) or every (W Z)', and 'Every (B H)' and 'Every (W T)' and 'Every (Z J)' produces 'Either every (A H) or every (J T) or every (W J)' as mentioned. Example of compositions from different figures and parts with sharing as mentioned: we say 'Either every (A B) or every (J B) or every (B T)', and 'Every (B H)' and 'No (T B)' and 'Every (B J)' produces 'Either every (A H) or no (J T) or some (T J)'. Example from different figures without sharing: we say 'Either every (A B) or every (J D) or every (D T)', and 'Every (B H)' and 'No (T D)' and 'Every (D J)' produces 'Either every (A H) or no (T) or some (T J)' as mentioned. [B] The second division produces the mentioned conclusion but its parts share in the subject if the shared thing is in one of the two ends of the conclusion, whether the parts of the disjunction or categoricals placed in the position of the minor premise, and in the predicate if they are placed in the position of the major premise, because of the impossibility of the occurring vacuity from one of the parts of the disjunction together with what shares with it from the categoricals. Example where the parts of the disjunction alone share in one of the two ends and the disjunction is minor premise: we say 'Either every (A B) or every (A D) or every (A J)', and 'Every (B H)' and 'Every (Z T)' and 'Every (J H)' produces 'Either every (A H) or every (A T) or every (A J)' as mentioned. Example where the parts of the disjunction alone share and the disjunction is major premise: we say 'Every (A B)' and 'Every (J D)' and 'Every (T W)' and 'Either every (B H) or every (D H) or every (W H)' produces 'Either every (A H) or every (J H) or every (T H)' as mentioned. Example where the categoricals share in one of the two ends of the conclusion and the categoricals are minor premise: we say 'Every (A B)' and 'Every (A D)' and 'Every (A T)' and 'Either every (B J) or every (D J) or every (T L)' produces 'Either every (A J) or every (D J) or every (T L)' as mentioned. Example where the categoricals share in one of the two ends only and the categoricals are major premise: we say 'Either every (A B) or every (J D) or every (T J)', and 'Every (B H)' and 'Every (D H)' and 'Every (J H)' produces 'Either every (A H) or every (J H) or every (T J)' as mentioned. So the parts of the conclusion share in the subject.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an advanced exposition of compound syllogisms (qiyas murakkab), specifically the mixed type that combines categorical (hamliyya) and disjunctive (munfasila) premises. The author is likely a commentator (sharh) expanding on a matn text. He begins by stating a general rule: when you have a set of categorical premises like 'كل ب ج', 'كل د ج', 'كل ه ج', they produce 'كل أ ج' through a previously mentioned proof. He then gives an example from different figures: a disjunction 'كل أ ب أو كل أ د أو لا شيء من ه أ' combined with categoricals produces a negative conclusion. Crucially, the author emphasizes that the conjunctive premise (muttasila) must be either a true universal ('موجبة حقيقية') or an exclusive disjunction ('مانعة الخلو'); otherwise, the combination of the shared part from the disjunction and the categorical premise does not guarantee truth. He refutes a view that exclusive joint denial ('مانعة الجمع') can produce a conclusion when used with exclusive disjunction, stating that is not accepted. This is a point of debate among earlier scholars.

He then turns to the second division of these mixed syllogisms, which he calls 'الذي لا يكون مقسما' (the one that is not partitioned). This division has four types (أقسام). The first type (الأول) is where the number of categorical premises equals the number of parts of the disjunction, and each categorical premise shares exactly one part of the disjunction in a productive composition. However, the categoricals themselves do not share a term other than the middle term, and the parts of the disjunction also do not share in that. Under these conditions, the syllogism produces an exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) composed of the results of all the compositions, which can be from one figure or different figures, regardless of whether the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals share the middle term. He provides four examples: (1) compositions from one figure with sharing of the middle term, (2) from one figure without sharing, (3) from different figures with sharing, (4) from different figures without sharing.

Next, the second type (القسم الثاني) produces the mentioned conclusion but with a twist: the parts of the conclusion share either in the subject or predicate depending on where the shared terms are placed. If the shared terms are in one side of the conclusion and are parts of the disjunction placed in the minor premise, or categoricals placed as minor premise, they share in the subject; if placed in the major premise, they share in the predicate. This avoids a vacuity where a part of the disjunction would be empty when combined with the categorical premise. He gives four sub-examples to illustrate: (1) disjunction as minor premise with parts sharing, (2) disjunction as major premise with parts sharing, (3) categoricals as minor premise with parts sharing, (4) categoricals as major premise with parts sharing. The passage concludes by noting that the parts of the conclusion share in the subject (i.e., the subject is common). Overall, this is a highly technical discussion of how to form valid conclusions from mixed premises, important for advanced logic in the Islamic tradition, particularly for constructing demonstrations in theology (kalam) and jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh).

The Syllogism

The Four Types of Mixed Syllogisms
Author enumerates and exemplifies four types of non-partitioned mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of mixed syllogisms based on the number of categorical premises, sharing of terms, and arrangement of disjunction and categoricals, and verify the validity conditions for each type.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms with equal categoricals and no extra term sharing produce exclusive disjunctions; the second type shares terms in the conclusion's subject or predicate.

The author distinguishes four detailed types of mixed syllogisms that are not partitioned (i.e., where the categoricals correspond to disjunct parts). The first type requires equal numbers and no extra sharing, yielding an exclusive disjunction. The second type produces a conclusion where parts share subject or predicate depending on the position of the shared terms. This lesson works through the conditions and examples for each type.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second division context
The author calls this 'الذي لا يكون مقسما' – the non-partitioned division of mixed syllogisms. It has four types.
This follows after the earlier condition discussion.
2
Identify type 1 conditions
Type 1: number of categoricals equals number of disjunct parts, each categorical shares one disjunct, categoricals share no extra term, disjunct parts share no extra term. It produces an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw).
The composition can be from one figure or different figures, with or without sharing the middle term.
3
Analyze type 1 examples
Four sub-examples are given: (a) one figure with sharing, (b) one figure without sharing, (c) different figures with sharing, (d) different figures without sharing. Compare the patterns.
Notice how each example fits the condition.
4
Identify type 2 conditions
Type 2: produces the 'mentioned conclusion' (i.e., an exclusive disjunction) but the parts share in the subject or predicate depending on whether the shared terms are in the minor or major premise. This avoids vacuity.
The sharing can be from the disjunction parts or categoricals, placed as minor or major premise.
5
Analyze type 2 examples
Four sub-examples: (a) disjunction as minor premise with parts sharing, (b) disjunction as major premise with parts sharing, (c) categoricals as minor premise with parts sharing, (d) categoricals as major premise with parts sharing.
Track how the conclusion's subject or predicate becomes shared.

And every 'B J' and every 'D J' and every 'H J' produces every 'A J' by the proof mentioned earlier. An example from different figures: we say 'Every (A B) or every (A D) or no (H A)', and 'No (B J)' and 'No (J D)' and 'Every J (H)' produces 'No (A J)' from one of the three figures which the syllogism contains, according to the proof mentioned. Commentary
This passage is an advanced exposition of compound syllogisms (qiyas murakkab), specifically the mixed type that combines categorical (hamliyya) and disjunctive (munfasila) premises. The author is likely a commentator (sharh) expanding on a matn text. He begins by stating a general rule: when you have a set of categorical premises like 'كل ب ج', 'كل د ج', 'كل ه ج', they produce 'كل أ ج' through a previously mentioned proof. He then gives an example from different figures: a disjunction 'كل أ ب أو كل أ د أو لا شيء من ه أ' combined with categoricals produces a negative conclusion. Crucially, the author emphasizes that the conjunctive premise (muttasila) must be either a true universal ('موجبة حقيقية') or an exclusive disjunction ('مانعة الخلو'); otherwise, the combination of the shared part from the disjunction and the categorical premise does not guarantee truth. He refutes a view that exclusive joint denial ('مانعة الجمع') can produce a conclusion when used with exclusive disjunction, stating that is not accepted. This is a point of debate among earlier scholars.

He then turns to the second division of these mixed syllogisms, which he calls 'الذي لا يكون مقسما' (the one that is not partitioned). This division has four types (أقسام). The first type (الأول) is where the number of categorical premises equals the number of parts of the disjunction, and each categorical premise shares exactly one part of the disjunction in a productive composition. However, the categoricals themselves do not share a term other than the middle term, and the parts of the disjunction also do not share in that. Under these conditions, the syllogism produces an exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) composed of the results of all the compositions, which can be from one figure or different figures, regardless of whether the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals share the middle term. He provides four examples: (1) compositions from one figure with sharing of the middle term, (2) from one figure without sharing, (3) from different figures with sharing, (4) from different figures without sharing.

Next, the second type (القسم الثاني) produces the mentioned conclusion but with a twist: the parts of the conclusion share either in the subject or predicate depending on where the shared terms are placed. If the shared terms are in one side of the conclusion and are parts of the disjunction placed in the minor premise, or categoricals placed as minor premise, they share in the subject; if placed in the major premise, they share in the predicate. This avoids a vacuity where a part of the disjunction would be empty when combined with the categorical premise. He gives four sub-examples to illustrate: (1) disjunction as minor premise with parts sharing, (2) disjunction as major premise with parts sharing, (3) categoricals as minor premise with parts sharing, (4) categoricals as major premise with parts sharing. The passage concludes by noting that the parts of the conclusion share in the subject (i.e., the subject is common). The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Compound Syllogisms with Shared and Unshared Middle Terms
Author presents two examples of compound syllogisms, one with shared middle term and one without.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between compound syllogisms with shared and unshared middle terms and derive their compound conclusions.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism joins disjunctive and categorical premises; its conclusion mirrors the disjunction's structure.

This passage presents two examples of compound syllogisms – one where the middle term is shared across the disjunctive premise and categorical minors, and one where no such sharing occurs. Understanding this distinction helps you trace how each disjunct combines with the categorical premises to yield a final disjunctive conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
In each example, find the statement beginning with 'إما' (either). It lists three mutually exclusive possibilities. In the first example: 'إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «ج ب» أو كل ب ط' (either every A is B or every C is B or every B is C). The second example uses terms A, B, C, D, T.
The disjunction type is exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw).
2
Identify the categorical premises
After the disjunction, find the premises starting with 'وكل' (and every) or 'ولا شيء' (and nothing). In the first example: 'وكل به ولا شيء من ط (ب) وكل ب ج' (and every B is H, and nothing of T is B, and every B is C). These are the minor premises that will be combined with each disjunct.
Some premises may be redundant or used with only some disjuncts – the author pairs them mentally.
3
Check for shared middle terms
Look at the terms that appear both in the disjunctive premise and the categorical premises. In the first example, 'B' appears in all three disjuncts and in every categorical premise – this is 'ishtirak' (sharing). In the second example, the disjuncts use B, D, and T, while the categoricals use B, D, and C – no single term is shared across all parts.
Sharing of a middle term can simplify the derivation, but both forms yield a valid compound conclusion.
4
Derive the conclusion
For each disjunct, combine it with the categorical premises as if forming a separate syllogism. The conclusion is a disjunction of the results from each disjunct. In the first example, the conclusion is 'either every A is H or nothing of C is T or some T is C' – each part comes from one disjunct combined with the minors.
The derivation assumes the categorical premises apply to all disjuncts; the 'as before' refers to standard rules of categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
