Second Division: Sharing Subject or Predicate in Compound Syllogisms
Author explains the second division of compound syllogisms where parts share subject or predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the parts of a compound disjunctive syllogism share the subject or predicate in the conclusion based on the position of the common term.

Core Idea
The shared term determines whether the conclusion's parts are united in subject or predicate.

This passage introduces the second division of compound disjunctive syllogisms, where the disjunctive parts and categorical premises share a term in one extreme of the conclusion. The author gives four examples to show how the position of the shared term (minor or major premise) dictates whether the conclusion's parts share a common subject or a common predicate. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing complex syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common term
Look for the term that appears in both the disjunctive parts and one of the categorical premises. This term will be either the subject or predicate of the conclusion's parts.
2
Determine the position of the common term
Check whether the common term comes from the minor premise (الصغرى) or the major premise (الكبرى). If it comes from the minor premise, the sharing will be in the subject of the conclusion. If from the major premise, the sharing will be in the predicate.
The minor premise is the one containing the subject of the conclusion; the major premise contains the predicate.
3
Analyze the conclusion's structure
Examine the conclusion: if the common term appears as the subject in all parts, then the parts share the subject. If it appears as the predicate in all parts, then they share the predicate.
4
Verify with the given examples
Map the first two examples: when the disjunction is minor and shares in subject, the conclusion's parts all have 'every A is ...'. When the disjunction is major and shares in predicate, the conclusion's parts all have '... is H'.
The third and fourth examples follow the same logic with categoricals as minor or major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] القسم الثاني ينتج النتيجة المذكورة لكن تشترك أجزاؤها في الموضوع إن كانت المشتركة في أحد طرفي النتيجة سواء كانت أجزاء الانفصال أو حمليات موضوعة مكان الصغرى، وفي المحمول إن كانت موضوعة مكان الكبرى لامتناع الخلو الواقع عن أحد أجزاء المنفصلة مع ما يشاركه من الحملية؛ مثال ما تشترك أجزاء المنفصلة فقط في أحد الطرفين والمنفصلة صغرى قولنا إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «أد» أو كل «أج»، وكل ب هـ» وکل از ط» وكل (ج) ح» ينتج إما أن يكون كل «أه» أو كل «أط» أو كل «أج» لما مرَّ . مثال ما تشترك أجزاء المنفصلة فقط والمنفصلة كبرى قولنا كل «أب» وكل (ج) د وكل ط ( وإما أن يكون كل ب هه أو كل دهه أو كل زه» ينتج إما أن يكون كل « أ هـ) أو كل ( هـ ) أوكل « ز » لما مر. مثال ما تشترك الحمليات في أحد طرفي النتيجة والحمليّات صغرى قولنا كل أب وكل «أد» وكل «أط» وإما أن يكون كل ب ج ) أو كل «دج» أو كل ط ل ينتج إما كل أج» أو كل دج» أو كل ط ل » لما مرَّ . مثال ما تشترك الحمليات في أحد طرفي النتيجة فقط والحمليّات كبرى إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل ج د» أو كل طج ، وكل ب هـ) وكل دهــ) وكل ج ه ينتج إما أن يكون كل «أ هـ) أو كل ((ج) (هـ) أو كل طج» لما مرَّ . فأجزاء النتيجة مشتركة في الموضوع في المثال الأول والثالث، وفي المحمول في المثال الثاني والرابع. ولا يمكن اشتراك أجزاء الانفصال والحمليّات في الحدّ / الأوسط في هذا القسم وإلا لا [۲۱۸] يكون عدد الحمليّات مثل عدد أجزاء الانفصال، وكلامنا فيه.
[B] The second division produces the mentioned conclusion, but its parts share in the subject if the shared [term] is in one of the extremes of the conclusion, whether they are the parts of the disjunction or categorical premises placed in the minor premise, and in the predicate if they are placed in the major premise, because of the impossibility of the actual absence of one of the parts of the disjunction along with its partner from the categorical. Example of only the disjunction parts sharing in one extreme and the disjunction is minor: our statement either every A is B or every A is D or every A is C, and every B is H and every D is T and every (C) is K produces either every A is H or every A is T or every A is C as before. Example of only the disjunction parts sharing and the disjunction is major: our statement every A is B and every (C) is D and every T is (?) and either every B is H or every D is H or every T is Z produces either every A is H or every (C) is H or every T is Z as before. Example of only the categoricals sharing in one extreme and the categoricals are minor: our statement every A is B and every A is D and every A is T, and either every B is C or every D is C or every T is L produces either every A is C or every D is C or every T is L as before. Example of only the categoricals sharing in one extreme and the categoricals are major: either every A is B or every C is D or every T is C, and every B is H and every D is H and every C is H produces either every A is H or every (C) is H or every T is C as before. So the parts of the conclusion share in the subject in the first and third examples, and in the predicate in the second and fourth examples. It is not possible for the parts of the disjunction and the categoricals to share in the middle term in this division, otherwise the number of categoricals would not equal the number of disjunction parts, and our discussion is about that.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines the second division of compound syllogisms where the disjunctive parts and the categorical premises share either the subject or the predicate of the conclusion. The author explains that when the shared term is in one extreme of the conclusion, the sharing occurs in the subject if the shared term is from the minor premise, and in the predicate if from the major. Four examples are given to illustrate the possible arrangements: (1) disjunction as minor with parts sharing in subject, (2) disjunction as major with parts sharing in subject, (3) categoricals as minor with parts sharing in predicate, (4) categoricals as major with parts sharing in predicate. The author notes that in this division, the disjunction parts and categoricals cannot share a middle term, because then the number of categoricals would not match the number of disjunction parts (a condition assumed for this division). Each example shows how the conclusion's parts are determined by the shared term.

Third Division: Parts Share Subject and Predicate
Author describes the third division with two sub-examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third division of compound syllogisms and distinguish its two sub-types based on how the conclusion's terms are shared.

Core Idea
In the third division, all possible conclusions are about the same subject-predicate pair.

The third division of compound syllogisms is more specific: the conclusion always shares both subject and predicate across its disjunctive possibilities. This passage from the matn provides two sub-examples that illustrate how the compositions (disjunctions and categoricals) can vary while keeping the conclusion's extremes fixed. Understanding this division helps you recognise when a complex argument converges on a single pair of terms, even with different quantities or qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common subject and predicate
In both examples, the conclusion is always about the same two terms: in the first, A and H; in the second, A and C. Note that the subject and predicate are fixed regardless of the quantity or quality of the conclusion.
This is the defining feature of the third division.
2
Separate the two sub-examples
The first sub-example has all compositions entirely different, yet the conclusion is about A and H with three possibilities (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative). The second sub-example has some compositions sharing terms, but the conclusion is only about A and C with two possibilities.
The second sub-example shows partial sharing among the compositions.
3
Map the variables
Use the logical_notation_panel to map each Arabic letter to its role. The letters أ, ب, ج, ه, ط, ز are placeholders for terms in Islamic sciences. This abstraction allows the syllogism to be applied to any subject matter.
Concrete examples help solidify understanding.
4
Analyze the logical form
Recognize that the disjunction (e.g., 'either every A is B or every A is C') combines with categorical premises to produce the conclusion(s). The reasoning_form block below formalizes this as a compound syllogism.
Compound means multiple steps, but here the outcome is a set of possible conclusions.

[T] The third division also produces the mentioned conclusion as before; and the parts of the conclusion are always shared in both subject and predicate together. Example where the compositions are entirely different in the conclusion: our statement either every A is B or every A is C or every T is A, and every B is H and nothing of H is Z and every T is H produces either every A is H or nothing of A is H or some A is H as before. Example where some compositions share some in the conclusion but not others: our statement either every A is B or every A is H, and every B is C and every D is C and nothing of H is C produces either every A is C or nothing of A is C as before.

Commentary
This passage presents the third division of compound syllogisms, where the conclusion's parts share both subject and predicate. The author gives two sub-examples. In the first, all the compositions (i.e., the disjunction parts and categoricals) are different, yet the conclusion is always about A and H, with varying quantity (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative). In the second, some compositions share a term in the conclusion while others do not, but still the conclusion revolves around A and C. The key point is that the sharing is in both extremes (subject and predicate), so the conclusion is always a categorical proposition about the same pair of terms, differing only in quantity/quality.

The Fourth Division: More Categoricals than Disjunction Parts
Author discusses the fourth division where categoricals exceed disjunction parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sub-cases of the fourth division of compound syllogisms and determine how many distinct syllogisms arise when one disjunction part pairs with multiple categorical premises.

Core Idea
When categoricals outnumber disjunction parts, each disjunction part may spawn multiple syllogisms with different conclusions.

The author now introduces the fourth division of compound syllogisms, where the number of categorical premises exceeds the number of disjunction parts. This division splits into sub-types based on whether the excess categoricals share a term with the disjunction parts. The first sub-type, detailed here, treats the case where the excess categoricals do not share any term with the disjunction parts, forcing one disjunction part to pair with two categoricals. This creates multiple distinct syllogisms, each yielding a different conclusion. Understanding this division equips you to handle complex argument structures with multiple categorical premises interacting with a single disjunction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the excess categoricals
Determine which categorical premises are in excess of the number of disjunction parts. In the first sub-type, the excess categoricals do not share any term with the disjunction parts.
2
Pair the disjunction part with each categorical
Recognize that one disjunction part now shares a term with two (or more) categoricals. This creates separate syllogisms for each pairing, as well as a syllogism that combines both categoricals.
The author uses the example: disjunction 'either every A is B or every A is H', with categoricals 'every B is C' and 'nothing of C is T'.
3
Distinguish the syllogisms
Each pairing yields a different syllogism. The syllogism using the first categorical is distinct from the one using the second, and both together form a third. These syllogisms produce different conclusions, which may contradict each other.
The text breaks off before stating the conclusions, but the principle is that the number of valid syllogisms multiplies with the number of categoricals.
4
Apply the schematic example
Use the author's abstract variables: A, B, C, H, T. The disjunction is 'كل أ ب أو كل أ هـ' (either every A is B or every A is H). The categoricals are 'كل ب ج' (every B is C) and 'لا شيء من ج ط' (nothing of C is T). Trace the three syllogisms: (1) pairing every A is B with every B is C; (2) pairing every A is B with nothing of C is T; (3) pairing with both. Note the different conclusions that would follow.
The actual conclusions are not provided due to textual break; but the structure is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ث] والقسم الرابع على قسمين؛ أحدهما أن يكون عدد الحمليات أكثر من عدد أجزاء الانفصال؛ فالزائدة إن لم يشارك شيئًا من أجزاء الانفصال فالجزء الواحد من أجزاء الانفصال يكون مشاركًا لحمليتين. والقياس باعتبار المشاركة لإحدى الحمليتين غير القياس باعتبار المشاركة الحملية الأخرى، وغير القياس باعتبار الحمليتين جميعًا؛ فينتج نتيجة أخرى على خلاف الأوليين؛ إذ هما من الأقسام المتقدمة فيقال مثلا دائما إما كل «أب» أو كل «أهـ» أو كل «ب» «ج» ولا شيء من ج ط كان القياس باعتبار مشاركة كل «أب» لكل ب ج غير القياس باعتبار مشاركة كل «أب» لقولنا لا شيء من ب هـ جميعا . وكل واحد من هذه الأقيسة الثلاثة ينتج نتيجة مخالفة لما ينتجه القياس الآخر إما باعتبار مشاركة كل «أب» لكل ب ج فينتج إما كل (۱) النسخة - ينتج إما كل «أهـ»، صح هامش.
[Th] The fourth division is of two types: one that the number of categoricals is more than the number of disjunction parts; the excess, if it does not share anything with the disjunction parts, then one part of the disjunction parts ends up sharing with two categoricals. The syllogism considering the sharing with one categorical is different from the syllogism considering the sharing with the other categorical, and different from the syllogism considering both categoricals together; so it produces another conclusion different from the first two, since they are from the previous divisions. For example, always either every A is B or every A is H or every B is C and nothing of C is T. The syllogism considering the sharing of every A is B with every B is C is different from the syllogism considering the sharing of every A is B with our statement nothing of B is H together. And each of these three syllogisms produces a conclusion different from what the other syllogism produces. For example, considering the sharing of every A is B with every B is C produces either every ... (1) footnote: the copy says 'produces either every A is H', corrected in margin.

This passage introduces the fourth division of compound syllogisms, where there are more categorical premises than disjunction parts. This creates multiple possible syllogisms depending on which categorical is used with the disjunction part, each yielding a different conclusion. The author gives a schematic example but the text breaks off at the point of stating the conclusion. The footnote indicates a correction in the manuscript. The key insight is that the number of valid conclusions multiplies with the number of categoricals, and these conclusions are contradictory or distinct as per the previous divisions.

Extra Categoricals Create Multiple Syllogisms
Analyzing the case of more categoricals than disjuncts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the multiple syllogisms formed when categorical premises exceed disjuncts in a mixed disjunctive syllogism and derive the corresponding conclusions.

Core Idea
Extra categoricals breed multiple syllogisms, each with its own conclusion.

This passage explores a nuanced case in mixed syllogisms: when you have more categorical premises than disjuncts. The author shows that the extra categorical creates multiple participation relationships, each yielding a distinct syllogism and conclusion. Understanding this helps you see how redundant premises can be systematically exploited to derive multiple valid inferences.

Guided Path
1
Count the premises
Identify the number of categorical premises (ḥamliyyāt) and the number of disjuncts in the disjunctive major premise. In the example, the disjunction 'either every A is B or every A is H' has two disjuncts, and there are two categoricals: 'every B is J' and 'no J is T'. So categoricals equal disjuncts? Actually the text says categoricals outnumber disjuncts; here one categorical is extra relative to the disjuncts? Note that the two categoricals correspond to the two disjuncts? We'll follow the author's claim that the extra categorical causes one disjunct to pair with two categoricals.
The example may be simplified for illustration.
2
Identify participation pairs
Determine which categorical premises share a middle term with each disjunct. The disjunct 'every A is B' shares the term B with the categorical 'every B is J'. Also, it shares B with the categorical 'no J is T'? Actually 'no J is T' does not share directly with 'every A is B' unless via B? The text says 'the syllogism by virtue of participation of every A is B with our statement 'no B is H'' – there is a misprint? The example seems to have a third categorical? The key is that the extra categorical leads to one disjunct being paired with two categoricals, forming two distinct syllogisms plus a third combining both.
Focus on the principle: multiple pairings yield multiple syllogisms.
3
Form the syllogisms
Construct each syllogism using one participation pair. For example: (1) disjunct 'every A is B' + categorical 'every B is J' gives a categorical syllogism concluding 'every A is J'. (2) disjunct 'every A is B' + categorical 'no B is H' (if that were present) gives 'no A is H'. (3) Combining both categoricals with the same disjunct yields a compound conclusion. The author lists three syllogisms from the example.
Each syllogism produces a different disjunctive conclusion.
4
Derive the conclusions
For each syllogism, derive the conclusion. In the example: using 'every A is B' and 'every B is J' yields 'either every A is J or no A is T'? Actually the author says 'either every A is J or no A is T'. The second syllogism yields 'either no A is H or no A is T'. The third yields 'either (every A is J and no A is H) or no A is T'. Notice how the extra premise expands the possibilities.
The disjunctive form of the conclusion reflects the original disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الرابع على قسمين؛ أحدهما أن يكون عدد الحمليات أكثر من عدد أجزاء الانفصال؛ فالزائدة إن لم يشارك شيئًا من أجزاء الانفصال فالجزء الواحد من أجزاء الانفصال يكون مشاركًا لحمليتين. والقياس باعتبار المشاركة لإحدى الحمليتين غير القياس باعتبار المشاركة الحملية الأخرى، وغير القياس باعتبار الحمليتين جميعًا؛ فينتج نتيجة أخرى على خلاف الأوليين؛ إذ هما من الأقسام المتقدمة. فيقال مثلا: دائما إما كل «أب» أو كل «أهـ» أو كل «بج» ولا شيء من «ج ط». كان القياس باعتبار مشاركة كل «أب» لكل «بج» غير القياس باعتبار مشاركة كل «أب» لقولنا لا شيء من «ب هـ» جميعا. وكل واحد من هذه الأقيسة الثلاثة ينتج نتيجة مخالفة لما ينتجه القياس الآخر: إما باعتبار مشاركة كل «أب» لكل «بج» فينتج إما كل «أج» أو لا شيء من «أط». وباعتبار مشاركة لقولنا لا شيء من «ب هـ» ينتج إما لا شيء من «أهـ» أو لا شيء من «أط». وأما باعتبار مشاركة لكل «بج» ولا شيء من «ب هـ» جميعا ينتج إما كل «أج» ولا شيء من «أهـ» وإما لا شيء من «أط» لما مر.
And the fourth division is divided into two parts. The first is that the number of categoricals is greater than the number of disjuncts. So the extra (categorical), if it does not share anything with the disjuncts, then the one part of the disjuncts will share with two categoricals. And the syllogism by virtue of participation with one of the two categoricals is different from the syllogism by virtue of participation with the other categorical, and different from the syllogism by virtue of both categoricals together. So it produces another result contradictory to the first two, as they are from the previous divisions. For example, always: either every A is B or every A is H, or every B is J and no J is T. The syllogism by virtue of participation of every A is B with every B is J is different from the syllogism by virtue of participation of every A is B with our statement 'no B is H' together. And each one of these three syllogisms produces a result different from what the other syllogism produces: either by virtue of participation of every A is B with every B is J, it produces either every A is J or no A is T. And by virtue of participation with our statement 'no B is H', it produces either no A is H or no A is T. And by virtue of participation with every B is J and no B is H together, it produces either every A is J and no A is H, or no A is T, as previously.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage begins the exposition of the fourth division of mixed syllogisms, which is itself divided into two cases. The first case is when the number of categorical premises exceeds the number of disjuncts in the disjunctive major premise. The author explains that the extra categorical, if it does not share any term with the disjuncts, will cause each disjunct to be associated with two categoricals. Consequently, different combinations of these categoricals yield different syllogisms, each producing a different conclusion. An example is given with three premises: the disjunction 'either every A is B or every A is H' and two categoricals 'every B is J' and 'no J is T'. The author shows three distinct syllogisms based on different participation relationships: (1) using 'every A is B' with 'every B is J', (2) using 'every A is B' with 'no B is H', and (3) using both categoricals together. Each yields a different disjunctive conclusion. The passage underscores that redundant categoricals generate multiple valid syllogisms within this framework.

Key Terms
الحمليات (al-ḥamliyyāt)
أجزاء الانفصال (ajzā' al-infisāl)
المشاركة (al-mushārakah)

C) The conclusion is a single categorical
D) The extra categorical is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
When categorical premises outnumber disjuncts, multiple valid syllogisms arise, each with its own distinct conclusion.

And no harmful transaction is permissible.' Identify the three syllogisms and their conclusions based on the participation of the categoricals with the disjunct 'every transaction is permissible'.

Valid Mixed Syllogisms with Shared Middle Term
Examines the valid subcase of mixed syllogisms with one categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid mixed syllogism where a single categorical premise participates with both disjuncts sharing the middle term.

Core Idea
One categorical can combine with a disjunction to yield a valid conclusion if the middle term is shared.

In this passage, the author presents the first subcase of mixed syllogisms where categoricals are fewer than disjuncts. The key condition for validity is that the categorical participates with both parts of the disjunction in a productive manner, and the two disjuncts share the middle term. This yields an affirmative exclusive disjunction. Understanding this pattern helps you identify valid inference forms in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the disjunction (e.g., 'إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو كل ج ب'). Note that it is a 'مانعة الخلو' (exclusive of emptiness, i.e., at least one true).
2
Identify the single categorical premise
Find the categorical that shares a term with both disjuncts (e.g., 'كل ب د'). It should be affirmative and universal.
3
Check the middle term
Verify that the term shared between the disjuncts (e.g., 'ب') is the same as the subject or predicate of the categorical. In this case, the categorical's subject is 'ب', which is the middle term linking 'أ' and 'ج' in the disjuncts.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a disjunction combining the results: 'إما كل أ د أو كل ج د'. It is also a 'مانعة الخلو' (affirmative exclusive).
5
Test with an Islamic example
Apply the form to a real fiqh scenario: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden or every gharar is forbidden. Every forbidden act is invalid. Therefore, every intoxicant is invalid or every gharar is invalid.'
This form is valid only when the categorical is affirmative and universal, and the middle term is shared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني من القسمين هو أن يكون عدد الحمليات أقل من عدد أجزاء الانفصال كما في قولنا: إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «ج ب»، وكل «ب د» ينتج إما كل «أد» أو كل «ج د»، هذا إذا كانت الحملية مشاركة لكل واحد من جزأي المنفصلة مشاركة منتجة موجبة مانعة الخلو؛ وجزءا المنفصلة يشتركان في حد الأوسط. وأما إذا لم يكن كذلك كما في قولنا: إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «أد»، ولا شيء من «أب د» ينتج إما لا شيء من «أد» أو لا شيء من «أب»، هذا إذا كانا يشتركان في غير الأوسط. فأما إذا لم يشتركا في حد أصلا فذلك مثل قولنا: إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «ج ب»، ولا شيء من «أب د» ينتج إما لا شيء من «أب» أو لا شيء من «ج ب». والثاني من هذا القسم ما تشارك الحملية أحد جزئي المنفصلة فقط، وإنه ينتج موجبة مانعة الخلو أيضًا كما في قولنا: إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «أج» أو كل «ج د» ينتج إما كل «أب» أو كل «أد» لما مر، هذا إذا كان الاشتراك متحققا وإن لم يكن في الأوسط. فأما إذا لم يكن متحققا أصلا فذلك مثل قولنا: إما كل «أب» أو كل «ج د» أو كل «د هـ» ينتج إما كل «أب» أو كل «ج هـ»، فهذه كلها من جملة ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين على اعتقاد أنها منتجة؛ والأمر على خلافه في البعض فإن الصغرى الحملية إذا كانت واحدة مع الكبرى المنفصلة فإنها منتجة. والشيخ صرّح في الشفاء أنها غير منتجة؛ فلو قلتَ مثلا: كل «ج ب» وإما أن يكون لا شيء من «ب أ» أو لا شيء من «ب هـ» ينتج إما لا شيء من «ج أ» أو لا شيء من «ج هـ».
And the second of the two divisions is that the number of categoricals is less than the number of disjuncts, as in our statement: either every A is B or every J is B, and every B is D, yields either every A is D or every J is D. This is when the categorical participates with each of the two parts of the disjunction in a productive participation, an affirmative exclusive of emptiness; and the two parts of the disjunction share in the middle term. And if it is not so, as in our statement: either every A is B or every A is D, and no A is B D, yields either no A is D or no A is B. This is when they share in something other than the middle term. And if they do not share in any term at all, then that is like our statement: either every A is B or every J is B, and no A is B D, yields either no A is B or no J is B. And the second of this division is when the categorical participates with only one of the two disjuncts, and it produces an affirmative exclusive of emptiness as well, as in our statement: either every A is B or every A is J or every J is D, yields either every A is B or every A is D, as previously. This is when the participation is realized even if not in the middle term. And if it is not realized at all, then that is like our statement: either every A is B or every J is D or every D is H, yields either every A is B or every J is H. All of these are among what a group of earlier scholars adopted, believing them to be productive; but the matter is contrary in some of them, for the minor categorical if it is alone with the disjunctive major, it is productive. And the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) stated explicitly in al-Shifa' that it is not productive. So if you say, for example: every J is B and either no B is A or no B is H, yields either no J is A or no J is H.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage continues the fourth division by examining the second case: categoricals are fewer than disjuncts. The author presents several subcases. First, when one categorical shares a productive participation (affirmative exclusive of emptiness) with both disjuncts, and the disjuncts share the middle term, the conclusion is a disjunction of two affirmatives. Second, when the participation is not productive (e.g., using a negative premise), and the disjuncts share something other than the middle term, the conclusion is a disjunction of two negatives. Third, when the disjuncts share no term at all, the conclusion also yields negatives. The author then discusses a further subdivision where the categorical participates with only one disjunct, which also yields an affirmative exclusive of emptiness, provided participation is realized (even if not in the middle term). If participation is not realized, the conclusion is a simple disjunction of two propositions. Finally, the author notes that earlier scholars considered these syllogisms productive, but Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) in al-Shifa' explicitly stated they are not productive, and provides an example where a single categorical with a disjunctive major is not productive. This critical note highlights the refinement of the theory.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It must be particular
D) It must have the same subject as the disjuncts

Key Takeaway
A valid mixed syllogism with one categorical requires that the categorical be affirmative and share the middle term with both disjuncts.

Construct a valid mixed syllogism following the pattern: take a disjunction of two universal affirmatives (مانعة الخلو) about the prohibition of two different acts (e.g., riba and gharar), and a categorical premise saying 'Every prohibited act is invalid'. Write the conclusion.

Ibn Sina's Refutation of Non-Productive Mixed Syllogisms
Critiques invalid mixed syllogisms using Ibn Sina's authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid forms of mixed syllogisms based on Ibn Sina's critique in al-Shifa'.

Core Idea
Not every combination of a categorical and a disjunction yields a valid conclusion.

The passage contrasts earlier scholars who accepted many mixed syllogisms as productive with Ibn Sina's explicit rejection in al-Shifa'. He provides counterexamples where a single categorical with a disjunction does not produce a valid conclusion. Understanding which forms are invalid protects you from faulty reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ibn Sina's claim
Find the sentence: 'والشيخ صرّح في الشفاء أنها غير منتجة' (The Shaykh stated in al-Shifa' that they are not productive).
2
Identify the rejected form
The example given: 'كل ج ب وإما أن يكون لا شيء من ب أ أو لا شيء من ب هـ ينتج إما لا شيء من ج أ أو لا شيء من ج هـ'. This is a categorical 'every J is B' with a disjunction of two negatives 'either no B is A or no B is H', concluding a disjunction of two negatives.
3
Analyze why it fails
The categorical does not properly link the disjunction, or the premises are negative. According to Ibn Sina, such mixing is not productive.
4
Compare with earlier scholars
The author notes that earlier scholars believed these forms were productive, but Ibn Sina corrected this.
5
Apply the critique
Test with a fiqh example: 'Every contract is valid and either no valid contract is void or no valid contract is uncertain' does not yield a valid conclusion.
The invalidity stems from the categorical not sharing the middle term in a 'productive' way, or from using negative premises.

The Fallacy of Mixing a Single Categorical with a Disjunctive Major
Author categorizes productive and non-productive combinations and refutes a fallacious proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the early logicians' proof that a single categorical premise with a disjunctive major yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
A single categorical premise cannot guarantee which disjunct is true; truth of an unspecified part does not imply truth of a specific part.

This dense passage from Mantiq Kabir explores the conditions under which combining a categorical premise with a disjunctive premise yields a valid conclusion. The author categorizes productive and non-productive cases, then singles out a flawed proof offered by earlier scholars. Understanding the fallacy protects you from invalid compound syllogisms in legal reasoning (fiqh) and theological arguments (kalam).

Guided Path
1
State the claim being refuted
The early scholars claimed that a single categorical premise (e.g., 'Every C is B') together with a disjunctive major (e.g., 'Either no B is A or no B is E') yields a valid disjunctive conclusion (e.g., 'Either no C is A or no C is E').
Locate this claim in the Arabic: 'فلو قلتَ مثلا كل ج ب وإما أن يكون لا شيء من ب أ أو لا شيء من هب ينتج...'. The pronoun 'ج' is the subject of the categorical.
2
Understand the attempted proof
The early scholars argued that because the categorical premise shares truth with one of the disjuncts (the categorical 'Every C is B' shares the term B with the disjuncts), one of the disjuncts must be true together with the categorical. They assumed this yields a specific disjunct.
The proof is: 'صدق واحد من أجزاء الانفصال مع ما يشاركه من الحملية' i.e., the truth of one disjunct along with what it shares with the categorical.
3
Identify the logical flaw
The author points out that if a thing shares truth with an unspecified part (i.e., it is true that one of the disjuncts is true, but not which one), it does not follow that it shares truth with any specific part. The disjuncts are unspecified; only the disjunction as a whole is known to be true.
The key phrase: 'فإن الشيء إذا كان مشاركا في الصدق لشيء غير معين فلا يلزم منه أن يكون مشاركًا لشيء معين'. This is the core of the critique.
4
Contrast with valid mixtures
Valid mixtures occur when the categorical premise combines separately with each disjunct in a full syllogism, producing two distinct conclusions that form a disjunction. For example, 'Either every A is B or every C is B' and 'Every B is D' yields 'Either every A is D or every C is D' because 'Every B is D' combines validly with each disjunct.
The valid case requires the categorical to participate with both disjuncts in a productive way, often sharing the middle term (حد الأوسط).
5
Apply Ibn Sina's authority
The author endorses Ibn Sina's verdict (in al-Shifa) that such a combination is not productive. The earlier 'proof' is dismissed as invalid.
The text says: 'والشيخ صرّح في الشفاء أنها غير منتجة... والحق أنه لا ينتج'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

«أه» وإما لا شيء من «أط» لما مر . والثاني من القسمين هو أن يكون عدد الحمليات أقل من عدد أجزاء الانفصال كما في قولنا إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل ج ب » وكل ب د ينتج إما كل أد» أو كل جد»، هذا إذا كانت الحملية مشاركة لكل واحد من جزأي المنفصلة مشاركة منتجة موجبة مانعة الخلو؛ وجزءا المنفصلة يشتركان في حد الأوسط. وأما إذا لم يكن كذلك كما في قولنا إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل «أد»، ولا شيء من اب د) ينتج إما لا شيء من «أد» أو لا شيء من «أب»، هذا إذا كانا يشتركان في غير الأوسط. فأما إذا لم يشتركا في حد أصلا فذلك مثل قولنا إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل (ج) ب ، ولا شيء من اب دا ينتج إما لا شيء من «أب» أو لا شيء من ج ب». والثاني من هذا القسم ما تشارك الحملية أحد جزئي المنفصلة فقط، وإنه ينتج موجبة مانعة الخلو أيضًا كما في قولنا إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل «أج» أو كل (ج) د ينتج إما كل «أب» أو كل «أد» لما مر ، هذا إذا كان الاشتراك متحققا وإن لم يكن في الأوسط. فأما إذا لم يكن متحققاً أصلا فذلك مثل قولنا إما كل «أب» أو كل «ج د» أو كل «د هـ» ينتج إما كل «أب» أو كل (ج) هـ ، فهذه كلها من جملة ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين على اعتقاد أنها منتجة؛ والأمر على خلافه في البعض فإن الصغرى الحملية إذا كانت واحدة مع الكبرى المنفصلة فإنها منتجة. والشيخ صرّح في الشفاء أنها غير منتجة ؛ فلو قلتَ مثلا كل «ج ب» وإما أن يكون لا شيء من ب أ) أو لا شيء من هب ينتج إما لا شيء من (ج) أ) أو لا شيء من (ج هـ) عند ذلك القوم بالبرهان الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو صدق واحد من أجزاء الانفصال مع ما يشاركه من الحملية. والحق أنه لا ينتج ؛ وهذا البرهان من جملة ما لا يبرهن به فإن الشيء إذا كان مشاركا في الصدق لشيء غير معين فلا يلزم منه أن يكون مشاركًا لشيء معين كهذا على التعيين أو لذلك كذلك. ولا يقال غير المعين لا يمكن أن يكون متحققاً إلا في ضمن المعين؛ فلو تحقق هو لتحقق المعين لا محالة؛ إذ هو بحسب التحقق من حيث هو التحقق لا بحسب غيره. ألا ترى أنه يصح أن يقال واحد من هذه الأمور لازم من لوازم ذلك الأمر مع أنه لا يصح أن يكون هذا على التعيين لازما أو ذلك فذلك، هذا إذا كانت المنفصلة مانعة الخلو. وكذلك إذا كانت مانعة الجمع لكن إنتاجها إنما يتحقق عندهم إذا كانت نتيجة التأليف مع الحملية منتجة للجزء المشارك من المنفصلة؛ وحينئذ إما أن يكون المشارك الحملية كل واحد من جزأي المنفصلة أو أحدهما فقط دون الأخرى. والأول ينتج موجبة مانعة الجمع من نتيجة التأليف من الحملية؛ والجزء المشارك لها من المنفصلة، ومن نتيجة التأليف من الحملية الأخرى؛ والجزء الآخر المشارك لها من المنفصلة إذا كان القياس مشتملا على الحمليتين كما في قولنا: والمنفصلة صغرى إما أن يكون لا شيء من «أب» أو لا شيء من ج (د) وكل ب (هـ) وكل د ط» ينتج إما أن يكون لا شيء من د» أهـ) أو لا شيء من ج ط لاستلزام لا شيء من «أهـ» للاشيء من «أب»؛ واستلزام لا شيء من ) » للاشيء من (ج) د بالقياس المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل. وأما إذا كانت المنفصلة هي الكبرى كما في قولنا كل «أب» وكل (ج) د وإما أن يكون بعض ب هـا أو بعض د ط» ينتج إما بعض أج» أو بعض ج ط لاستلزام الجزء الأول للأول من المتصلة، والثاني للثاني كذلك
The great logic: 'Every A is C' or 'No A is E'. Considering participation in our saying 'No B is D' produces either 'No A is E' or 'No A is E'. And considering participation for 'Every B is C' and 'No B is D' together produces either 'Every A is C' and 'No A is E' or 'No A is E' as before. The second of the two divisions is that the number of categorical premises is less than the number of parts of the disjunction, as in our saying 'Either every A is B or every C is B' and 'Every B is D' produces either 'Every A is D' or 'Every C is D'. This is when the categorical premise participates with each of the two parts of the disjunction in a productive positive exhaustive disjunction; and the two parts of the disjunction share the middle term. But if it is not so, as in our saying 'Either every A is B or every A is D' and 'No A is D' produces either 'No A is D' or 'No A is B'. This is when they share in something other than the middle. If they do not share at all in a term, then it is like our saying 'Either every A is B or every C is B' and 'No A is D' produces either 'No A is A' or 'No C is B'. The second of this division is when the categorical premise shares with only one of the two parts of the disjunction, and it also produces a positive exhaustive disjunction, as in our saying 'Either every A is B or every A is C or every C is D' produces either 'Every A is B' or 'Every A is D' as before. This is when the sharing is realized even if not in the middle. But if it is not realized at all, then it is like our saying 'Either every A is B or every C is D or every D is E' produces either 'Every A is B' or 'Every C is E'. All these are part of what some early scholars believed to be productive; but the matter is contrary in some cases, for the categorical minor together with the disjunctive major is productive. The Sheikh (Ibn Sina) stated in al-Shifa that it is not productive. For example, if you say 'Every C is B' and 'Either no B is A or no B is E' produces either 'No C is A' or 'No C is E' according to those people, by the proof mentioned earlier, which is the truth of one part of the disjunction along with what shares with it from the categorical. The truth is that it does not produce. And this proof is among what does not prove, because if something shares in truth with an unspecified thing, it does not necessitate that it shares with a specified thing, whether this one specifically or that one. And it cannot be said that the unspecified cannot be realized except within the specified; for if it is realized, the specified would necessarily be realized, since it is with respect to realization as such, not with respect to something else. Do you not see that it is correct to say 'One of these things is a necessary consequence of that thing' while it is not correct that this one specifically is a necessary consequence or that one? This is when the disjunction is exhaustive. Likewise, if it is exclusive, its production is only realized according to them if the result of the composition with the categorical produces the part that shares with the disjunction. Then either the categorical shares with each of the two parts of the disjunction or with only one of them without the other. The first produces a positive exclusive disjunction from the result of the composition from the categorical and the part sharing with it from the disjunction, and from the result of the composition from the other categorical and the other part sharing with it from the disjunction, if the syllogism includes both categoricals, as in our saying: the disjunctive minor 'Either no A is B or no C is D' and 'Every B is E' and 'Every D is F' produces either 'No A is E' or 'No C is F' because 'No A is E' entails 'No A is B', and 'No C is F' entails 'No C is D' by the syllogism composed of the categorical and conditional. And if the disjunction is the major, as in our saying 'Every A is B' and 'Every C is D' and 'Either some B is E or some D is F' produces either 'Some A is E' or 'Some C is F' because the first part entails the first of the conditional, and the second similarly entails the second.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion of syllogistic reasoning involving the combination of categorical (حملیة) and disjunctive (منفصلة) premises. The author examines various scenarios to determine when such combinations produce valid conclusions. The text is structured by first distinguishing cases based on whether the categorical premise shares with both parts of the disjunctive, with only one part, or with neither, and also considers the type of disjunction (exhaustive, مانعة الخلو, vs. exclusive, مانعة الجمع). The author then critiques a proof offered by earlier scholars (قوم من المتقدمين) and endorses the position of Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) that a single categorical premise with a disjunctive major does not produce a valid conclusion. The flaw in the supposed proof is that it confuses the truth of an unspecified disjunct with the truth of a specific disjunct. The passage then extends the discussion to exclusive disjunctions, showing how productive combinations require that the categorical premises each produce one of the disjuncts. The analysis uses symbolic placeholders (أ، ب، ج ، د, هـ ) as logical variables, e.g., 'Every B is A' or 'No C is D', which can be instantiated with Islamic legal examples: for instance, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (حرام)' and 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' would illustrate a categorical syllogism. The author's refutation is a key logical point: from 'Either P or Q' and 'P is true' we can infer 'P', but from 'Either P or Q' and 'Some R is true' we cannot infer which disjunct is true, because the unspecified shared truth does not guarantee a specific one. This is analogous to knowing that either wine (khamr) or nabidh is forbidden, but only knowing that some drink is forbidden does not tell us which one.

منفصلة مانعة الجمع
نتيجة التأليف
حد الأوسط
القياس المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل

B) The categorical premise must be negative, but it is affirmative.
C) The proof assumes that an unspecified truth participates in the truth of a specific disjunct, which does not logically follow.

Key Takeaway
A single categorical premise with a disjunctive major does not produce a valid conclusion because truth of an unspecified disjunct does not entail truth of a specific disjunct.

The Syllogism
advanced
7 min
The Failure of Indeterminate Truth-Sharing
Author refutes a claim that a disjunctive syllogism with a conjunctive premise yields a determinate conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why an argument that uses a disjunctive premise and a conjunctive premise fails to produce a determinate conclusion when the truth-sharing is indeterminate.

Core Idea
Indeterminate truth-sharing cannot yield a specific conclusion.

Some logicians thought that if a disjunctive premise (either A or B) is true and a conjunctive premise (all C is D) shares truth with one part, then we can specify which part. The author of our passage refutes this, arguing that the sharing is indeterminate. This lesson unpacks his critique and shows why such proofs are invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed proof
Find the structure alleged to produce a conclusion: a disjunctive premise (e.g., 'either A or B is true') combined with a conjunctive premise (e.g., 'all C are D'), where the conjunctive premise shares truth with one part of the disjunction. The claim is that we can then determine which part is true.
2
Pinpoint the indeterminacy
The author states that if something participates in truth with an indeterminate thing (i.e., we don't know which part), then it does not necessitate that it participates with a determinate specific part. This is the core logical flaw.
The participation is indeterminate because we only know that one part is true, but not which one.
3
Reject the objection
The author anticipates an objection: 'The indeterminate cannot exist except within the determinate; so if it exists, the determinate exists.' He rejects this by noting that existence is considered by itself, not by virtue of specification. The indeterminate is not a separate entity.
The fallacy is to treat the indeterminate as a distinct thing that must be realized in some determination.
4
Apply the rule
The author gives an example: it is valid to say 'one of these things is a necessary concomitant of that thing' but invalid to specify 'this specifically is a concomitant' (or that one). This illustrates that general statements do not license specific conclusions. The critique applies when the disjunctive is mani'at al-khuluw (at least one true).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عند ذلك القوم بالبرهان الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو صدق واحد من أجزاء الانفصال مع ما يشاركه من الحملية. والحق أنه لا ينتج ؛ وهذا البرهان من جملة ما لا يبرهن به فإن الشيء إذا كان مشاركا في الصدق لشيء غير معين فلا يلزم منه أن يكون مشاركًا لشيء معين كهذا على التعيين أو لذلك كذلك. ولا يقال غير المعين لا يمكن أن يكون متحققاً إلا في ضمن المعين؛ فلو تحقق هو لتحقق المعين لا محالة؛ إذ هو بحسب التحقق من حيث هو التحقق لا بحسب غيره. ألا ترى أنه يصح أن يقال واحد من هذه الأمور لازم من لوازم ذلك الأمر مع أنه لا يصح أن يكون هذا على التعيين لازما أو ذلك فذلك، هذا إذا كانت المنفصلة مانعة الخلو.
Some people rely on the proof that has been mentioned, namely that one of the parts of the disjunction is true together with what participates with it from the conjunctive premise. But the truth is that it does not produce a conclusion. This proof is among what cannot be used to prove, because if something participates in truth with an indeterminate thing, it does not necessitate that it participates with a determinate thing, such as this specifically or that similarly. And it cannot be said that the indeterminate cannot exist except within the determinate; for if it exists, then the determinate would necessarily exist, because its existence is by virtue of existence as such, not by virtue of something else. Do you not see that it is valid to say 'one of these things is a necessary concomitant of that thing' while it is not valid that this specifically is a concomitant or that specifically. This is when the disjunctive is exclusive of emptiness.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement: the author attacks a purported proof that some logicians have used. The proof claims that if a disjunctive premise (e.g., either A or B) is true and a conjunctive premise (e.g., all C is D) shares truth with one part of the disjunction, then the conclusion can specify which part is true. The author denies this. He argues that the participation in truth is indeterminate—we do not know which specific part is involved. Therefore, no determinate conclusion follows. He rejects the objection that the indeterminate must be realized within the determinate; existence is considered in itself, not via specification. He gives an example: it is valid to say 'one of these is a necessary concomitant' but not to specify which. This applies when the disjunctive is 'mani'at al-khuluw' (exclusive of emptiness, i.e., at least one disjunct is true). The author's critique is precise: indeterminate truth-sharing does not yield a specific conclusion.

المنفصلة
مانعة الخلو
البرهان

Key Takeaway
A proof that combines a disjunctive and a conjunctive premise fails to yield a specific conclusion when the truth-sharing is indeterminate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Disjunctive with conjunctive: both parts participate
First scenario: both parts of disjunction participate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid compound syllogism when a positive exclusive-of-combination disjunction is combined with a conjunctive premise that participates with both disjuncts.

Core Idea
When a conjunctive supports both parts of an exclusive disjunction, the conclusion preserves the exclusive relationship.

This lesson focuses on the first scenario described by the author: a positive mani'at al-jam' (exclusive of combination) disjunction where the conjunctive premise participates with both of its parts. The author shows how to derive a new disjunction by combining the conjunctive with each part separately and then uniting the results. Understanding this case is foundational for handling more complex compound syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
The disjunction is positive and exclusive of combination (mani'at al-jam'), meaning at most one part is true.
This is crucial because the method differs from exclusive of emptiness.
2
Confirm the conjunctive participates with both parts
Check that the conjunctive premise shares a term with each disjunct. For example, in 'Either no A is B or no C is D', the conjunctive 'Every B is E' participates with the first part via B, and 'Every D is F' with the second via D.
If only one participates, the result changes (see next lesson).
3
Construct two separate syllogisms
For each disjunct, combine it with its respective conjunctive to get an intermediate conclusion. For the first part 'no A is B' and 'Every B is E', we get 'no A is E' via a first-figure categorical syllogism. Similarly, 'no C is D' and 'Every D is F' give 'no C is F'.
These are immediate inferences, not full syllogisms, but the author calls them 'result of combination'.
4
Combine the two conclusions into a new disjunction
The final conclusion is a positive exclusive-of-combination disjunction of the two intermediate results: 'Either no A is E or no C is F'.
The author explicitly states this for the case where the disjunctive is minor.
5
Verify the method when the disjunctive is major
If the disjunctive is major, the process is analogous: combine each conjunctive with the corresponding part of the disjunction to produce a new disjunction.
The author gives an example with 'some' premises as well.

Likewise if it is exclusive of combination, but its production is only realized in their view if the result of the combination with the conjunctive premise yields the participating part of the disjunctive. Then either the shared premise participates with both parts of the disjunctive or with only one without the other. The first produces a positive exclusive of combination from the result of the combination with the conjunctive and the participating part of the disjunctive, and from the result of the combination with the other conjunctive and the other participating part—if the syllogism includes both conjunctives, as in our saying (with the disjunctive as minor): 'Either no A is B or no C is D, and every B is E, and every D is F' yields 'Either no A is E or no C is F' because 'no A is E' implies 'no A is B', and 'no [C] is F' implies 'no C is D' by the syllogism composed of conjunctive and conditional. And if the disjunctive is major, as in our saying: 'Every A is B and every C is D, and either some B is E or some D is F' yields 'Either some A is E or some C is F' because the first part implies the first of the conditional and the second part the second. As for the second case—that the shared premise participates with only one part of the disjunctive—it also produces a positive exclusive of emptiness, as in our saying (disjunctive as minor): 'Either no A is B or every C is D, and every B is E' yields 'Either no A is E or every C is D' exclusive of combination, because 'no A is B' implies 'no A is E'. And if the disjunctive is major, as in our saying: 'Every A is B, and either some B is E or every D is F' yields 'Either some A is E or every A is F' because 'some A is E' implies 'some B is E' by the syllogism composed of conjunctive and conditional. Do not think that the method for producing exclusive of combination is the same as that used for exclusive of emptiness; rather, one has one method and the other has another, known by reflection. This is when the disjunctive is positive. As for when it is negative, if it is exclusive of combination, its ruling is the ruling of exclusive of emptiness when positive, meaning that what the positive exclusive of emptiness produces with the conjunctive, the negative exclusive of combination produces with it. As for the divided syllogism whose conclusion is conjunctive, if the disjunctive is negative exclusive of combination, it produces the possibility of the truth of that conjunctive, because the truth of each of the parts of the disjunction together with the conjunctive that participates with it entails the truth of that conjunctive. So possibility entails possibility. And it produces a negative exclusive of combination from the result of the combination and the non-participating part, if the participating part is one of the disjuncts, and from the result of the other combination if it participates with the conjunctive. For the participating side entails the result of the combination by what we said about the syllogism composed of conjunctive and conditional. Then the negative disjunctive if it is exclusive of emptiness, its ruling is the ruling of exclusive of combination when positive, meaning that the conditions considered in producing the positive when it is exclusive of combination are considered when it is exclusive of emptiness. The condition is that the result of the combination with the conjunctive premise produces the participating part of...

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe: the author explains how disjunctive syllogisms (with conjunctive premises) produce conclusions for both types of disjunction. He first considers the case where the disjunctive is 'mani'at al-jam'' (exclusive of combination, i.e., at most one true) and the conjunctive premise participates with either both or only one disjunct. He gives examples: when the disjunctive is the minor premise and both conjunctives are present, the conclusion is a positive exclusive of combination. For instance, 'Either no A is B or no C is D, and every B is E, and every D is F' yields 'Either no A is E or no C is F' via compound reasoning. Similarly, when the disjunctive is major, a similar pattern holds. When the conjunctive participates with only one disjunct, the conclusion is a positive exclusive of emptiness, as in 'Either no A is B or every C is D, and every B is E' yields 'Either no A is E or every C is D' exclusive of combination. The author warns not to confuse the methods for the two types of disjunction. He then discusses negative disjunctives: a negative exclusive of combination behaves like a positive exclusive of emptiness, and vice versa. The divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam) with a negative exclusive of combination yields the possibility (jawaz) of the conjunctive conclusion. The text ends mid-sentence, cutting off the condition. The author's treatment is thorough, showing how to derive conclusions from complex mixed syllogisms using Aristotelian principles.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Disjunctive with conjunctive: only one part participates
Second scenario: only one part of disjunction participates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid compound syllogism when a positive exclusive-of-combination disjunction is combined with a conjunctive premise that participates with only one disjunct, yielding a positive exclusive-of-emptiness conclusion.

Core Idea
When a conjunctive supports only one part of an exclusive disjunction, the conclusion becomes exclusive of emptiness (at least one true).

The author now turns to the second scenario: the conjunctive premise participates with only one of the two parts of the disjunction. In this case, the conclusion is a positive mani'at al-khuluw (exclusive of emptiness), meaning at least one of the resulting disjuncts must be true. The author illustrates with examples for both positions of the disjunctive (minor and major). Understanding this contrast with the first scenario is critical for correctly analyzing mixed syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the participation pattern
Here, the conjunctive premise shares a term with only one disjunct. For example, in 'Either no A is B or every C is D', the conjunctive 'Every B is E' participates only with the first part via B, while the second part has no matching conjunctive.
The second part remains unchanged in the conclusion.
2
Apply the syllogistic method to the participating part
Combine the participating disjunct with the conjunctive to derive an intermediate conclusion. For 'no A is B' and 'Every B is E', we get 'no A is E'.
This step is the same as in the first scenario, but now it applies only to one part.
3
Form the new disjunction
The final conclusion is a disjunction of the intermediate result and the unchanged other part: 'Either no A is E or every C is D'. The author specifies that this is a positive exclusive-of-emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) disjunction.
Exclusive of emptiness means at least one part is true, possibly both. This contrasts with exclusive of combination where at most one is true.
4
Verify with disjunctive as major example
When the disjunctive is major, e.g., 'Every A is B, and either some B is E or every D is F' (here the conjunctive 'Every A is B' participates only with the first disjunct), the conclusion is 'Either some A is E or every A is F'.
The second part of the disjunction ('every D is F') is transformed through the conjunctive into 'every A is F' via a categorical syllogism, because the conjunctive provides the link.
5
Contrast the method with the first scenario
The author warns: 'Do not think that the method for producing exclusive of combination is the same as that used for exclusive of emptiness.' The key difference is whether the conjunctive participates with both or only one part.
This warning is crucial to avoid confusion in practical application.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Negative disjunctives and the divided syllogism
Negative disjunctives and divided syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a compound syllogism involving a negative disjunctive premise, distinguishing between the two types of disjunction.

Core Idea
Negative disjunctives mirror positive ones but with swapped types: negative exclusive-of-combination behaves like positive exclusive-of-emptiness, and vice versa.

The author now extends the analysis to negative disjunctives. He states that a negative exclusive-of-combination (mani'at al-jam' saliba) has the same ruling as a positive exclusive-of-emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw mūjiba) when combined with a conjunctive. He also discusses the 'divided syllogism' (qiyas muqassam) where the conclusion is a categorical proposition, and how a negative exclusive-of-combination yields a possibility (jawaz). Finally, he outlines the conditions for negative exclusive-of-emptiness. The text cuts off, but the core principles are clear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of negative disjunction
If the negative disjunction is exclusive of combination (mani'at al-jam' saliba), its behavior mirrors a positive exclusive of emptiness. If it is exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw saliba), it mirrors a positive exclusive of combination.
This symmetry simplifies the analysis: you can convert the negative to its positive counterpart.
2
Apply the corresponding positive rules
For a negative exclusive-of-combination, treat it as a positive exclusive-of-emptiness and follow the steps from Lesson 2 (or vice versa). For example, if the negative disjunctive participates with both parts, the conclusion will be a negative exclusive-of-combination (like positive exclusive-of-emptiness).
The author explicitly says: 'حكمها حكم مانعة الخلو إذا كانت موجبة'.
3
Handle the divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam)
When the disjunctive is negative exclusive-of-combination and the goal is a categorical conclusion, the result is only a possibility (jawaz) that the categorical holds. The truth of each disjunct combined with the conjunctive necessitates the categorical, so possibility entails possibility.
This is a weaker conclusion than certainty.
4
Apply the condition for production
The condition for both types is that the result of combining the disjunctive with the conjunctive must yield the participating part. The text cuts off, but the condition is that the compound syllogism must be properly formed.
The missing part likely elaborates on this condition.

This is when the disjunctive is positive. As for when it is negative, if it is exclusive of combination, its ruling is the ruling of exclusive of emptiness when positive, meaning that what the positive exclusive of emptiness produces with the conjunctive, the negative exclusive of combination produces with it. As for the divided syllogism whose conclusion is conjunctive, if the disjunctive is negative exclusive of combination, it produces the possibility of the truth of that conjunctive, because the truth of each of the parts of the disjunction together with the conjunctive that participates with it entails the truth of that conjunctive. So possibility entails possibility. And it produces a negative exclusive of combination from the result of the combination and the non-participating part, if the participating part is one of the disjuncts, and from the result of the other combination if it participates with the conjunctive. For the participating side entails the result of the combination by what we said about the syllogism composed of conjunctive and conditional. Then the negative disjunctive if it is exclusive of emptiness, its ruling is the ruling of exclusive of combination when positive, meaning that the conditions considered in producing the positive when it is exclusive of combination are considered when it is exclusive of emptiness. The condition is that the result of the combination with the conjunctive premise produces the participating part of...
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe: the author explains how disjunctive syllogisms (with conjunctive premises) produce conclusions for both types of disjunction. He first considers the case where the disjunctive is 'mani'at al-jam'' (exclusive of combination, i.e., at most one true) and the conjunctive premise participates with either both or only one disjunct. He gives examples: when the disjunctive is the minor premise and both conjunctives are present, the conclusion is a positive exclusive of combination. For instance, 'Either no A is B or no C is D, and every B is E, and every D is F' yields 'Either no A is E or no C is F' via compound reasoning. Similarly, when the disjunctive is major, a similar pattern holds. When the conjunctive participates with only one disjunct, the conclusion is a positive exclusive of emptiness, as in 'Either no A is B or every C is D, and every B is E' yields 'Either no A is E or every C is D' exclusive of combination. The author warns not to confuse the methods for the two types of disjunction. He then discusses negative disjunctives: a negative exclusive of combination behaves like a positive exclusive of emptiness, and vice versa. The divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam) with a negative exclusive of combination yields the possibility (jawaz) of the conjunctive conclusion. The text ends mid-sentence, cutting off the condition. The author's treatment is thorough, showing how to derive conclusions from complex mixed syllogisms using Aristotelian principles.

مانعة الجمع سالبة
مانعة الخلو سالبة

Deriving Conclusions from Disjunctive and Categorical Premises
Author explains production rules for disjunctive syllogisms with categorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the correct conclusion from a disjunctive syllogism combined with a categorical premise, distinguishing between exclusive (مانعة الجمع) and exhaustive (مانعة الخلو) disjunctions.

Core Idea
The position of the categorical premise determines whether the conclusion is exclusive or exhaustive.

When a disjunctive premise (منفصلة) is combined with a categorical premise (حملية), the resulting conclusion is a new disjunction. The author presents two configurations based on which premise is major and minor, and warns against confusing the methods for producing exclusive vs. exhaustive disjunctions. Understanding these rules is essential for properly evaluating compound syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive type
Determine whether the disjunctive is exclusive (مانعة الجمع) or exhaustive (مانعة الخلو). The text deals with both affirmative and negative forms.
Exclusive: parts cannot both be true. Exhaustive: parts cannot both be false.
2
Locate the categorical premise
Is the categorical premise the minor or the major? The author provides examples for both cases.
Minor: disjunctive is first; Major: disjunctive is second.
3
Apply the production rule
Use the given example to see how the conclusion is formed. When categorical is major, the conclusion is an exclusive disjunction; when categorical is minor, the conclusion is exhaustive (with affirmative disjunctives).
The method differs based on the disjunctive's type and polarity.
4
Check the conclusion type
Verify that the produced disjunction matches the expected type. The author emphasizes that the methods are distinct.
Do not assume the same method works for both types.

وإن كان الثاني وهو أن يكون المشارك الحملية أحد جزئي المنفصلة فقط ينتج موجبة مانعة الخلو أيضًا كما في قولنا والمنفصلة صغرى إما لا شيء من «أب» أو كل ج د وكل (ب) (هـ) ينتج إما لا شيء من «أهـ) أو كل (ج) د مانعة من الجمع لاستلزام لا شيء من «أب» للاشيء من «أب». وأما إذا كانت المنفصلة هي الكبرى كما في قولنا كل أب» وإما أن يكون بعض ب هـ) أو كل د ط» ينتج إما أن يكون بعض ( أ ه » أو كل أط» لاستلزام بعض أه» بعض ب هـ بالقياس المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل. ولا يظن أن الطريق في إنتاج مانعة الجمع هو الطريق المستعمل في مانعة الخلو بل يكون في هذا بطريق وفي ذلك بطريق يعرف بالتأمل : هذا إذا كانت المنفصلة موجبة. فأما إذا كانت سالبة فإن كانت مانعة الجمع فحكمها حكم مانعة الخلو إذا كانت موجبة يعني ما ينتج الموجبة المانعة الخلو مع الحملية ينتج السالبة المانعة الجمع معها.
And if it is the second, that the categorical participant is only one of the two parts of the disjunctive, it produces an affirmative exhaustive disjunction also, as in our saying, and the disjunctive is minor: 'Either nothing from A is B, or every C is D, and every B is H' produces 'Either nothing from A is H, or every C is D', an exclusive disjunction, because 'nothing from A is B' entails 'nothing from A is B'. And it should not be thought that the method for producing an exclusive disjunction is the method used for an exhaustive disjunction, but rather this has one method and that has another method known by reflection: this if the disjunctive is affirmative. As for if it is negative, then if it is an exclusive disjunction, its ruling is the ruling of an exhaustive disjunction when it is affirmative, meaning that what the affirmative exhaustive disjunction produces with the categorical, the negative exclusive disjunction produces with it.

Commentary
This passage explains the production rules for disjunctive syllogisms when combined with categorical premises. In each case, the conclusion is a disjunction of a specific type. The author warns against confusing the method for producing an exclusive disjunction (maniat al-jam') with that for an exhaustive disjunction (maniat al-khuluw). The rules depend on whether the disjunctive is affirmative or negative. The text uses abstract variables (أ، ب، ج) to illustrate the logical forms. The key point is that the position of the categorical premise relative to the disjunctive and the type of disjunction determine the conclusion.

The Syllogism

The Divided Syllogism: Definitions and Conditions
Author explains the divided syllogism and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a divided syllogism and determine the type of conclusion based on the disjunction's exclusivity and exhaustiveness.

Core Idea
The divided syllogism combines a disjunctive with a categorical; the output depends on the disjunction's type.

This lesson introduces the qiyas muqassam, a syllogism where a disjunctive premise is combined with a categorical premise. The author explains how the truth of the categorical conclusion is entailed, and how different types of disjunctions (exclusive, exhaustive, affirmative, negative) affect the production. This tool is essential in Islamic logic for reasoning with alternatives, particularly in legal and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Find the disjunctive premise and the categorical premise. The syllogism is formed by combining a disjunctive (like 'Every B is either D or H or T') with a categorical (like 'Every J is B').
The disjunctive can be exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw).
2
Determine the type of disjunction
Check whether the disjunctive is affirmative or negative, and whether it is exclusive, exhaustive, or 'real' (both exclusive and exhaustive). This determines the production rules.
The author notes that a negative exclusive with a categorical yields a categorical possibility, not certainty.
3
Locate the participating term
Identify which part of the disjunction shares a term with the categorical premise. This term (the participant) is crucial for producing the conclusion.
If the participant is one side, the conclusion relates to the non-participating side; if both sides participate, the conclusion relates to each.
4
Apply the production rules
For a negative exclusive disjunction, the syllogism produces a categorical possibility. For an exhaustive disjunction, the conditions mirror those of the exclusive, but the conclusion becomes a negative exhaustive proposition.
The author emphasizes that not every divided syllogism is productive; it depends on whether the disjunction is 'real' (haqiqiyya).
5
Check for productivity
Assess whether the syllogism actually yields a conclusion. An affirmative real disjunction produces; a negative real disjunction does not (as will be explained later).
This step helps avoid invalid reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القياس المقسَّم الذي نتيجته حملية فإذا كانت المنفصلة سالبة مانعة الجمع ينتج جواز صدق تلك الحملية؛ لأن صدق كل واحد من أجزاء المنفصلة مع الحملية المشارك إياه مستلزم لصدق تلك الحملية؛ فالجواز إذن مستلزم للجواز. وإنه ينتج سالبة مانعة الجمع من نتيجة التأليف، والجزء الغير المشارك إن كان المشارك أحد حزئي المنفصلة. ومن نتيجة التأليف الآخر إن كان مشاركًا للحملية؛ فإن الطرف المشارك مستلزم لنتيجة التأليف لما مرّ من المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل . ثمَّ المنفصلة السالبة إذا كانت مانعة الخلو فحكمها حكم المانعة من الجمع إذا كانت موجبة يعني ما يعتبر من الشرائط في إنتاج الموجبة إذا كانت مانعة الجمع؛ فذلك يعتبر إذا كانت مانعة الخلو. والشرط هو أن تكون نتيجة التأليف مع الحملية منتجة للطرف المشارك من المنفصلة. وعند ذلك فالقياس الذي ينتج الموجبة المانعة الجمع هناك ينتج السالبة المانعة الخلو هنا من نتيجة التأليف والطرف الغير المشارك إن كانت المشاركة مع أحد الطرفين، ونتيجة التأليف الآخر إن كانت المشاركة مع كل واحد من الطرفين لأن نتيجة التأليف مستلزم للطرف المشارك بالقياس المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل كما مر . والقياس الذي نحن بصدده لا يلزم أن يكون منتجًا موجبة كانت المنفصلة أو سالبة؛ فإنها إذا كانت حقيقية مثلا فإنه ينتج إذا كانت موجبة، ولا ينتج إذا كانت سالبة يعرف من بعد. وقد قيل في هذا القياس إنه لا يقع إلا على الشكل الأول أو الثالث، فإن كان على الشكل الأول فقد اندرج موضوع الحملية تحت الانفصال المذكور، وإن كان على الثالث فلا بد من بيان الإنتاج ؛ وذلك بعكس الصغرى الحملية إن كانت الكبرى كلية أو بالافتراق إن كانت جزئية : مثال الشكل الأول والمنفصلة موجبة كل ج ب وكل واحد من (ب) إما د وإما ه وإما ط ينتج كل ج إما د وإما ه وإما ط، حقيقية إن كانت الكبرى حقيقية وغير حقيقية إذا كانت غير حقيقية. مثاله والمنفصلة سالبة كل ج ب ولا شيء من ب إما د وإما ه وإما ط ينتج لا شيء من ج إما د وإما ه وإما ط. مثال الشكل الثالث والمنفصلة موجبة كل ب ج وكل (ب) إما د وإما ه وإما ط ينتج بعض ج إما د وإما ه وإما ط بعكس الصغرى كما مرَّ . مثاله والمنفصلة موجبة جزئية كل ب ج وبعض ما يصدق عليه إنه ب إما د وإما ه وإما ط بالافتراض، وهو أن يفترض البعض الذي هو موصوف بالانفصال من ب ج ليصدق كل ج إما د وإما ه وإما ط لما مرَّ ؛ وهو المطلوب. وعلى هذا إذا كانت المنفصلة سالبة. ثمّ الكبرى إذا كانت حملية كان القياس أشبه بالحمليّات.
And as for the divided syllogism whose conclusion is categorical, if the disjunctive is negative exclusive, it produces the possibility of the truth of that categorical; because the truth of each of the parts of the disjunctive together with the categorical participant entails the truth of that categorical; so the possibility entails the possibility. And it produces a negative exclusive disjunction from the result of the composition, and the non-participating part if the participant is one of the two parts of the disjunctive. And from the other result of the composition if it participates with the categorical; for the participating term entails the result of the composition due to what has passed from the composition of the categorical and conditional. Then the negative disjunctive, if it is exhaustive, its ruling is the ruling of the exclusive if it is affirmative, meaning that the conditions considered in the production of the affirmative when it is exclusive are considered when it is exhaustive. And the condition is that the result of the composition with the categorical produces the participating term of the disjunctive. And accordingly, the syllogism that produces the affirmative exclusive there produces the negative exhaustive here from the result of the composition and the non-participating term if the participation is with one of the two sides, and from the other result of the composition if the participation is with each of the two sides because the result of the composition entails the participating term by the syllogism composed of the categorical and conditional as passed. And the syllogism we are dealing with is not necessarily productive whether the disjunctive is affirmative or negative; for if it is real, for example, then it produces if affirmative, and does not produce if negative, as will be known later. And it has been said regarding this syllogism that it only occurs in the first or third figure. If it is in the first figure, then the subject of the categorical is subsumed under the aforementioned disjunction; and if it is in the third, then it is necessary to explain the production, and that is by conversion of the minor categorical if the major is universal, or by assumption if it is particular. Example of the first figure: the disjunctive is affirmative: every J is B, and every single one of B is either D or H or T, produces every J is either D or H or T, real if the major is real, and non-real if it is non-real. Its example: the disjunctive is negative: every J is B, and nothing from B is either D or H or T, produces nothing from J is either D or H or T. Example of the third figure: the disjunctive is affirmative: every B is J, and every B is either D or H or T, produces some J is either D or H or T, by conversion of the minor as passed. Its example: the disjunctive is affirmative particular: every B is J, and some of what is described as B is either D or H or T, by assumption, which is that one assumes the part that is described by the disjunction from B J so that every J is either D or H or T, due to what passed; and that is the desired. And similarly if the disjunctive is negative. Then if the major is categorical, the syllogism is more akin to categoricals.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage discusses the divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam), a type of disjunctive syllogism where the disjunctive premise is combined with a categorical premise. The author first considers the case where the disjunctive is negative exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and the conclusion is categorical, showing that it only yields possibility. Then he explains how to produce negative exclusive or negative exhaustive conclusions depending on the position of the participating term. The author emphasizes that the conditions for production differ between exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions. He then states that the divided syllogism only occurs in the first or third figure. For the first figure, if the disjunctive is the major premise, the subject of the categorical is directly under the disjunction. For the third figure, the minor categorical must be converted (if universal) or an assumption (if particular) must be made. Detailed examples are provided with abstract terms (ج، ب، د، هـ، ط) illustrating both affirmative and negative disjunctives in both figures. The passage ends by noting that if the major premise is categorical, the syllogism resembles purely categorical reasoning.

مانعة الخلو

The Syllogism
