Figures of the Divided Syllogism: Examples
Author provides examples of the divided syllogism in first and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid divided syllogism in the first or third figure and verify its conclusion using conversion or assumption.

Core Idea
The divided syllogism only occurs in the first or third figure; the method of inference depends on the figure and type of disjunction.

This lesson continues the discussion of the divided syllogism, focusing on its occurrence in the first and third figures. The author provides detailed examples using abstract terms (ج, ب, د, هـ, ط) to illustrate how the conclusion is derived. For the first figure, the subject of the categorical is directly under the disjunction. For the third figure, conversion or assumption is needed. This practical demonstration solidifies the theoretical rules from the previous lesson.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the figure
Determine whether the syllogism is in the first figure (subject of categorical subsumed under disjunction) or third figure (predicate of categorical is the subject of the disjunction).
The author states it only occurs in these two figures.
2
First figure with affirmative disjunction
Example: 'Every J is B' and 'Every B is either D or H or T' → 'Every J is either D or H or T'. The conclusion is universal and matches the disjunction's type (real or non-real).
No conversion needed; the categorical minor fits directly.
3
First figure with negative disjunction
Example: 'Every J is B' and 'Nothing from B is either D or H or T' → 'Nothing from J is either D or H or T'. The conclusion is also universal negative.
The same structure applies.
4
Third figure with affirmative universal disjunction
Example: 'Every B is J' and 'Every B is either D or H or T' → 'Some J is either D or H or T'. This requires conversion of the minor premise (aks) to get a universal categorical before combining.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate: 'Every B is J' becomes 'Some J is B'.
5
Third figure with affirmative particular disjunction
When the disjunctive is particular ('Some B is either D or H or T'), use assumption (iftirāq): assume that the part of B that is disjunctive is the same as the part that is J, then derive a universal conclusion.
This method ensures the conclusion covers all J.

مستلزم للجواز. وإنه ينتج سالبة مانعة الجمع من نتيجة التأليف، والجزء الغير المشارك إن كان المشارك أحد حزئي المنفصلة. ومن نتيجة التأليف الآخر إن كان مشاركًا للحملية؛ فإن الطرف المشارك مستلزم لنتيجة التأليف لما مرّ من المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل . ثمَّ المنفصلة السالبة إذا كانت مانعة الخلو فحكمها حكم المانعة من الجمع إذا كانت موجبة يعني ما يعتبر من الشرائط في إنتاج الموجبة إذا كانت مانعة الجمع؛ فذلك بالافتراق إن كانت جزئية : مثال الشكل الأول والمنفصلة موجبة كل ج ب وكل واحد من (ب) إما د وإما ه وإما ط ينتج كل ج إما د وإما ه وإما ط، حقيقية إن كانت الكبرى حقيقية وغير حقيقية إذا كانت غير حقيقية. مثاله والمنفصلة سالبة كل ج ب ولا شيء من ب إما د وإما ه وإما ط ينتج لا شيء من ج إما د وإما ه وإما ط. مثال الشكل الثالث والمنفصلة موجبة كل ب ج وكل (ب) إما د وإما ه وإما ط ينتج بعض ج إما د وإما ه وإما ط بعكس الصغرى كما مرَّ . مثاله والمنفصلة موجبة جزئية كل ب ج وبعض ما يصدق عليه إنه ب إما د وإما ه وإما ط بالافتراض، وهو أن يفترض البعض الذي هو موصوف بالانفصال من ب ج ليصدق كل ج إما د وإما ه وإما ط لما مرَّ ؛ وهو المطلوب. وعلى هذا إذا كانت المنفصلة سالبة. ثمّ الكبرى إذا كانت حملية كان القياس أشبه بالحمليّات.
And as for the divided syllogism whose conclusion is categorical, if the disjunctive is negative exclusive, it produces the possibility of the truth of that categorical; because the truth of each of the parts of the disjunctive together with the categorical participant entails the truth of that categorical; so the possibility entails the possibility. And it produces a negative exclusive disjunction from the result of the composition, and the non-participating part if the participant is one of the two parts of the disjunctive. And from the other result of the composition if it participates with the categorical; for the participating term entails the result of the composition due to what has passed from the composition of the categorical and conditional. Then the negative disjunctive, if it is exhaustive, its ruling is the ruling of the exclusive if it is affirmative, meaning that the conditions considered in the production of the affirmative when it is exclusive are considered when it is exhaustive. And the condition is that the result of the composition with the categorical produces the participating term of the disjunctive. And accordingly, the syllogism that produces the affirmative exclusive there produces the negative exhaustive here from the result of the composition and the non-participating term if the participation is with one of the two sides, and from the other result of the composition if the participation is with each of the two sides because the result of the composition entails the participating term by the syllogism composed of the categorical and conditional as passed. And the syllogism we are dealing with is not necessarily productive whether the disjunctive is affirmative or negative; for if it is real, for example, then it produces if affirmative, and does not produce if negative, as will be known later. And it has been said regarding this syllogism that it only occurs in the first or third figure. If it is in the first figure, then the subject of the categorical is subsumed under the aforementioned disjunction; and if it is in the third, then it is necessary to explain the production, and that is by conversion of the minor categorical if the major is universal, or by assumption if it is particular. Example of the first figure: the disjunctive is affirmative: every J is B, and every single one of B is either D or H or T, produces every J is either D or H or T, real if the major is real, and non-real if it is non-real. Its example: the disjunctive is negative: every J is B, and nothing from B is either D or H or T, produces nothing from J is either D or H or T. Example of the third figure: the disjunctive is affirmative: every B is J, and every B is either D or H or T, produces some J is either D or H or T, by conversion of the minor as passed. Its example: the disjunctive is affirmative particular: every B is J, and some of what is described as B is either D or H or T, by assumption, which is that one assumes the part that is described by the disjunction from B J so that every J is either D or H or T, due to what passed; and that is the desired. And similarly if the disjunctive is negative. Then if the major is categorical, the syllogism is more akin to categoricals.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage discusses the divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam), a type of disjunctive syllogism where the disjunctive premise is combined with a categorical premise. The author first considers the case where the disjunctive is negative exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and the conclusion is categorical, showing that it only yields possibility. Then he explains how to produce negative exclusive or negative exhaustive conclusions depending on the position of the participating term. The author emphasizes that the conditions for production differ between exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions. He then states that the divided syllogism only occurs in the first or third figure. For the first figure, if the disjunctive is the major premise, the subject of the categorical is directly under the disjunction. For the third figure, the minor categorical must be converted (if universal) or an assumption (if particular) must be made. Detailed examples are provided with abstract terms (ج، ب، د، هـ، ط) illustrating both affirmative and negative disjunctives in both figures. The passage ends by noting that if the major premise is categorical, the syllogism resembles purely categorical reasoning.

The Syllogism

Syllogisms with Disjunctive Minor in First and Third Figures
Author explains production rules for disjunctive-minor syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid syllogism with a disjunctive minor and categorical major in the first or third figure and verify its production.

Core Idea
A disjunctive minor with a categorical major yields necessary conclusions only in first and third figures.

This lesson explores a special type of syllogism where the minor premise is disjunctive and the major is categorical. The author explains that such syllogisms only occur in the first or third figure. We'll learn the production rules and how conversion or presupposition enables the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figures
Recognize that the author restricts this syllogism to first and third figures. In the first figure, the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor; in the third, it is subject of both premises.
This exclusion of the second and fourth figures is a known result in classical logic.
2
Understand the production condition
The disjunctive major must be affirmative (e.g., 'every B is either D or H') for the syllogism to produce a conclusion. If the disjunctive is negative, it does not produce, as the author states.
The author mentions 'real' (haqiqiyya) disjunction; for non-real disjunctions, the rule may differ.
3
Analyze the first figure example
Take the categorical minor 'every J is B' and the disjunctive major 'each B is either D or H'. Since the subject of the minor (J) falls under the disjunction via B, the conclusion 'every J is either D or H' follows directly.
This is analogous to a standard first-figure categorical syllogism but with a disjunctive conclusion.
4
Analyze the third figure example
Here the categorical is major: 'every B is J' and the disjunctive is minor: 'each B is either D or H'. To derive a conclusion, convert the categorical minor ('every B is J' becomes 'some J is B' by conversion per accidens) and then apply the first-figure reasoning, yielding 'some J is either D or H'.
If the categorical is particular, the author suggests using 'presupposition' (iftiraq) to handle the partial overlap.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Construct a first-figure syllogism: 'Every prayer is an act of worship. Every act of worship is either obligatory or supererogatory. Therefore, every prayer is either obligatory or supererogatory.' Identify the figure and verify production.
This mirrors the author's example with variables replaced by fiqh terms.

The disjunctive. And accordingly, the syllogism that produces the affirmative exclusive-of-union (there) produces the negative exclusive-of-void (here) from the result of composition and the non-participating term, if the participation is with one of the two terms, and the result of the other composition if the participation is with each of the two terms, because the result of composition entails the participating term by the syllogism composed of categorical and conditional as previously. And the syllogism we are dealing with is not necessarily productive whether the disjunctive is affirmative or negative; for if it is real, for example, then it produces if affirmative, and does not produce if negative, as will be known later. And it has been said concerning this syllogism that it occurs only in the first or third figure. If it is in the first figure, then the subject of the categorical falls under the mentioned disjunction; and if it is in the third, then the demonstration of production is necessary; and that is by conversion of the categorical minor if the major is universal, or by disjunction (iftiraq) if it is particular. Example of the first figure: the disjunctive affirmative: every J is B, and each one of (B) is either D or H or T, produces every J is either D or H or T, real if the major is real [220w] and non-real if it is non-real. Example: and the disjunctive negative: every J is B, and none of B is either D or H or T, produces none of J is either D or H or T. Example of the third figure: the disjunctive affirmative: every B is J, and each (B) is either D or H or T, produces some J is either D or H or T, by conversion of the minor as previously. Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for syllogisms that have a disjunctive (munfasila) premise as the minor and a categorical (hamliyya) premise as the major. The author explains that such syllogisms only occur in the first or third figure. In the first figure, the subject of the categorical minor (which is the middle term) falls under the disjunctive major, yielding a disjunctive conclusion. For example, if every J is B and each B is either D or H or T, then every J is either D or H or T. If the disjunctive is negative, the conclusion is also negative. In the third figure, since the middle term is the subject of the categorical major, we need to convert the minor (if the major is universal) or use a presupposition (iftiraq) to derive the conclusion. For instance, if every B is J and each B is either D or H or T, then some J is either D or H or T. The author notes that if the disjunctive is real (haqiqiyya), the conclusion follows; otherwise, it may not. The passage ends by stating that if the major is categorical, the syllogism behaves like a categorical one.

Shaykh's Rules for Disjunctive Syllogisms with Shared Parts and Composite Conditionals
Author explains Ibn Sina's view on disjunctive with shared parts and composite conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Shaykh's conditions for productive disjunctive syllogisms with shared parts and extend them to composite syllogisms involving conditionals.

Core Idea
Disjunctive rules cascade into composite conditional syllogisms.

Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) sets precise conditions for when a disjunctive premise with shared parts yields a valid conclusion. The author not only explains these conditions but also shows how they govern compound syllogisms where a categorical premise is replaced by a conditional. This progression reveals the deep unity of syllogistic reasoning in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the disjunctive type
Check if the disjunctive premise is minor or major and whether it has shared parts (i.e., a common term other than the middle). The conditions differ based on this.
Shared parts here refer to the disjunctive whose parts share a term, not the middle.
2
Apply the affirmation condition
When the disjunctive is minor and the major is categorical, the disjunctive must be affirmative to produce. When the disjunctive is major, if it is affirmative it always produces; if negative, it produces only if its parts are affirmative.
This is a key rule from the Shaykh.
3
Remember the istithna' exception
In an exceptive syllogism (istithna'), the conclusion follows absolutely, regardless of the disjunctive's quality. This is a special case.
Istithna' corresponds to modus ponens and modus tollens.
4
Extend to composite conditionals
Replace the categorical premise with a conjunctive or disjunctive conditional. The same production rules apply to the resulting composite syllogism.
The composite syllogism's conclusion will involve conditionals.
5
Verify with examples
Work through the given examples: one with a conjunctive conditional (if A then B) and one with a disjunctive conditional (either C or D, exclusive). Confirm that the conclusions follow the rules.
Examples help solidify understanding.

وأما قول الشيخ وهو أن المنفصلة المشتركة الأجزاء في أحد الجزأين اللذين هما غير الأوسط إذا كانت صغرى والحمليّات كبرى وهي الاشتراك في حد يشترط في إنتاج القياس أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة. وأما إذا كانت هذه المنفصلة كبرى فإن كانت موجبة فإنه ينتج لا محالة، وإن كانت سالبة فإنه لا ينتج إلا إذا كانت موجبة الأجزاء. ثمَّ قال وأنتَ فقد أحطت بالإنتاج إذا كانت سالبة وهي صغرى بعد حصول الشرائط وبإنتاجها أيضًا إذا كانت سالبة وهي كبرى على ذلك التفصيل إلا في صورة الاستثناء فإنه ينتج مطلقا». فالمراد منه ما تدل عليه هذه الألفاظ من حيث هي هذه الألفاظ : وهذا هو الحق. وأما الحكم بإنتاج هذه القضية بعد الاطلاع على الشرائط التي مرَّ ذكرها من التأليف المنتج وغيره فذلك من جملة ما يعرف بالعقل أن المنفصلة إذا كانت حقيقية يلزم كذا وإذا كانت غير المشتركة يلزم كذا؛ واللازم أعني النتيجة يتبع المنفصلة في الكيف دائما إذا علمتَ هذا فنقول إن القياس المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل إذا كان مكان الحملي فيه شرطية متصلة كانت أو منفصلة فحكمه حكم المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل إلا أن نتيجة التأليف من حملية وشرطية أو من شرطيتين. مثال ما تكون الشرطية فيه متّصلة كلَّما كان «أب» ف اج دا وكل «هـ ط»، وكلما كان كل «حج» فـ «وز» ينتج كلما كان «أب» ف اج دا فقد يكون إذا كان كل هـج فــ و ز» . ومثال ما تكون الشرطية فيه منفصلة كلَّما كان «أب» فــ «ج د» فكل «هـ ط»، ودائما إما أن يكون كل طج» أو «و ز مانعا من الخلو ينتج كلَّما كان «أب» فـ (ج د» فإما أن يكون كل «هــج » أو «و ز مانعا من الخلو . وعلى هذا فيما يكون من أمثال هذه الأقيسة. وأما القياس المؤلف من الحملي والمنفصل فلو كان مكان الحملي [۲۲] فيه شرطية فحكمه حكم المؤلف من الحملي والمنفصل. / مثال ما تكون
As for the statement of the Shaykh, which is that the disjunctive whose parts share in one of the two parts that are not the middle, when it is minor and the categoricals are major, and it is the sharing in a limit, the condition for production of the syllogism is that the disjunctive be affirmative. As for when this disjunctive is major, then if it is affirmative, it produces necessarily; and if it is negative, it does not produce unless its parts are affirmative. Then he said: And you have encompassed production when it is negative and a minor after obtaining the conditions, and also its production when it is negative and a major according to that detail except in the case of exception (istithna'), for it produces absolutely. So the intended meaning is what these words indicate in themselves, and this is the truth. As for the judgment of production of this proposition after examining the conditions previously mentioned from the productive composition and others, that is among what is known by reason, that if the disjunctive is real, such and such follows; and if it is non-shared, such and such follows; and the necessary, namely the conclusion, always follows the disjunctive in quality. If you know this, then we say: The syllogism composed of categorical and conditional, if the categorical is replaced by a conditional (whether conjunctive or disjunctive), its ruling is like the ruling of the composition of categorical and conditional, except that the result of composition is from categorical and conditional or from two conditionals. Example of where the conditional is conjunctive: Whenever A is B, then J is D, and every H is T, and whenever every H is J, then W is Z, produces: Whenever A is B, then J is D, and it may be that whenever every H is J, then W is Z. And example of where the conditional is disjunctive: Whenever A is B, then J is D, and every H is T, and always either every H is J or W is Z, exclusive of void, produces: Whenever A is B, then J is D, and either every H is J or W is Z, exclusive of void. And likewise for similar syllogisms. As for the syllogism composed of categorical and disjunctive, if the categorical is replaced by a conditional, its ruling is like the ruling of the composition of categorical and disjunctive. / Example of where

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) regarding disjunctive syllogisms with shared parts. The author explains that when the disjunctive is minor and the major is categorical, the disjunctive must be affirmative for production. When the disjunctive is major, if affirmative it always produces; if negative, it produces only if its parts are affirmative. An exception is the case of 'istithna'' (exceptive syllogism), which always produces. The author then generalizes to composite syllogisms where a categorical premise is replaced by a conditional (either conjunctive or disjunctive). The ruling then follows that of the composite syllogism of categorical and conditional, but the conclusion may involve conditionals. Two examples are given: one with a conjunctive conditional and one with a disjunctive conditional. The passage ends by stating that if a categorical is replaced by a conditional in a composite disjunctive syllogism, the same rules apply.

Production Conditions for Disjunctive Syllogisms with Common Parts
Author interprets Shaykh's statement on disjunctive proposition's production conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a disjunctive syllogism with common parts yields a conclusion based on the quality (positive/negative) and position (minor/major) of the disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
A negative disjunctive as minor never produces; as major it produces only if its parts are positive.

When a disjunctive proposition shares a common term with the categorical premises in a syllogism, special conditions apply. The Shaykh's ruling clarifies when such a compound syllogism is productive. This lesson breaks down those conditions, focusing on the quality of the disjunctive and its role as minor or major premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunctive syllogism
Recognize that the disjunctive proposition has a common part with the categorical premises in one of its two non-middle parts. This is a special case of the disjunctive syllogism.
The common part is not the middle term; it appears in one disjunct.
2
Determine the position of the disjunctive (minor or major)
Check whether the disjunctive is the minor premise (the first premise) or the major premise (the second premise). The conditions differ accordingly.
In the source, the Shaykh treats both cases separately.
3
Apply the quality condition based on position
If the disjunctive is minor, it must be positive (affirmative) for the syllogism to produce. If it is major, a positive disjunctive always produces, but a negative disjunctive produces only if its own parts are positive.
The exception is the case of selective exception (istithnā'), where production is unconditional regardless of quality.
4
Verify with an example
Construct a sample syllogism with a positive disjunctive minor and check its productivity. Then try a negative disjunctive minor and see that it fails.

وأما قول الشيخ وهو أن المنفصلة المشتركة الأجزاء في أحد الجزأين اللذين هما غير الأوسط إذا كانت صغرى والحمليّات كبرى وهي الاشتراك في حد يشترط في إنتاج القياس أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة. وأما إذا كانت هذه المنفصلة كبرى فإن كانت موجبة فإنه ينتج لا محالة، وإن كانت سالبة فإنه لا ينتج إلا إذا كانت موجبة الأجزاء. ثمَّ قال وأنتَ فقد أحطت بالإنتاج إذا كانت سالبة وهي صغرى بعد حصول الشرائط وبإنتاجها أيضًا إذا كانت سالبة وهي كبرى على ذلك التفصيل إلا في صورة الاستثناء فإنه ينتج مطلقا». فالمراد منه ما تدل عليه هذه الألفاظ من حيث هي هذه الألفاظ : وهذا هو الحق. وأما الحكم بإنتاج هذه القضية بعد الاطلاع على الشرائط التي مرَّ ذكرها من التأليف المنتج وغيره فذلك من جملة ما يعرف بالعقل
As for the statement of the Shaykh, that the disjunctive with common parts in one of the two parts that are not the middle, when it is minor and the categoricals are major, and the commonality is in a limit, it is required for the production of the syllogism that the disjunctive be positive. And if this disjunctive is major, then if it is positive, it produces necessarily; and if it is negative, it does not produce except if its parts are positive. Then he said: 'You have encompassed production when it is negative as a minor after obtaining the conditions, and also its production when it is negative as a major according to that detail except in the case of exception, for it produces absolutely.' So the intended meaning is what these words indicate inasmuch as they are these words; and this is the truth. As for the judgment of production of this proposition after examining the conditions that were mentioned of the productive composition and others, that is part of what is known by the intellect.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage interprets a statement by the Shaykh regarding the disjunctive proposition that has a common part with the categorical premises in one of the two non-middle parts. When this disjunctive is the minor premise and the categoricals are major, the disjunctive must be positive for the syllogism to produce a conclusion. If the disjunctive is the major premise, then if it is positive it always yields a conclusion; if it is negative, it yields a conclusion only if its own parts are positive. The Shaykh then summarizes that the student has fully understood the production conditions when the disjunctive is negative as a minor, and also when it is negative as a major, with the same detail except in the case of selective exception (istithna'), where it produces unconditionally. The author affirms that this interpretation is correct and that the actual logical entailment depends on the conditions known by reason.

Key Takeaway
The productivity of a disjunctive syllogism with common parts hinges on the quality and position of the disjunctive: positive if minor; if major, negative only when its parts are positive, except in the istithnā' form.

Mixed Syllogisms: Categorical with Conditional Premises
Author lays out rule for compound syllogisms where one premise is conditional and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a mixed syllogism that combines a categorical premise with either a connected or disjunctive conditional premise, and determine the form of its conclusion.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism with a conditional yields a conclusion in that conditional's form.

This lesson explores syllogisms that blend categorical and conditional premises. While classical logic often treats these separately, our author shows that when one premise is a conditional (connected or disjunctive), the conclusion takes the same conditional form. Understanding this allows you to handle complex arguments found in Islamic theology, where conditional reasoning is common.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types
Look at the syllogism: one premise is a categorical (e.g., 'Every H is T') and the other is a conditional (e.g., 'Whenever A is B then J is D').
The categorical premise is a standard universal affirmative.
2
Classify the conditional
Determine if the conditional is connected (متصلة) or disjunctive (منفصلة). In connected, it's an 'if-then'; in disjunctive, it's an 'either-or' with exclusive (مانع من الخلو).
Disjunctive here is exclusive disjunction.
3
Apply the composition rule
The conclusion of the composition is from a categorical and a conditional, meaning the conclusion takes the logical form of the conditional premise while incorporating information from the categorical.
The text says 'its ruling is the ruling of the syllogism composed of a categorical and a conditional'.
4
Trace the variable examples
Follow the author's examples: for connected conditional, the conclusion is a conditional. For disjunctive, the conclusion is a disjunction.
Pay attention to the shared terms (the middle term) between premises.
5
Generalize to two conditionals
The author states that any syllogism of two conditionals sharing a complete part from one and incomplete from the other reduces to these two types.
This shows the universality of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا علمت هذا فنقول إن القياس المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل إذا كان مكان الحملي فيه شرطية متصلة كانت أو منفصلة فحكمه حكم المؤلف من الحملي والمتصل إلا أن نتيجة التأليف من حملية وشرطية أو من شرطيتين. مثال ما تكون الشرطية فيه متّصلة كلَّما كان «أب» ف اج دا وكل «هـ ط»، وكلما كان كل «حج» فـ «وز» ينتج كلما كان «أب» ف اج دا فقد يكون إذا كان كل هـج فــ و ز» . ومثال ما تكون الشرطية فيه منفصلة كلَّما كان «أب» فــ «ج د» فكل «هـ ط»، ودائما إما أن يكون كل طج» أو «و ز مانعا من الخلو ينتج كلَّما كان «أب» فـ (ج د» فإما أن يكون كل «هــج » أو «و ز مانعا من الخلو . وعلى هذا فيما يكون من أمثال هذه الأقيسة. وأما القياس المؤلف من الحملي والمنفصل فلو كان مكان الحملي فيه شرطية فحكمه حكم المؤلف من الحملي والمنفصل. / مثال ما تكون الشرطية فيه (۱) متصلة قولنا إما أن يكون كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» وإما أن يكون كل هـ ط» مانعا من الخلو، وكلَّما كان كل طج» فــ «وز» ينتج إما أن يكون كلَّما كان «أب» فــ (ج د» وإما أن يكون قد يكون إذا كان «هج» فـوز مانعا من الخلو. مثال ما تكون الشرطية فيه منفصلة إما أن يكون كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» وإما أن يكون كل هـ ط» مانعا من الخلـو، ودائما إما أن يكون كل «طج» أو «و ز » مانعـا مـن الخلو أيضًا ينتج إما أن يكون كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» وإما أن يكون إما كل «ج» أو «وز» مانعا من الخلو. وبالجملة يندرج كل قياس مركب من شرطيتين يشتركان في جزء تام من إحدى المقدمتين غير تام من الأخرى تحت هذين القياسين: إما ما يكون من المتصلتين ففي المؤلف من الحملي والمتّصل، وإما ما يكون من المنفصلتين ففي المؤلف من الحملي والمنفصل.
If you know this, then we say: The syllogism composed of a categorical and a conditional—if the categorical is replaced by a conditional, whether connected or disjunctive—its ruling is the ruling of the syllogism composed of a categorical and a conditional, except that the conclusion of the composition is from a categorical and a conditional or from two conditionals. Example where the conditional is connected: Whenever 'A is B' then 'J is D', and 'Every H is T', and whenever 'Every H is J' then 'W is Z' yields: Whenever 'A is B' then 'J is D', so it may be that whenever 'Every H is J' then 'W is Z'. Example where the conditional is disjunctive: Whenever 'A is B' then 'J is D', and 'Every H is T', and always either 'Every T is J' or 'W is Z' as exclusive, yields: Whenever 'A is B' then 'J is D', so as a result: either 'Every H is J' or 'W is Z' as exclusive. And so on for similar syllogisms. As for the syllogism composed of a categorical and a disjunctive, if the categorical is replaced by a conditional, its ruling is the ruling of the syllogism composed of a categorical and a disjunctive. Example where the conditional is connected: Our statement: Either whenever 'A is B' then 'J is D', or 'Every H is T' exclusive, and whenever 'Every T is J' then 'W is Z' yields: Either whenever 'A is B' then 'J is D', or it may be that whenever 'H is J' then 'W is Z' exclusive. Example where the conditional is disjunctive: Either whenever 'A is B' then 'J is D', or 'Every H is T' exclusive, and always either 'Every T is J' or 'W is Z' also exclusive yields: Either whenever 'A is B' then 'J is D', or either 'Every J' or 'W is Z' exclusive. In summary, every syllogism composed of two conditionals that share a complete part from one premise and an incomplete part from the other falls under these two syllogisms: either what is of two connected conditionals falls under the composed of categorical and connected, or what is of two disjunctive conditionals falls under the composed of categorical and disjunctive.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage explains how to handle syllogisms where one premise is a categorical and the other is a conditional (either connected or disjunctive). The rule is that the resulting composition is analogous to the case where both premises are categorical, but the conclusion takes the form of the conditional. The author provides two main examples. First, when the conditional is connected: using variables, if we have 'Whenever A is B then J is D' and 'Every H is T', and we also have that 'Whenever every H is J then W is Z', the conclusion is that 'Whenever A is B then J is D, so it may be that whenever every H is J then W is Z.' This seems to illustrate a mixed syllogism with a hypothetical premise. Second, when the conditional is disjunctive: similarly, the conclusion preserves the disjunctive form. Then the author notes that if the categorical premise is replaced by a conditional, the same pattern holds. Finally, a general statement: any syllogism composed of two conditionals that share a complete part in one premise and an incomplete part in the other can be reduced to these two types.

Three Divisions of the Middle Term in Muttasila-Munfasila Syllogisms
Author enumerates three divisions based on the middle term's completeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three ways the middle term appears in compound syllogisms composed of a connected and disjunctive conditional: complete part, incomplete part, or mixed.

Core Idea
The middle term's completeness determines the syllogism's structural possibilities.

In this passage, the author begins the fifth section of compound syllogisms, which mix a connected conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila). The first key distinction is the nature of the middle term: whether it is a complete part of each premise, incomplete in each, or complete in one and incomplete in the other. This classification sets the stage for understanding how the figures interact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
From the text: the middle term is either a complete part of both premises, incomplete in both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other. Locate the enumeration markers [1], [ب], [ت] in the Arabic.
The markers [1], [ب], [ت] correspond to the three divisions.
2
Understand 'complete part'
A complete part means the middle term constitutes the entire subject or predicate of the premise. In categorical logic, this would be like the middle term being the whole subject or predicate. Here it applies to conditional premises as well.
3
Apply the classification
Given a compound syllogism, determine which category the middle term falls into by examining whether it appears as a full term or a part of a term in each premise.
This is a parsing exercise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس من الأقسام الخمسة فيما يتألف من المتصلة والمنفصلة؛ وإنه على ثلاثة أقسام: فإن الأوسط فيه [1] إما أن يكون جزءاً تاماً من كل واحدة من المقدمتين، [ب] أو غير تام من كل واحدة منهما، [ت] أو تاما من إحدى المقدمتين غير تام من الأخرى. فإن كان الأول فالمتّصلة إن كانت صغرى فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثاني، والشكل الثالث عن الرابع. وإن كانت كبرى فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثالث، والشكل الثاني عن الرابع لما مرَّ من قبل أن المقدم في المنفصلة لا يتميز عن التالي بالطبع. ثمَّ المتّصلة إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى؛ وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون الحد الأوسط تاليها أو مقدمها : فهذه أقسام أربعة. والشرط في الكل أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين موجبة، وإحديهما كلية ولا يلزم
The fifth section of five sections concerning what is composed of the connected and the disjunctive: and it is of three divisions. For the middle term in it is either [1] a complete part of each of the two premises, [b] or incomplete of each of them, [t] or complete of one of the premises and incomplete of the other. If it is the first, then if the connected is minor, the first figure cannot be distinguished from the second, and the third from the fourth. And if it is major, then the first figure cannot be distinguished from the third, and the second from the fourth, due to what has passed before: that the antecedent in the disjunctive is not naturally distinguished from the consequent. Then the connected is either minor or major; and in both cases, the middle term is either its consequent or its antecedent: these are four divisions. The condition in all is that one of the premises be positive, and one of them be universal, and it does not follow...
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the fifth type of compound syllogism: those composed of a connected conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive conditional (munfasila). The author states that this type has three divisions based on the nature of the middle term: either it is a complete part of both premises, or incomplete in both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other. In the first case, if the connected premise is minor, the first figure cannot be distinguished from the second, and the third cannot be distinguished from the fourth. If the connected is major, the first figure cannot be distinguished from the third, and the second from the fourth. The reason is that in a disjunctive, the antecedent and consequent are not naturally distinguished. Then the author further divides based on whether the connected is minor or major, and whether the middle term is its antecedent or consequent, yielding four sub-divisions. The general condition is that one premise must be positive and one universal. The text cuts off mid-sentence at 'and it does not follow...'

المقدم في المنفصلة

Indistinguishability of Figures in Muttasila-Munfasila Syllogisms
Author explains figure indistinguishability and four subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict which figures become indistinguishable based on whether the connected premise is minor or major.

Core Idea
When the middle term is a complete part, if the connected is minor, first and second merge; if major, first and third merge.

This passage continues the fifth section by examining the first division (middle term complete in both premises). The author explains that when the connected premise is minor, the first figure is indistinguishable from the second, and the third from the fourth. When the connected is major, the first merges with the third, and the second with the fourth. This is because in a disjunctive, the antecedent and consequent are not naturally ordered. Then the author further subdivides based on whether the middle term is the antecedent or consequent of the connected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Assume the middle term is complete in both premises. Now check if the connected premise (muttasila) is minor or major.
This is the first division out of three.
2
Apply the rule for minor
If the connected is minor, then the first figure cannot be distinguished from the second, and the third cannot be distinguished from the fourth. Memorize this pairing.
The text says: 'فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثاني، والشكل الثالث عن الرابع'
3
Apply the rule for major
If the connected is major, then the first figure cannot be distinguished from the third, and the second from the fourth.
4
Identify the four subdivisions
The connected can be minor or major, and in each case the middle term can be its antecedent or consequent, yielding four possibilities. This is a further parsing exercise.
The text says: 'فهذه أقسام أربعة'

Three Divisions of Compound Syllogisms (Conditional + Disjunctive)
Classifies compound syllogisms by middle term completeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify compound syllogisms of conditional and disjunctive premises based on the completeness of the middle term.

Core Idea
The middle term's completeness determines the structural types of compound syllogisms.

This passage introduces the fifth section of syllogisms, which combines a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) premise. The key is the role of the middle term—whether it appears as a complete or incomplete part of each premise. Understanding this classification is essential before evaluating the validity of such syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main divisions
Locate in the Arabic the three ways the middle term can appear: complete in both premises, incomplete in both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other.
2
Analyze the subcases when the middle term is complete
Note that when the middle term is complete, the conditional can be minor or major, and in each case the middle term can be its antecedent or consequent—giving four subcases.
3
Recognize the conditions for productivity
The author states that not every division yields a valid conclusion; one premise must be affirmative and one universal.
4
Understand the general inference principle
In conditionals, conjunction of entailers implies conjunction of entailments; in disjunctives, impossibility of conjunction of entailers and contradiction of entailments apply.

القسم الخامس من الأقسام الخمسة فيما يتألف من المتصلة والمنفصلة؛ وإنه على ثلاثة أقسام: فإن الأوسط فيه [1] إما أن يكون جزءاً تاماً من كل واحدة من المقدمتين، [ب] أو غير تام من كل واحدة منهما، [ت] أو تاما من إحدى المقدمتين غير تام من الأخرى. فإن كان الأول فالمتّصلة إن كانت صغرى فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثاني، والشكل الثالث عن الرابع. وإن كانت كبرى فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثالث، والشكل الثاني عن الرابع لما مرَّ من قبل أن المقدم في المنفصلة لا يتميز عن التالي بالطبع. ثمَّ المتّصلة إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى؛ وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون الحد الأوسط تاليها أو مقدمها : فهذه أقسام أربعة. والشرط في الكل أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين موجبة، وإحديهما كلية ولا يلزم أن يكون كل قسم من الأقسام العقلية المندرجة في هذه الأقسام الأربعة منتجا بل قد يكون منها ما لا يكون منتجا. والاستدلال بجواز الاجتماع بين الملزومات على الاجتماع من اللوازم في المتصلات وبامتناع الاجتماع بين الملزومات . وتناقض اللوازم في المنفصلات من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان بعد الاطلاع على حقيقة كل واحدة من المتصلات والمنفصلات. ولا خفاء في أن الأقسام متعددة حسب تعدد القضايا المتصلة والمنفصلة؛ والبيان في كل قسم على حسب ذلك القسم. [أ] أما القسم الأول وهو أن تكون المتّصلة صغرى؛ والأوسط تاليها كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، ودائمًا إما «ج د» أو «هز» مانعا من الجمع ينتج دائمًا إما «أب» أو «هز» مانعا من الجمع لامتناع اجتماع الأكبر مع الأوسط اللازم للأصغر. وإن كانت المنفصلة مانعة الخلو فإنه ينتج متّصلة كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فــ «ج د»، ودائما إما «ج د» أو «هـ ز» مانعا من الخلو ينتج قد يكون إذا لم يكن أب» فـ هز» لما أن المتّصلة تنعكس عكس النقيض؛ [٢٢١] وذلك مما يستلزم / هذا من الثالث. وكذلك إذا كان إحدى المقدمتين جزئية لكن النتيجة جزئية مانعة الخلو لما أن امتناع اجتماع الشيء مع اللازم مما يوجب امتناع اجتماعه مع الملزوم: هذا إذا كانت المقدمتان موجبتين . . فأما إذا كانت إحديهما سالبة كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ (ج د»، وليس البتة إما (ج) د» أو «هـز» مانعا من الخلو فإنه ينتج ليس البتة إما «أب» أو «هز» عن الخلو لأن «هـ ز » لما جاز خلوه عن (ج) د اللازم لا «ب» لزوما كليًّا دائما لزم جواز خلوه عن الملزوم. وكذلك إذا كانت إحدى المقدمتين جزئية والنتيجة جزئية لما مر.
The fifth section of the five sections concerning what is composed of the conditional and the disjunctive; and it is of three divisions: for the middle term is either (a) a complete part of each of the two premises, (b) or not a complete part of each of them, (c) or a complete part of one of the two premises but not a complete part of the other. If it is the first, then if the conditional is minor, the first figure is not distinguished from the second, nor the third from the fourth. And if it is major, the first figure is not distinguished from the third, nor the second from the fourth, due to what has passed before: that the antecedent in the disjunctive is not naturally distinguished from the consequent. Then the conditional is either minor or major; and in both cases the middle term is either its consequent or its antecedent: these are four divisions. And the condition in all is that one of the two premises is affirmative, and one of them is universal, and it is not necessary that every division of the rational divisions subsumed under these four divisions is productive; rather some of them may not be productive. And the inference by the possibility of conjunction between the entailing things to the conjunction of the entailments in conditionals, and by the impossibility of conjunction between the entailing things and the contradiction of the entailments in disjunctives, is among what has no need for explanation after understanding the reality of each of the conditionals and disjunctives. And there is no concealment that the divisions are numerous according to the multiplicity of conditional and disjunctive propositions; and the explanation of each division is according to that division. (a) As for the first division, which is that the conditional is minor and the middle term is its consequent, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Always either C is D or H is Z', exclusive of conjunction, it produces 'Always either A is B or H is Z', exclusive of conjunction, due to the impossibility of the conjunction of the major with the middle which is necessary for the minor. And if the disjunctive is exclusive of emptiness, then it produces a conditional, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Always either C is D or H is Z', exclusive of emptiness, it produces 'It may be that if A is not B, then H is Z', because the conditional converts by contraposition; and that entails this from the third. And likewise if one of the two premises is particular, but the conclusion is particular and exclusive of emptiness, because the impossibility of the conjunction of a thing with the necessary entails the impossibility of its conjunction with the necessitating thing: this is if the two premises are affirmative. As for if one of them is negative, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'It is absolutely not the case that either C is D or H is Z' exclusive of emptiness, then it produces 'It is absolutely not the case that either A is B or H is Z' exclusive of emptiness, because when it is possible for H is Z to be empty of C is D—which is necessary for A is B as a universal constant necessity—it follows that it is possible for it to be empty of the necessitating thing. And likewise if one of the two premises is particular, and the conclusion is particular, as has passed.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of compound syllogisms that combine a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) premise. The author states that this fifth type has three main divisions based on whether the middle term is a complete part of both premises, incomplete in both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other. If the middle term is complete in both, then the conditional—whether minor or major—causes indistinguishability between certain figures because the antecedent and consequent in the disjunctive are not naturally ordered. From this, four sub-divisions arise depending on whether the conditional is minor or major and the middle term is its antecedent or consequent. The author then notes that not all these divisions are productive (yield a valid conclusion), and provides a general principle: in conditionals, the possibility of conjunction between the entailers implies conjunction of the entailments; in disjunctives, impossibility of conjunction between entailers and contradiction of entailments. The first specific example given is when the conditional is minor and the middle term is its consequent. With an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam'), the conclusion is another exclusive disjunctive. With an exhaustive disjunctive (mani'at al-khuluw), the conclusion is a conditional. The author also handles cases with negative premises and particular premises, explaining how the conclusions follow based on logical principles such as contraposition and the relationships between entailers and entailments. The explanations rely on understanding the nature of conditionals and disjunctives. All examples use symbolic letters (أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, ز) as stand-ins for terms.

منفصلة
الحد الأوسط

Checkpoint
How many divisions of the middle term are there in compound syllogisms of conditional and disjunctive?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 4
D) 5

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms of conditional and disjunctive are classified by how the middle term appears in the premises, and not all are productive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a diagram showing the three divisions of the middle term and their subcases. For each subcase, provide an example from usul al-fiqh using the terms 'wajib', 'haram', 'mubah'.

The Syllogism
advanced
5 min
First Example: Exclusive Disjunctive Conclusion from Conditional Minor
First example of compound syllogism with exclusive disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate a compound syllogism with a conditional minor and an exclusive disjunctive major, yielding an exclusive disjunctive conclusion.

Core Idea
When the middle term is the consequent of the conditional, an exclusive disjunctive major gives an exclusive disjunctive conclusion.

This lesson focuses on the first specific case of compound syllogisms: when the conditional is the minor premise, its consequent is the middle term, and the disjunctive is exclusive (mani'at al-jam'). The author provides a concrete example with symbolic terms and explains why the conclusion follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the conditional premise: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D'. Locate the exclusive disjunctive premise: 'Always either C is D or H is Z' (exclusive of conjunction).
2
Determine the middle term
The middle term is 'C is D' because it appears as the consequent of the conditional and as the first disjunct of the disjunctive.
3
Apply the rule for exclusive disjunctive
Since the disjunctive prevents conjunction of its disjuncts, the conclusion is an exclusive disjunctive where the first part is the antecedent of the conditional ('A is B') and the second part is the other disjunct ('H is Z').
4
Verify the reasoning
The conclusion follows because the major term cannot coexist with the middle term, which is necessarily tied to the minor term. Thus, if A is B, then C is D; but C is D cannot be together with H is Z; so if A is B, then H is Z cannot coexist with the result? Actually, the conclusion is: Always either A is B or H is Z (exclusive), meaning they cannot both be true. The passage says: 'due to the impossibility of the conjunction of the major with the middle which is necessary for the minor.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second Example: Conditional Conclusion from Exhaustive Disjunctive
Second example with exhaustive disjunctive yielding conditional conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate a compound syllogism with a conditional minor and an exhaustive disjunctive major, yielding a conditional conclusion.

Core Idea
When the disjunctive is exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw), the conclusion becomes a conditional derived by contraposition.

This lesson continues the first case but changes the disjunctive to an exhaustive type (mani'at al-khuluw). The author shows that the conclusion is now a conditional, not a disjunctive. He also addresses the case where one premise is particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The conditional is the same: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D'. The disjunctive is exhaustive: 'Always either C is D or H is Z' (exclusive of emptiness).
2
Determine the middle term
Again, the middle term is 'C is D', the consequent of the conditional and the first disjunct.
3
Apply the rule for exhaustive disjunctive
Since the disjunctive excludes the possibility that both are false, if C is D is false, then H is Z must be true. Combined with the conditional, if A is B then C is D, so if C is D is false (i.e., A is not B), then H is Z must be true. This yields: 'If not (A is B), then H is Z'.
4
Understand the role of contraposition
The conditional 'If A is B then C is D' is equivalent to 'If not (C is D) then not (A is B)'. But here the conclusion is 'If not (A is B) then H is Z', which uses the exhaustive disjunctive and the conditional's contrapositive.
5
Handle the case of particular premises
If one premise is particular, the conclusion is also particular but still exhaustive in disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw). The reasoning uses the principle: if something cannot be together with the consequent, it cannot be together with the antecedent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس من الأقسام الخمسة فيما يتألف من المتصلة والمنفصلة؛ وإنه على ثلاثة أقسام: فإن الأوسط فيه [1] إما أن يكون جزءاً تاماً من كل واحدة من المقدمتين، [ب] أو غير تام من كل واحدة منهما، [ت] أو تاما من إحدى المقدمتين غير تام من الأخرى. فإن كان الأول فالمتّصلة إن كانت صغرى فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثاني، والشكل الثالث عن الرابع. وإن كانت كبرى فلا يتميز الشكل الأول عن الثالث، والشكل الثاني عن الرابع لما مرَّ من قبل أن المقدم في المنفصلة لا يتميز عن التالي بالطبع. ثمَّ المتّصلة إما أن تكون صغرى أو كبرى؛ وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون الحد الأوسط تاليها أو مقدمها : فهذه أقسام أربعة. والشرط في الكل أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين موجبة، وإحديهما كلية ولا يلزم أن يكون كل قسم من الأقسام العقلية المندرجة في هذه الأقسام الأربعة منتجا بل قد يكون منها ما لا يكون منتجا. والاستدلال بجواز الاجتماع بين الملزومات على الاجتماع من اللوازم في المتصلات وبامتناع الاجتماع بين الملزومات . وتناقض اللوازم في المنفصلات من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان بعد الاطلاع على حقيقة كل واحدة من المتصلات والمنفصلات. ولا خفاء في أن الأقسام متعددة حسب تعدد القضايا المتصلة والمنفصلة؛ والبيان في كل قسم على حسب ذلك القسم. [أ] أما القسم الأول وهو أن تكون المتّصلة صغرى؛ والأوسط تاليها كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، ودائمًا إما «ج د» أو «هز» مانعا من الجمع ينتج دائمًا إما «أب» أو «هز» مانعا من الجمع لامتناع اجتماع الأكبر مع الأوسط اللازم للأصغر. وإن كانت المنفصلة مانعة الخلو فإنه ينتج متّصلة كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فــ «ج د»، ودائما إما «ج د» أو «هـ ز» مانعا من الخلو ينتج قد يكون إذا لم يكن أب» فـ هز» لما أن المتّصلة تنعكس عكس النقيض؛ [٢٢١] وذلك مما يستلزم / هذا من الثالث. وكذلك إذا كان إحدى المقدمتين جزئية لكن النتيجة جزئية مانعة الخلو لما أن امتناع اجتماع الشيء مع اللازم مما يوجب امتناع اجتماعه مع الملزوم: هذا إذا كانت المقدمتان موجبتين . . فأما إذا كانت إحديهما سالبة كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ (ج د»، وليس البتة إما (ج) د» أو «هـز» مانعا من الخلو فإنه ينتج ليس البتة إما «أب» أو «هز» عن الخلو لأن «هـ ز » لما جاز خلوه عن (ج) د اللازم لا «ب» لزوما كليًّا دائما لزم جواز خلوه عن الملزوم. وكذلك إذا كانت إحدى المقدمتين جزئية والنتيجة جزئية لما مر.
The fifth section of the five sections concerning what is composed of the conditional and the disjunctive; and it is of three divisions: for the middle term is either (a) a complete part of each of the two premises, (b) or not a complete part of each of them, (c) or a complete part of one of the two premises but not a complete part of the other. If it is the first, then if the conditional is minor, the first figure is not distinguished from the second, nor the third from the fourth. And if it is major, the first figure is not distinguished from the third, nor the second from the fourth, due to what has passed before: that the antecedent in the disjunctive is not naturally distinguished from the consequent. Then the conditional is either minor or major; and in both cases the middle term is either its consequent or its antecedent: these are four divisions. And the condition in all is that one of the two premises is affirmative, and one of them is universal, and it is not necessary that every division of the rational divisions subsumed under these four divisions is productive; rather some of them may not be productive. And the inference by the possibility of conjunction between the entailing things to the conjunction of the entailments in conditionals, and by the impossibility of conjunction between the entailing things and the contradiction of the entailments in disjunctives, is among what has no need for explanation after understanding the reality of each of the conditionals and disjunctives. And there is no concealment that the divisions are numerous according to the multiplicity of conditional and disjunctive propositions; and the explanation of each division is according to that division. (a) As for the first division, which is that the conditional is minor and the middle term is its consequent, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Always either C is D or H is Z', exclusive of conjunction, it produces 'Always either A is B or H is Z', exclusive of conjunction, due to the impossibility of the conjunction of the major with the middle which is necessary for the minor. And if the disjunctive is exclusive of emptiness, then it produces a conditional, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Always either C is D or H is Z', exclusive of emptiness, it produces 'It may be that if A is not B, then H is Z', because the conditional converts by contraposition; and that entails this from the third. And likewise if one of the two premises is particular, but the conclusion is particular and exclusive of emptiness, because the impossibility of the conjunction of a thing with the necessary entails the impossibility of its conjunction with the necessitating thing: this is if the two premises are affirmative. As for if one of them is negative, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'It is absolutely not the case that either C is D or H is Z' exclusive of emptiness, then it produces 'It is absolutely not the case that either A is B or H is Z' exclusive of emptiness, because when it is possible for H is Z to be empty of C is D—which is necessary for A is B as a universal constant necessity—it follows that it is possible for it to be empty of the necessitating thing. And likewise if one of the two premises is particular, and the conclusion is particular, as has passed.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of compound syllogisms that combine a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) premise. The author states that this fifth type has three main divisions based on whether the middle term is a complete part of both premises, incomplete in both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other. If the middle term is complete in both, then the conditional—whether minor or major—causes indistinguishability between certain figures because the antecedent and consequent in the disjunctive are not naturally ordered. From this, four sub-divisions arise depending on whether the conditional is minor or major and the middle term is its antecedent or consequent. The author then notes that not all these divisions are productive (yield a valid conclusion), and provides a general principle: in conditionals, the possibility of conjunction between the entailers implies conjunction of the entailments; in disjunctives, impossibility of conjunction between entailers and contradiction of entailments. The first specific example given is when the conditional is minor and the middle term is its consequent. With an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam'), the conclusion is another exclusive disjunctive. With an exhaustive disjunctive (mani'at al-khuluw), the conclusion is a conditional. The author also handles cases with negative premises and particular premises, explaining how the conclusions follow based on logical principles such as contraposition and the relationships between entailers and entailments. The explanations rely on understanding the nature of conditionals and disjunctives. All examples use symbolic letters (أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, ز) as stand-ins for terms.

If the disjunctive premise is exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw), what type of conclusion does the compound syllogism yield?
A) Exclusive disjunctive
B) Conditional

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Conditional Minor with Disjunctive Major (Affirmative Premises)
Author presents the first division of mixed muttasil-munfasil syllogisms with affirmative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a mixed syllogism where the minor is an affirmative conditional and the major is an affirmative disjunctive, distinguishing between exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions.

Core Idea
An affirmative conditional minor with an exclusive disjunctive major yields an exclusive disjunction; with an exhaustive disjunctive, it yields a conditional.

This lesson dives into the first productive combination of mixed syllogisms. When the minor premise is a conditional and the major is a disjunctive, the conclusion depends on the type of disjunction. The author uses formal variables to illustrate, but the principles apply to any Islamic science argument, such as fiqh rulings on impurity or permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Note the conditional minor (e.g., 'Whenever A is B, C is D') and the disjunctive major (e.g., 'Always either C is D or H is Z'). In Islamic examples, A could be 'substance is khamr', B 'intoxicating', C 'najis', D 'impure', etc.
2
Determine the disjunction type
Check if the disjunctive is exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). This is crucial for the conclusion form.
Exclusive: cannot both be true; exhaustive: cannot both be false.
3
Apply the rule for exclusive
If exclusive, the conclusion is an exclusive disjunction of the antecedent (A is B) and the second disjunct (H is Z). Reason: the middle term (C is D) is necessary for the antecedent and cannot coexist with the major (H is Z).
Example: 'Whenever it is khamr, it is najis' and 'Always either it is najis or it is vinegar (exclusive)' yields 'Either it is khamr or it is vinegar (exclusive)'.
4
Apply the rule for exhaustive
If exhaustive, the conclusion is a conditional: 'If not A is B, then H is Z'. Derived by contrapositive of the original conditional and the exhaustive disjunction.
5
Handle particular premises
If one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular exhaustive (exclusive? The text says 'جزئية مانعة الخلو' i.e., particular exhaustive).
This is an exception; the general rule requires universal premises for universal conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون كل قسم من الأقسام العقلية المندرجة في هذه الأقسام الأربعة منتجا بل قد يكون منها ما لا يكون منتجا. والاستدلال بجواز الاجتماع بين الملزومات على الاجتماع من اللوازم في المتصلات وبامتناع الاجتماع بين الملزومات وتناقض اللوازم في المنفصلات من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان بعد الاطلاع على حقيقة كل واحدة من المتصلات والمنفصلات. ولا خفاء في أن الأقسام متعددة حسب تعدد القضايا المتصلة والمنفصلة؛ والبيان في كل قسم على حسب ذلك القسم.

أما القسم الأول وهو أن تكون المتصلة صغرى؛ والأوسط تاليها كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، ودائمًا إما «ج د» أو «هز» مانعا من الجمع ينتج دائمًا إما «أب» أو «هز» مانعا من الجمع لامتناع اجتماع الأكبر مع الأوسط اللازم للأصغر. وإن كانت المنفصلة مانعة الخلو فإنه ينتج متصلة كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فــ «ج د»، ودائما إما «ج د» أو «هـ ز» مانعا من الخلو ينتج قد يكون إذا لم يكن «أب» فـ «هز» لما أن المتصلة تنعكس عكس النقيض؛ وذلك مما يستلزم هذا من الثالث. وكذلك إذا كان إحدى المقدمتين جزئية لكن النتيجة جزئية مانعة الخلو لما أن امتناع اجتماع الشيء مع اللازم مما يوجب امتناع اجتماعه مع الملزوم: هذا إذا كانت المقدمتان موجبتين . . فأما إذا كانت إحديهما سالبة كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إما «ج د» أو «هـز» مانعا من الخلو فإنه ينتج ليس البتة إما «أب» أو «هز» عن الخلو لأن «هـ ز » لما جاز خلوه عن «ج د» اللازم لا «ب» لزوما كليًّا دائما لزم جواز خلوه عن الملزوم. وكذلك إذا كانت إحدى المقدمتين جزئية والنتيجة جزئية لما مر.

وإن كانت المنفصلة السالبة مانعة الجمع فلا ينتج أصلا لاحتمال صدقه مع التلازم بين الطرفين تارة ومع التعاند الحقيقي أخرى. وإن كانت السالبة متصلة فالمنفصلة إذا كانت مانعة الخلو فإنه ينتج كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فــ «ج د»، ودائمًا إما «ج د» أو «ه ز» مانعا من الخلو ينتج ليس البتة «أب» د» إما «أب» وإما «هز» مانعا من الخلو وإلا لصدق نقيضها قد يكون إما «أب» أو «هـ ز » مانعا من الخلو؛ ويلزمه قد يكون إذا لم يكن «هز» فـ «أب». وإنه مع المنفصلة المذكورة ينتج من الشكل الثالث قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»؛ وقد كان ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، هذا خلف. ولا نزاع فيه إذا قيل إنه ينتج مانعة الجمع لما أن لازم النقيض مع المتصلة اللازمة للمنفصلة ينتج من الشكل الأول قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»؛ وإنه ينافي السالبة المتصلة الكلية. وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين جزئية فإنه ينتج سالبة جزئية مانعة الخلو إن كانت الجزئية منفصلة لما مرَّ في بيان المنفصلة الكلية المانعة من الخلو، ومانعة الجمع إن كانت متصلة لما مرَّ في بيان لزوم المنفصلة الكلية المانعة من الجمع إن كانت المنفصلة مانعة الجمع. والمقدمتان كليتان فإنه ينتج سالبة جزئية مانعة من الخلو كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، ودائمًا إما «ج د» وإما «هز» مانعا من الجمع ينتج قد لا يكون إما «أب» أو «هز» مانعا من الخلو وإلا لصدق نقيضه دائما إما أن يكون «أب» أو « هـز» مانعا من الخلو. ولازم المنفصلة مع لازم النقيض ينتج من الشكل الأول كلما كان «ج د» في «أب»؛ وينعكس إلى قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»؛ وقد كان ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، هذا خلف. وإن كانت إحديهما جزئية فإن كانت الجزئية منفصلة كانت النتيجة ما ذكرناه بالبيان الذي مرَّ ذكره. وإن كانت متصلة فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لصدقه مع الاتصال بين الطرفين مرة، ومع الانفصال بينهما أخرى.
That every division of the mental divisions subsumed under these four divisions may be productive, indeed some may be unproductive. Reasoning by permissibility of coexistence between the implied things (malzumats) upon the coexistence of the implications (lawazim) in conditionals, and by impossibility of coexistence between the implied things and the contradiction of the implications in disjunctives, is among what has no need for explanation after understanding the reality of each of conditionals and disjunctives. There is no concealment that the divisions are multiple according to the multiplicity of conditional and disjunctive propositions; the explanation for each division is according to that division.

As for the first division: that the conditional is the minor premise and the middle term is its consequent, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Always either C is D or H is Z' as exclusive (mani'at al-jam'), it always yields 'Either A is B or H is Z' as exclusive, because the major cannot coexist with the middle which is necessary for the minor. And if the disjunctive is exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw), then it yields a conditional, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Always either C is D or H is Z' as exhaustive, it yields 'It may be that if not A is B, then H is Z', because the conditional converts by contrapositive; and that necessitates this from the third [figure]. Likewise, if one of the two premises is particular, then the conclusion is particular exhaustive, because the impossibility of a thing's coexistence with the implication necessitates the impossibility of its coexistence with the implied thing: this if both premises are affirmative. As for if one of them is negative, as in our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Never either C is D or H is Z' as exhaustive, it yields 'Never either A is B or H is Z' as exhaustive, because when H is Z is allowed to be free of C is D which is necessary for B in a universal perpetual manner, its freedom from the implied thing (malzum) necessarily follows. Likewise if one of the two premises is particular, the conclusion is particular for what has passed.

And if the negative disjunctive is exclusive, it yields nothing at all due to the possibility of its truth with the concomitance between the two sides sometimes and with real opposition other times. And if the negative is conditional, then the disjunctive if it is exhaustive yields, as in our saying: 'Never if A is B then C is D', and 'Always either C is D or H is Z' as exhaustive, it yields 'Never either A is B or H is Z' as exhaustive, otherwise its contradictory would be true: 'It may be that either A is B or H is Z' as exhaustive; and it necessitates 'It may be that if not H is Z, then A is B'. And it, with the mentioned disjunctive, yields from the third figure 'It may be that if A is B then C is D'; and 'Never if A is B then C is D' — this is a contradiction. There is no dispute that it yields exclusive, because the implication of the contradictory with the conditional necessary for the disjunctive yields from the first figure 'It may be that if A is B then C is D'; and that contradicts the universal negative conditional. And if one of the two premises is particular, then it yields a particular negative exhaustive if the particular is disjunctive, for what has passed in the explanation of the universal exhaustive disjunctive, and exclusive if it is conditional for what has passed in the explanation of the necessary universal exclusive disjunctive if the disjunctive is exclusive. Both premises universal: it yields a particular negative exhaustive, as in our saying: 'Never if A is B then C is D', and 'Always either C is D or H is Z' as exclusive, it yields 'It may not be that either A is B or H is Z' as exhaustive, otherwise its contradictory would be always true: 'Either A is B or H is Z' as exhaustive. And the implication of the disjunctive with the implication of the contradictory yields from the first figure 'Whenever C is D is in A is B'; and it converts to our saying: 'It may be that if A is B then C is D'; and 'Never if A is B then C is D' — this is a contradiction. And if one of them is particular, then if the particular is disjunctive, the conclusion is what we mentioned by the explanation that passed. And if it is conditional, then it yields nothing at all due to its truth with the connection between the two sides sometimes, and with disconnection between them other times.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of mixed syllogisms where one premise is a conditional (muttasila) and the other is a disjunctive (munfasila). The author first notes that not every combination of these mental divisions produces a valid conclusion; some are unproductive. He then proceeds to enumerate cases, following the order of the figures of the syllogism and the types of disjunction (exclusive or exhaustive).

The first case treats the conditional as the minor premise with its consequent as the middle term. When the disjunctive is exclusive (mani'at al-jam'), the conclusion is an exclusive disjunction of the minor's antecedent and the disjunctive's second disjunct, because the major (the second disjunct) cannot coexist with the middle (the consequent), which is necessary for the minor's antecedent. When the disjunctive is exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw), the conclusion is a conditional: 'if not A is B then H is Z', derived by contraposition of the original conditional and the exhaustive disjunction. If one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular exhaustive.

When one premise is negative, e.g., the conditional is affirmative and the disjunctive is negative exhaustive, the conclusion is a negative exhaustive disjunction of the minor's antecedent and the second disjunct. This follows because if the second disjunct can be absent while the consequent (the middle) is always present, it can also be absent while the antecedent is present.

Next, cases where the disjunctive is negative and exclusive yield no conclusion because the truth of the premises can be satisfied in different ways (either with genuine concomitance or real opposition). When the conditional is negative and the disjunctive is exhaustive, the conclusion is a negative exhaustive disjunction. The author proves this by reductio: assuming the contradictory leads to a contradiction via the first and third figures. Similarly, if one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular.

Finally, when both premises are universal, the conclusion is a particular negative exhaustive, again proven by reduction. If the particular premise is conditional, no conclusion follows due to the possibility of connection or disconnection between the terms, mirroring the earlier case.

Throughout, the author uses variables (أب, ج د, هز) as propositional variables, not real terms. The examples are purely formal, but the logical principles apply to any content, such as in fiqh: 'Whenever it is intoxicating, it is forbidden' (conditional) and 'Either it is khamr or it is nabidh' (disjunctive) producing a conclusion about the category of a specific drink.

مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

ملزوم

Checkpoint
In a mixed syllogism with an affirmative conditional minor ('Whenever A is B, C is D') and an affirmative exclusive disjunctive major ('Always either C is D or H is Z, exclusive'), what is the conclusion?
A) Always either A is B or H is Z, exclusive
B) Always either A is B or H is Z, exhaustive
C) If not H is Z, then A is B
D) Never either A is B or H is Z

Key Takeaway
An affirmative conditional minor with an exclusive disjunctive major yields an exclusive disjunction; with an exhaustive disjunctive, it yields a conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the conditional 'Whenever the prayer is invalid, it must be repeated' and the exclusive disjunction 'Either the prayer is invalid or it is complete', determine the conclusion using the rule from this lesson. Verify with the author's reasoning.
?
Task 2: Construct a mixed syllogism using an exhaustive disjunction from a fiqh context (e.g., 'Either this monetary transaction is riba or it is permissible') and derive the conditional conclusion. Explain the logical steps.
